THE RED PAGE.
Austerile? Ausfertile?
ARIGHT pessimist is “ Onlys ” the Aus.
terile. A very Austral Schopenhauer, dam-
per-fed, tanned with tea, and very rude,
indeed, to the Great Australian Myth. But
I quarrel with his science. Why should we be
deprived of our ancient marsupial flesh-eaters by
this melancholy person who sighs for lions and
leopards? When the world was younger huge
marsupial carnivora ranged our country, and their
degenerate posterity, driven out by the exotic
dingo, live as the Tasmanian devil and wolf, fierce
and untameable as any cave-lion or wolverene.
uf * a 9 nl / S J’ ca , n our P lat ypus and echidna cold-
Wooded if he choose—maybe they deserve the
epithet for their low body-temperature ; but I for
one stick to our ancient lion as a noble blood-
lover, capable of devouring a dozen effete British
lions stoup and roup to his breakfast. Thirty feet
clear did some of his brethren measure, and each
had canine-teeth a foot long. He would make a
noble centre-piece for our military banners.
And why curse the aborigines as among the
lowest of mankind ? We have civilised them off
so fast that they have become a mere groundwork
for the embroidered fancies of liars and of igno-
ramuses; but there is reason for a shrewd guess that
they came from India way, even as we ourselves
Such of their laws and customs as have been
rescued from oblivion show that, in matters of
sanitation and of the prevention of too close inter-
breeding, they were by no means an utterly bar-
barous people. They had no narrow fertile
valleys or indented borders of mountain-shielded
coast to form mental forcing-houses; and even
the pastorahst stage of development was forbidden
them by the lack of domesticable animals
Hunters they remained by pure force of physio-
graphic circumstance, and environment gave them
never a chance to develop.
And why the particular environment ? We are
so accustomed to proclaiming ourselves Young
Australia that we have grown to look upon this
continent as one recently created for our special
benefit by a beneficent Batron who is always ready
to alter the original plans of his work for the con-
venience of a few. of his specially-created puppets
Everywhere are the signs of incalculable age, and
yet we go on howling ourselves hoarse, with eyes
tight-shut, lest we be brought to realise that the
supposed young virgin is a toothless, wizened bel-
dame whose last lover was dust long before man
emerged from monkeydom on the plains of Tatr-
tary. Sometimes we wonder why Australia has
no mountains, no navigable rivers, why her wes-
tern and central portions are dreary sand deserts •
and we are told by effusive Ignorance that these
features have not yet had time to develop.
We swallow it all and go on singing our
own praises, only stopping to choke some
miserable traitor who dares to open his eyes
and say what he sees. Our mountains are
the mere hills of the originals, planed down bv
aeons of ram-denudation and filling up the vallevs
where the rivers flowed. Even Kosciusko’s 7000-
and-odd- feet, so carefully taught to our children
represents only the pedestal on which stood a pile
that would overtop Everest with Mont Blanc on
top of-it. Since Australia was born the rains and
snows have worked ceaselessly, lowering that vast
pile by a few inches each century through incal-
culable ages where the units were millions, until a
mere stump remains and the superstructure is
spread over thousinds of square miles of sandy
plain or sea bottom. Australia was imprisoned
in her youth, and remains a withered hag with
the ribs of the world sticking through her skinny
sides. 'L he weird fauna and flora, are relics of that
youth, preserved as an old maid preserves the
withered rose and satin shoes of her first dance to
be tossed carelessly aside by a young heir whose
generation has forgotten their'history. A land of
ghosts, a land of awful loneliness borne in silence
through countless geological ages, a land which
we must not look too closely into or we will go
mad and babble out dreadful truths, and our
blind fellows will strangle us. Scarred
and broken, poor aged Australia stumbles
on without hope, of relief or oblivion
until the experiment comes to an end and
this world is rolled up and cast into the fire. She
is forgotten even as the prisoners in the river-cells
of the Bastille when their gaoler got drunk or
careless ; but, unlike those, she cannot die.
And this national Australia of ours! this im-
ported product foisted on a decrepit land which
is too aged and too poor to support itself. Has
she the proper family history for the work to be
done if it is to succeed ? Born in a convict barra-
coon ; nurtured on rum and the blood which
dripped from the lash ; ravished in her adolescence
by hot-eyed gold-seekers hungry to tear the jewels
from her shrunken bosom ; churched of the pro-
geny amid a thunderstorm of cannon-fire and a
tropic ram of Boer blood ;—is this the mother
who will nurture us arid lead our steps from
J I ime only will show ; but the augury is not
good. An intense egotism is the keynote of Aus-
tralian character at present; and it will lead us
into difficulties, perchance into destruction ere
we are purged of it.
Mean while, we close our purblind eyes more
tightly to howl the louder in our own praise. Un-
happy is the Australian who can see, unhappy is
he who dares to reason and to follow the point-
ings of that objectionable person at the well-
bottom whose protests we are trying to drown
with the champagne arid the shouting of a so-
called national awakening. We are the fish-
spawn on the ruins of a sea-buried Atlantis, an
ant-hill m the ruins of Baalbek thanking the Ant-
god for his special creation of such a splendid nest
site.

Point Blank.
IT appears that Onlys, having excogitated
the word Austerile m a moment of idle-
ness, was delightfully pleased with himself
and proceeded to build about his word a
network peculiarly adjectival, ribbed with wrong
?5u e ” ’ ti)e ' vh . ole aet . up on a lop-sided
backbone. The result is a caricature skeleton.
‘‘Onlys’’ sourly dubs,our native land “unde-
veloped virgin” or “sapless old maid.” Why
does he not say which ? Then he goes on to ask
if she can be a full-breasted mother” to us, her
children ? Old maids and virgins have no milk
and certainly no children. He calls her “thin-
Hanked, barren, immature, ”in one breath. An
w ai l m , a / b ® barren—through age—but that is
not her fault. She has not, m her youth, had the
opportunities of disproving the charge. And an
immature virgin has yet to meet the test.
We are not Australia’s children. We are a
band of ravishers and robbers. We have chased
her own offspring from her breasts and have our-
selves sucked hard at them for all we were worth
WhQn she becomes dry we will abuse and beat her
as the heathen beats his wet-weather Joss.
We are cuckoos, we sit upon stolen nests We
are changelings, come in the night.
Who can call Australia barren, with her deep
inexhaustible rifts of gold ? What other country ex-
cept Africa can feed and fatten the woolly millions
that yearly go to swell the squatter’s pockets to
bursting point? Her resources are unending.
She has a vegetation of her own. Her animals
are to be found nowhere else—they are harmless
and that is saying a great deal. Most of our pests
have been imported—witness the fox, the rabbit
the sparrow, etc. ’
The inventor of “ Austerile,” like the small boy
at the circus, has a leaning to lions and tigers—
t.e., at a safe distance and shut-off from him and
his ha penny bag of pea-nuts. The kangaroo has
earned from him only the adjective “ weird.” He
cannot see in this creature any of its beauty, peace
or grace. He prefers, as said before, the
crouching, cowardly, uneatable tiger. “Onlys”*
knowledge of Australian ornithology evidently
does not extend much beyond a coloured plate o
a jackass or a wire cage with a cockatoo in it He
says nothing of the bell-bird, the magpie,’ the
woodpecker and the rest. He writes “large un
wieidy birds,” and makes an end. That is’just
like “Onlys” and his sort.
. s ?"y s Onlys,” “Australia had breathing
individuality, would we have joined in the savage
hounding-down of . the Boer?” We have in
dmduality, but it is family individuality, and we
are merely brother to dur brother when we join him
m a hard-pushed raid on a rich land three weeks
across the water. It is the highwayman of the
old country sharing his pistol with the red-shirted
bushranger.
Now for our literature. ‘ ‘ Onlys ” has an array
of four representative men, two, at least, of whom
brought their brains and pessimism from over the
sea—Marcus Clarke and Lindsay Gordon The
other two are Boake and Lawson. Gordon shot
himself ; Boake saw the Destroyer through a
loop in his whip-lash; and Clarke’s most intimate
friends are known to whisper confidentially when
speaking of his death. Lawson still lives.
The -first three were bom pessimists, and, no
matter what land they were in, would have
touched the same melancholy “note.” Lawson
has had a hard life, and is not, after all, such a
painter of blank pictures as “ Onlys ” would, have
him. He has laughter when the humour
pleases, and we can readily give pardon for a little
timely pathos. His gloom is not to be wondered
at,—and again it is not Australia that is respon-
sible, but the man. '
Briinton Stephens is .as nearly Australian as any
mie of them, and “Banjo” Paterson certainly 7
What is-their note?-Ao« pessimism, pessimism
and pessimism.. “ Steele .Budd” with fun’
of sa like h ~ ps “
The word “ Austerile ” has no meaning.
Barkow-Noethe.
“Are you a drift Sargasso ¥ ” —Fenton.
LET us endeavour to answer two questions—
Firstly, who are these so-called Aus-
tralians?
; . Well, as to pedigree, they are European,
Asiatic, Oceanic, and the native-born (this last
but a step remote from the preceding). As to
civilisation, they rank everywhere from agesß.C.
to the present A. D. As to colour, white, yellow,
brown, and black—and shades thereof—will
describe. Their mental atmosphere, in the aggre-
gate, is a confusion of old-world conventions,
dogmas and formulae, somewhat the worse for
careless handling, but still assertive. Their chief
characteristic traits may be briefly summarised as
occasional spasmodic fits of great' energy with in-
tervals of much tiredness, postponing the inevit-
able, celebrating, laying the odds, and a fondness
for forcible, exaggerated and picturesque expres-
sion. Is it reasonable to call such a kaleidoscopic,
heterogeneous conglomerate a race? Can we
speak of an Australian as we speak of an English-
man ? Obviously we cannot.
There is no Australian race ; it is not yet born ;
it is still an embryo in the womb of Time slowly
evolving from the crude elements that Destiny has
flung together : a process of careful selection, of
fusion, welding, moulding and polishing lies be-
fore it during the wax and wane of many genera-
tions. Even the American is not yet crystallised.
Patience ! This grey and grim old continent,
with its vast distances and terrifying loneliness,
its long and deadly spells of silence broken too
often only by vague discordant music.—shall we
say it has no heart because we, forsooth, have
never heard it beat ? It is not death, nor decay,
nor wasted vitality that stamps that brow so for-
biddingly. It may be the sickness of hope de-
ferred, of longing unsatisfied.
Australia waits—how many countless years she
has waited ! —waits for the warm and passionate
lover that shall make her fruitful. He comes not,
cannot come, being the Spirit of a race yet unborn.
He will come; therein lies our hope. He shall
come; therein rings the keynote of our resolu-
tion.
The Eucalyptus supplies a symbol. Here is a
race of hardy warriors—-nothing so tough, so
elastic, so durable in all the world. Look at the
fine vigour, the Bohemian daring of those 3inuous
limbs, the careless grace of the foliage, the whole-
some sweetness of the leafy aroma purifying the
air. If Australia can grow such a tree, why not
such a race ? Give her time.
What then to do ? Our second question now
faces us. What have we done ? Have we given
this our country of the best that was in us, our
ripest thought, our fullest energy; have we made
great sacrifices, life itself if need be ? Few there
are have done so, but few are not enough. Have
we called to our aid all that Art, Philosophy and
Science can effect to smooth the roughness of the
way ? Have we not too often, rather, wandered
from the way, shrinking from the task, or oppos-
ing but faint and feeble effort to that which verged
upon the irresistible ? But a little time has been
ours, we plead, for the great work. True, that is
a consolation: let it not be an unction. What
then to do ?
Other places, other manners. Old-world
theories must be abandoned. All the accumu-
lated prejudices, moral, social and political, which
the Past presses on the drooping shoulders of
humanity—a terrible incubus ! —must be gradually
but surely cast off. Let us, in their place, engrain
I into the individual and national character what
Nietzsche rightly calls the four cardinal virtues—
sincerity, courage, generosity and courtesy—
Australia will herself lay upon us a new burden
sweeter and nobler than the old, which will
strengthen the sinews of body and soul, making
the brain alert, resourceful and energetic. Out of
the travail of the race will be born its spirit, that
finer sense of things. She that now holds her face
averse will smile in welcome, the careworn mood ,
will vanish, Australia, the sunny southerner,
youngest of nations, will assume her sovereignty ;
the admiration of the world will rise as incense
about her; the blood of the people will pulsate
with one common throb. Her hour will have
come !
Our past is smeared with many muddy blotches,
the present looks cold and colourless, but the
future is always ours, the ample future, though
we should not waste.it because it is limitless. So '
far we are almost nothing and have done little.
Yet it is something to have learnt that.
Australians, greatness lies before us. Let us
deserve it and it is ours !

Alphabetical Verses—the Letter A .
GOING TO SLEEP.
The light is fading down the sky,
The shadows grow and multiply ;
I hear the thrushes’ evening song ;
But I have borne with toil and wrong
So long, so long!
Dim dreams my drowsy senses drown—
So, darling, kiss my eyelids down !
My life’s brief spring went wasted by,
My summer ended fruitlessly ;
I learned to hunger, strive and wait:
I found you, love—O happy fate !
So late, so late !
Now all my fields are turning brown—
So, darling, kiss nly eyelids down !
O blessed sleep ! O perfect rest!
Thus pillowed on your faithful breast,
Nor life nor death is wholly drear,
O tender heart, since you are here—
So dear, so dear !
Sweet love ! my soul’s sufficient crown !
Now, darling, kiss my eyelids down !
1882-
Elizabeth Akers Allen.
BEER.
Here,
With my beer <
I sit,
While gQlden moments flit:
Alas!
They pass
Unheeded by;
And as they fly,
I,
Being dry,
Sit, idlj’- sipping here
My beer.
O, finer far
Thau fame, or riches, are
The graceful smoke-wreaths of this free oir*H
Why
Should I
Weep, wail, or sigh ?
What if luck has passed me by ?
What if my hopes are dead,—
My pleasures fled ? I
Have I not still
My fill.
Of right good cheer, —
Cigars and beer?
Go, whining youth,
Forsooth !
Go, weep and wail,
Sigh and grow pale,
Weave melancholy rhymes
On the old times,
Whose joys like ahadowy ghosts appears,
But leave to me my beer !
Gold is dross, —
Love is loss,—
So, if I gulp my sorrows down,
Or see them drown
In foamy draughts of old nut-brown,
Then do I wear the crown
Without the cross !
1834-65.
George Arnoid.
THE NIGHT BEFORE LARRY WAS STRETCHED.
The night before Larry was stretch’d,
The boys they all paid him a visit;
A bit in their sacks, too, they fetch’d—
They sweated their duds till they riz it;
For Larry was always the lad,
W hen a friend was condemn’d to the squeezer,
But he’d pawn all the togs that he had,
Just to help the poor boy to a sneezer,
And moisten his gob ’fore he died.
“ 'Pon my conscience, dear Larry,” says I,
“ I’m sorry to see you in trouble,
And your life’s cheerful noggin run dry,
And yourself going off like its bubble*! ”
“ H °uld your tongue in that matter," says he ;
I or the neckcloth I don’t care a button,
And by this time to-morroty you’ll see
Your Larry will be dead as mutton :
All for what? ’Ease his courage was good! ”
The boys they came crowding in fast;
They drew their stools close round about him,
Six glims round his coffin they placed—
He couldn’t be well waked without ’em.
I ax’d if he was fit to die,
Without having duly repented ?
Says Larry, “ That’s all in my eye,
And all by the clargy invented,
To make a fat bit for themselves.’’
Then the cards being called for, they play’d,
Till Larry found one of them cheated ;
Quick he made a hard rap at his head—
The lad being easily heated.
“ So ye chates me bekase I’m in grief !
Oh ! is that, by the Holy, the rason ?
Soon I’ll give you to know, you d—d thief !
That you ’re cracking your jokes out of sasoz,
And scuttle your nob with my fist!"
Then in came the priest with his book,
He spoke him so smooth and so civil;
Larry tipp’d him a Kilmainham look,
And pitched bis- big wig to the devil.
Then raising a little his head
To get a sweet drop of the bottle,
And pitiful sighing, he said,
“ Ob ! the hemp will be soon round my throttle,
And choke my poor windpipe to death ! ”
So mournful these last., words he spoke,
VVe all vented our tears in a shower ;
For my part, I thought my heart broke
To see him cut down like a flower !
On his travels we watch’d him next day,
Oh, the hangman, I thought I could kill him !
Hot one word did our poor hurry gay,
Nor chang’d till he came to King William ;
Och, my dear ! then his colour turned white.
When he came to the nubbling-cheat,
He was tacked up so neat and so pretty ;
The rambler jugg’d off from his feet,
And he died with his.face to the city.
He kick’d, too, but that was all prided
For soon you might see ’twas all over ;
And as soon as the noose was untied,
Then at darkey we waked him in clover,
And sent him to take a ground-sweat.
Anon.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Land I Love I
Land I love ! I will wrest your meaning-
See, I swear I will know you yet.
You shall reveal the soul of your song,
And I will set it, as never set.
March of shadows to muted music,
Heat-mists creeping, I know, I know ;
And I know, dear Rain, that your desolate story
Has a hidden sweet and an inner glory.
Trees of mine ! ah, the nights I listen,
Nights I steal through your black, black shade,
I and the old gums sorrow alone,
The young gums give me their accolade.
Mile on mile through the death-grey silence,
Twilight, midnight, or yellow moon,
And ’tis I who know that your desolate story
Has its hidden sweet and its inner glory.
Dark and dawn through the grey gums sweeping,
Blazing gold of the afternoon,
All have revealed the soul of their song,
But where, O Land, is my promised tune ?
I am silent, I have no music,
Maestoso nor Allegro,—
But you know how fain is my impotent story
To unfold the hymn of your veiled, great glory.
Only this can I sing, and singing,
Land of mine ! you will understand.
You have revealed the heart of my song,
While I went seeking for yours, O Land 1
Your young lips have disclosed my courage,
Deathless courage, my Continent !
For I learnt from you that my life’s own story
Has a deeper depth and a higher glory.
Heat and haze ! you have crept and caught me.
See, ’tis you who will know me yet.
You have revealed the soul of my song ;
’Tis you who have set it, as never set.
March of shadows to muted music,
White gums waiting, we know, we know !
And we know, Dear Land, that our desolate
story
Has its hidden sweet and its inner glory.
N.B.W.
LOUISE MACK.
Drifting.
The moon on the water, the wash of the tide
As it soothingly laps ’gainst our boat while we
glide,—
The dim, hazy shore and the waves’ gentle croon—
They are all making music of exquisite tune ;
And the pure naked stars as they bathe in the
sea
Are whispering love-songs to you and to me.
See ! the mist-softened lamps of the pier fade
from sight
As we two drift away to the dreamland of night;
And we rock in the midst of the silver-gold beam
While our hearts are baptised in the love-stirring
gleam;
And the sea seems a place for the fairies to play
As it stretches, like Fancy, gold-lit, far away.
And your voice in its silvery sweetness, so low,
Makes music with stars and with sea as we go ;
And your eyes are more bright than the bright
lights above,
For they smile with the passionate sweetness of
love,
Oh ! the touch of your hand and the warmth of
your lips
Are as dear as is harbor to storm-stricken ships.
Glad, glad is the night when, with none else
beside,
You and I in the moonlight drift down with the
tide.
v.
M.M.
The Paris-Calais train lately covered the
distance between the two places (185 miles) in
184 min. 30sec., thus lowering the previous record
by more than half-an-hour.
There has been a good deal of Australian
Consul in evidence at Government Houses,
memorial services, and so forth, since the death
of Queen Victoria. All the Queen’s Consuls for
foreign powers were lately re-appointed King’s
Consuls, and many dim mysterious jokes in the
diplomatic line of business were brought to light
with due solemnity. South America is a rich
source of Consular humor. Insolvent republics
change their governments pretty frequently, but
the Australian agents remain constant to their
nominal billets and their uniforms from revolution
to revolution. Some of them represent confederate
peoples—black, , white, and tawny—almost as
numerous as the population of Australia. For in-
stance, Marshall Lyle, the legal champion of
Deeming, is Consul-General for Colombia. From
his office in Melbourne he keeps watch over
the Australian interests of Spaniards, negroes, and
cannibal Indians. If a great trade between
Colombia and this country were to break out sud-
denly, Marshall Lyle would find his official duties
very embarrassing, but up to now he has been
interviewed by only one Colombian, and that was
mainly for drinking purposes. Caicedo, the
wondrous wire-dancer, who came out here under
a £6O per week engagement to Williamson and
Musgrove, was a Colombian. As a stranger
in a strange land the little man sought
out his consul and fell upon his neck with en-
thusiasm. He used to take the official out to
banquets a deux and propose his health, where-
upon Lyle would toast the Republic of the United
States of Colombia in fervent language which
Caicedo didn’t understand. Lyle, looking back
dreamily to that boom-time of his consulate, often
wonders whether he will have the pleasure of en-
countering another Colombian before he dies.

ROLLICKING AFTER-DINNER SPEECH.

takers’ trade! ’”
CANDIDATE FOR COMMONWEALTH HONORS : “Now, what you want in public life,

bores.”)
A RATHER SUDDEN HALT.
On account of the appearance of a bubonic rat in the vicinity of Sydney Harbor the N.S.W. Mounted Contingent for S Africa has postponed its departure. To think
that one miserable little rat can hold an army in check ! Here’s a tip for De Wet.

SATURDAY, MARCH 16, 1901.
Britain’s Dark Days of Protection.
A pew weeks ago a Sydney Freetrade paper
published a number of opinions of various
English authorities on the fiscal question.
Many of the authorities were painfully
obscure, and many of the opinions were vague
and hesitating, but one or two people spoke
out boldly enough. “ England has no wish
to go back to the days of Protection,” said
one, and another declared that the masses in
England were in favour of Freetrade, and
inquired indignantly if the masses might not
be permitted to know what is good for them,
and to understand their own business. In
view of the very much larger masses in other
countries which also know what is good for
them, and are equally well acquainted with
their own . business, the latter query is
not necessarily so convincing as it
looks. And as to the former, there are
very few now who have any definite
recollection of what England was like under
Protection, and the evidence of the people
who don’t want to go back to the dark days
of high tariffs is mostly hearsay and tradi-
tion. Still there are grandfathers and grand-
mothers among them who recollect some-
thing of Protected England in the 30’s and
thereabouts, and remember quite rightly
that it was a dolorous place and one that no
well-constituted human being wants to re-
turn to.
* * * *
Towards the end of the eighteenth century
the century before last—England had
been a systematically-protected State for
over 100 years. It was an amazingly rich
State as ideas went in those days. All the
world was poor then. There was practically
no steam-power ; the steam-engine nowa-
days does, at the same cost, about
100 times as much work as the horse,
and it is no wonder that manufactures went
slowly in that era of mostly animal labour.
And as a consequence the toiler had
to work long hours in order to over-
take the demand, and even ivith all the
work he could do the supply of goods
was not abundant. Also, goods were costly
because it took many men to produce them,,
and wages were low because one man could
produce little. Commerce, too, was small
and slow, because there were neither rail-
ways, steamboats, telegraphs, telephones,
gas, electricity, nor decent roads, and even
canals were scarce. Money was not abun-
dant, for the goldfields of California, Aus-
tralia, Maoriland, South Africa, India and
Alaska were all undiscovered, and the world’s
stock of coin was .very small. Yet England
was an exceedingly rich country. Mul-
hall’s “ Dictionary of Statistics ” gives, on
page 262, some remarkable information
about its wealth and debt at various re-
mote dates. In the latter days of the 18th
century the total wealth of Great Britain
was £177 per head —actually far more
than Europe’s average is to-day, despite
all improvements. These were incredible
riches according to the resources of a period
that was mainly before steam and before
machinery. The things that made England
an unhappy country to live in then —the
scarcity of books, of culture, of political
liberty, of decent artificial light, of good
drainage, good means of communication, of
large and small and middle-sized items of
civilisation—-were matters which had no con-
nection with its fiscal policy whatsoever.
* * * *
And about then the country came to be
blessed with a glorious fool-Minister who
plunged it into 20 years of war in order to
prop up an effete French dynasty. Britain
spent, roughly speaking, eleven hundred
millions on that amazing insanity. The
taxes were trebled. About £600,000,000
were added to the National Debt. Some-
thing like 10,000 British vessels, large and
small, are alleged to have been captured,
sunk, or burnt, for even the mistress of the
sea had a woefully bad time on the waters.
The Bank of England went broke, so far as
cash was concerned, for 20 years, and paid in
paper. Wheat went up to famine prices—not
as some Freetraders allege, by reason of the
corn duties, for the amount actually paid in
duty on imported corn under the old sliding-
scale system was only 6d. per quarter, or
about Is. on every £8 worth—but because
local agriculture suffered from neglect and
bad seasons, and swarms of privateers made
imports precarious. When the war ended
in 1816 the wealth of England (Mulhall’s
“Dictionary of Statistics,” page 262) was
only £l2O per head. The increase of 20
years, and nearly one-third of all the pre-
viously existing wealth had been crowded
into cannons and fired off at an irrelevant
Italian usurper, in a quarrel that was never
Britain’s quarrel from the beginning.
* * * *
Then began the weariest task which
Britain ever undertook—the work of paying
the bill. There was still little commerce, for
want of good roads, railways, steamboats
and telegraphs, and other things that were
either not yet invented or only in the ex-
perimental stage. Manufacturing was still
mostly a laborious, slow, and unprofitable
hand process. There was still little money
with which to carry on commerce, because
the world’s stock of coin had run low with
the gradual failure of the old mines, and
those of California, Australia, Maoriland,
South Africa and Alaska were still undis-
covered. Hours of labor were still long,
because the old factory operative in these
days of primitive appliances could produce
only about the same quantity of goods in 14
hours as the man of to-day, with improved
machinery, can in two or three, and a civi-
lised community had to work some 14 hours
a day to supply its requirements, even
though they were much more limited
than they are now. And yet Protec-
tionist Britain made amazing progress. In
21 years (1816-1837) it reduced its debt by
£112,000,000; in the next 52 years, from 1837
to 1889, the reduction was only £90,000,000.
In 21 years of Protection the debt was
diminished by £l3 for every inhabitant;
during the ensuing 52 years, mostly under
Freetrade, the reduction was also £l3 per
inhabitant, so the rate of progress in the
second period was less than half as fast as
in the first. In 21 years of Protection the
aggregate wealth of the country increased
from £l2O to £l5B per inhabitant, an in-
crease of £3B per inhabitant, or £1 16s. 2d.
per inhabitant per annum. In the next 52
years, mostly under Freetrade, wealth further
increased to £250 per inhabitant, an expan-
sion of £92 per inhabitant, or £1 15s. sd.
per inhabitant annually. With all the
alleged advantages of Freetrade, with all
the wealth created by railroads, steamboats,
and steam machinery, with all the golden
plunder of Australia and Maoriland
flowing into its coffers, the process of
recovery was not so rapid in the Freetrade
period as in the Protectionist one which
preceded it. Yet the former was also the
period when the ruin of the Napoleonic wars
was still at its worst, when commerce was
hardly invented for want of the necessary
appliances, and when manufacture was still
a comparatively primitive process, and man
was yet, to a great extent, his own motive-
power.
* * * *
The real beginnings of Freetrade came in
1846 with what is called the abolition of the
Corn-Laws, when the sliding scale was
abolished and the duty on imported grain
was reduced to a maximum of Is. per quar-
ter. People who look back with a vague
and hazy eye into the remote regions of his-
tory still talk hearsay tales about the awful
price of the principal article of food before
the heavy protective duty was taken off, and
the blessed relief which fell on the com-
munity immediately after. But there was
no excessive price in the latterdaysof the Pro-
tective period, save when British commerce
and agriculture were knocked end ways by war,
and except what was caused by expensively
humping grain to cities by road, and grind-
ing it with costly and primitive appliances.
And there was no relief under Freetrade, or
comparative Freetrade, until steam revolu-
tionised production and put commerce on a
new basis. From 1827 to 1846, under the
old Corn Laws, the highest price of wheat
in England was 71s. per quarter, in 1839.
From 1846 to 1859 under alleged Freetrade,
the highest price was 755. per quarter in
1855. Taking the 19th century up to 1860,
wheat was once (in 1835) as low as 395.
per quarter under Protection ; and it
was also once (in 1851) as low as 395. per
quarter under Freetrade. The price of
wheat went up lamentably in the years of
the great war and the depreciated paper
money, but it dropped as steadily under
Protection as it did afterwards under
Freetrade. The average price was 84s.
4d. per quarter from 1811 to 1820. It was
665. per quarter from 1821 to 1830 ; and
575. per quarter from 1831 to 1840—the last
complete decade under Protection. From
1841 to 1850, partly under the
old Corn Law regime and partly under
the new enlightened Freetrade system, the
average price was 535. 3d. per quarter; and
from 1851 to ’59, wholly under the new,
beautiful, enlightened, cheap-bread system,
the average price went up to 555. and the
people’s principal victual was dearer than
in the dark days just before the bless-
ings of Cobdsn fell upon them. The article
on “ Prices ” on pages 473 and 474 of Mul-
hall’s “Dictionary of Statistics” is in-
teresting reading for those who have a hazy
recollection that the beginning of Freetrade
was the beginning of all good things in
England.
* * * ♦
But just when the new order of things
was in danger of proving a little worse than
the old one—when the burst of prosperity
didn’t come, and prices rose (according to
the average prices given on page 399 of
Mdlhall’s “Industries and Wealth of
Nations ” the first result of Freetrade
was to put up prices and keep them
up for 30 years —till 1874), and wages
didn’t rise—various extraneous things hap-
pened. Between 1850 and 1860 the
steamboat, which was previously only a
clumsy and almost useless toy, began to
revolutionise commerce. The gold dis-
coveries in California suddenly bloated the
world with wealth in the early ’so’s, and the
gold mines of Australia bloated it still more
almost immediately afterwards. There were
only 838 miles of very primitive railway in
Britain in 1840; there were over 10,000
miles in 1860. Steam-power had only begun
in 1840 ; all the steam-engines of the world
were then equal to only 1,650,000 horse-
power ; by 1860 they had multiplied five-
fold, and by 1888 thirty-fold. Food became
cheaper in time because it was carried to the
consumer by rail instead of reaching him, at
a dozen times the charge for carriage, by
waggon over bad roads. Hours of labour
became shorter, because when a man, by the
aid of steam, could do several times as much
work in eight hours as he formerly could by
hand-power in 14 hours, there was no longer
the need to work 14 hours. Wages became
higher because the worker could do so much
more work. Manufactured goods became less
expensive because steam and improved
machinery made it possible to produce
them at vastly less cost, despite shorter
hours and higher wages. Freetrade got a
gorgeous advertisement in England because
it happened to come, or to begin coming,
just when the locomotive, steamboat, and
most of the great mechanical inventions also
arrived. But for that it would probably
have died still-born. Other nations got a
larger share of the profit arising from the
new order of things, but they didn’t happen
to have a new fiscal fetish on hand to ascribe
it to. If it had been cannibalism in-
stead of Freetrade that Britain had just
adopted, cannibalism would probably have
got as big an advertisement as Freetrade did.
» ♦ * *
The average Briton doesn’t want to go
back to Protection because he remembers it
vaguely, or has heard of it vaguely, as some-
thing that existed along with hand-looms,
and slow sailing-ships, and long hours, and
low wages, and tallow candles, and internal
commerce expensively conducted by coach
and waggon. And the American and
German don’t want to go back to
their old policy of low tariffs, because to
them low tariffs were associated with long
hours, and low wages, and dim tallow candles,
and hand-loom labor, and unprofitable horse-
waggon commerce. Even if it is shown
that Britain prospered more in the new days
of Freetrade and steam-power than in the
old days of Protection and hand-labor and
of painful recovery from the most costly war
in all its history, that fact proves nothing on
behalf of Freetrade, unless the Freetrader is
prepared to declare that the steamboat is of
no more importance than the scow, and that
the locomotive is only as fast and as cheap
as the bull-dray, and that the old hand-loom
worked as well as its steam successor. If
all the mechanical improvements of the last
50 years are quite useless, and if Britain s
wealth never increased before it got
Freetrade, then the fact that its wealth
has increased since it got Freetrade
is evidence that Freetrade is good, always
provided the increase has been faster than
that of countries which didn’t get Free-
trade. If Britain, with Freetrade and steam-
power, has kept the lead it had in 1840—if it
has lost nothing in the race against the nations
which have Protection and steam-power—
then Freetrade has a good case. If not, it
has no case in particular. And Britain
hasn’t kept the lead it had in
1840. Its people, presumably, have the
same grit and intelligence as they had
when Protectionist Britain ruled the world,
but Freetrade Britain doesn’t rule the world.
As for the news that Freetrade England,
with locomotives, steam machinery, steam-
ships, telegraphs, and the like, is richer than
Protectionist England in the days of sailing-
ships, horse-waggons, and hand-power, that
is nothing, so far as the fiscal question is con-
cerned. Protectionist England, in the 18th
century, with its hand-looms and its waggon
commerce, was also richer than aboriginal
Freetrade England in the Ist century, with
no looms at all, and its commerce carried
per hand by our remote ancestor stained
with woad. That is equally relevant, or
equally irrelevant.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Dry Dog 1 Reid’s Latest.
Dry Dog Reid, Australia’s great manufacturer of
facts and evidences, broke out in a new place last
Thursday night at Sydney Protestant Hall. This
is the Dry Dog man’s latest impossible horror:
I pity the workers of Victoria when I go through the
factories, and see in them, not the workers of Victoria,
hut their children. (Cheers.) I pity the industries of
Victoria when I see man after man thrown out into the
streets, because they have grown too big for them, and
young children brought in to take their places—(cheers)
and when I know that for every man you find in these
Victorian factories you find half-a-dozen children.
(Cheers.) This evil will attend almost all countries in
spite of the wisest laws, but I can’t understand men
willingly agreeing to buy these social curses. ICheers.)
These will come, to some" extent, in spite of you, but that
men should try and grow them I can’t understand.
(Cheers).
Poor, grieved little man ! Rut again there is this
discrepancy between Reid’s statistics and The
Bulletin’s statistics: This paper gives its
authority, and even the page of its authority, for
its statements so that the public can verify them
down to the last item. And George Reid simply
says that things are so, and his only authority is
himself—the reliable individual who insulted
N.S.W. Parliament with that bowled-out list of
Dibbs’s alleged Civil Service appointments, and
the sham collection of Victorian prices, and
whose name has now become a synonym for
general inaccuracy and for bogus items about
starch, and another term for the thing that isn’t
so. Therefore, George Reid, as is his nature,
carefully abstains from giving the address of any
one Victorian factory where there are six children
for one man, or even two children for one man, for
if he did the proprietor would probably bowl him
out within 24 hours. And The Bulletin
assumes, on good evidence, that he does this
because there is no such factory, and because he is
perfectly aware of the fact, and is merely the
same unscrupulous globular fraud as usual. The
evidence in question is to be found on pages 256,
395 and 400 of N.S.W. Statistician Coghlan’s
latest “ Seven Colonies,” and it reads as follows :
N.S.W. Victoria.
Children actually attending State
schools .. .. .. 149,349 .. 143,844
Ditto, private schools .. .. 47,560 .. 35,232
196,909 .. 179,076
Total number of children under 15 520,705 .. 391,600
Of whom about one-half are below
school age (6 years) .. .. 260,352 .. 195,800
Children between 6 and 15 not at-
tending school .. .. 63,443 .. 16,724
These latter are, with very few exceptions, either
working or loafing at street corners, and qualify-
ing for gaol. Anyhow, out of 16,724 male children
in Victoria who are above six years of age and
who are not at school, George Reid says, with
tears in his eyes, that owing to the oppressive
nature of the Protectionist policy 37,000 odd are
toiling in factories —there are 44,041 males in
Victorian factories and Reid asserts that the pro-
portions are one man to six children. This is in
addition to the thousands of boys employed in
shops and offices and on farms. And he implies
that, owing to the blessings of Freetrade, there is
no such employment of children in factories in
N.S.W. Wherefore, if Victoria has 37,000 boys
employed in factories out of 16,724 available, and
N.S.W. owing to the blessings of Freetrade isn’t
subject to this evil, and as N.S.W. has no
more of other kinds of employment to offer
than Victoria, it seems that out of the
cornstalk State’s 63,443 unaccounted-for boys
fully 130,000 must be loafing at street corners
with no employment whatever. Truly the Dry
Dog man is a wonderful inventor of impossible
figures, and the most foolish purveyor of bosh in
Australian politics. New South Wales, by the
statistics of its own official authority, has a far
smaller proportion of its children regularly
attending places of education than Victoria has.
The proportion of families which hire governesses
and tutors isn’t large in any country, so the dead
certain result is that far more N.S.W. than Vic-
torian children are either working or loafing, or
else that N.S.W. has far more of both loafing and
working children. And it would take a very
great deal better evidence than the bald assertion
of G. Reid to get over Coghlan’s testimony.

A Politically Homeless Sinister.
Westralia is going to have a State election
almost immediately, for the sufficient reason that
it has got into such a tight place that it is im-
possible to get on without one. Westralia’s
Assembly used to last for four years, but the
recent Reform Act changed it into a triennial
body. The Act also widened the franchise, cut
up some overgrown electorates into two or three,
abolished some others, and up - ended things
generally. The idea was that the existing
Parliament would dissolve when its three
years were up, but many members, who
represented mainly sheep and cattle and wire
fences, had no desire to face their new human
constituents, and it was decided by a little strain-
ing of the law that this Assembly should continue
to exist under the old system, and fill in its com-
plete four years. Then trouble began to arise.
Members died or resigned. If the old electorate
still remained, other men were elected —it is now
declared quite illegally—on the old roll, and if the
old electorate had gone to pieces the place was left
vacant. But at last Moran of Coolgardie accepted


a portfolio; his seat thus became vacant, and his
re-election was a matter of urgency. Then it
began to be remembered that there was no longer
any electorate of Coolgardie. It was decided that
Mohan couldn’t be re-elected for one of the three
divisions into which Coolgardie has been cut up, for
they are part of a new order of tilings which won’t
come into force till this Parliament expires. Also,
he couldn’t be elected on the new rolls, for they are
not in force till this Parliament expires ; nor on the
old ones, for they are abolished. The Speaker
formally issued his writ for a new election to the
Returning Officer for Coolgardie, but there being
no longer any such person the document was
vainly offered to under-secretaries, J’s.P., police-
men, dog-catchers and men who worked conden-
sers and looked after water-tanks. Finally, Moran
found that he couldn’t be re-elected for any place,
and he couldn’t keep his new office unless he was
re-elected. Also, if he gave up his new office he
couldn’t even then—his seat having once become
vacant —get back into his old position as private
member for Coolgardie. Further, his portfolio
couldn’t be given to anyone else without putting
the other man into much the s ime difficulty. So
Westralia is going to have a General Election as
soon as possible.
The Old English Corn-Law Delusion.
The S.M. Herald really ought to hire a man who
knows something about British history, and then
it wouldn’t drop into such foolishness as this :
Not that freetraders are afraid of fitrures ; only they
know how deceptive statistics are if all the facts are not
taken into account. For example, we are referred to
England, and we are told that after the centuries of pro-
tection England had so prospered that she was able te
adopt a Freetrade policy. The inference seems to be that
after two centuries of Protection Australia may be in a
similar happy position. The statement and the supposed
analogy are both preposterous. As to the prosperity of
England under Protection we may cite the following sen-
tence from the London Times of January 14, 1801, re-
cently reprinted by the Times, with other extracts, to
show what the paper was a century ago :—“ We have the
satisfaction to state that the Lord Mayor yesterday
ordered the price of bread to be lowered two assizes and
a half, which will reduce the quartern.loaf to Is. Bd. on
Thursday.” A noteworthy evidence of prosperity truly,
that a feeling of satisfaction should be produced by the
reduction of the price of bread, on the authority of the
Lord Mayor, to Is. Bd. a quartern loaf.
And in those days the British duty on imported
wheat was sixpence per quarter so long as the
price didn’t drop below 545. per quarter —a micro-
scopic charge of less than one per cent. When
the price fell below 50s. per quarter a heavy duty
became operative, and when the price was
between 50s. and 545. a moderate duty of
2s. 6d. per quarter was levied. In 1801,
the date above mentioned, the price of wheat in
England was £5 18s. per quarter, so the duty was
about 7s. 6d. on every £IOO worth of wheat—less
than the port dues that are charged in most English
ports to-day. But the British harvest was bad, and
the great Napoleonic wars had cut off foreign
supplies, for this was before Trafalgar, and Bri-
tain’s command of the sea was precarious;
and the country was pouring out money like
water in a huge foreign conflict, and the State
had suspended cash payments—it suspended for
25 years—and Bank of England notes were at a
discount and viewed with grave suspicion, and
the practical Freetrade which reduced the
wheat duty to 7s. 6d. per £IOO worth left
England in the awful state above-described.
The noteworthy point is, however, that the
article—bread—which in those days went up
to the most exorbitant price, was the one in
which there was practical freetrade. Clothes and
boots were very heavily protected, but there were
no boot riots or pant riots as a set-off to the bread
riots of these good old days.
The Future of S'A :’rica.
Wrote Olive Schreiner to arocent meeting of
S’African women :—•
Great and overwhelming have been our losses during
the year. Yet, great as they have been, the loss of Eng-
land has been greater.
England has lost among the officers and men who, far
far from their native land, have fallen in this, to them,
foreign country, many of her noblest and bravest sons.
I myself have lost personally, in one of the English officers
who died in the Free State, one of the oldest and most
valued of my friends ; a man whose humanity, and whose
generosity of feeling towards a brave foe, were worthy of
the best traditions of the England of the past England
has lost heavily in her best; while her worst, the men of
greed, ambition and cunning, who shaped this war, are
with her still, to do her further injury. Great and irre-
parable has been her loss in this matter.
But, in another direction, she has had yet greater loss
than this. She has lost for ever the faith and the
affection of the bulk of people in this colony. Four-
teen months ago, in spite of much that had taken place
in the past, we, the bulk of the people in this colony,
believed in England, and in the power of the more
righteous element in her people ultimately to over-
power and control the baser and more servile. Four-
teen months ago I sat in a little up-country Boer
farm-house ; and when we spoke of the possibility of
war, men and women alike said, “ There can be no
war, our Queen would never allow it. She has always
loved us and been good to us ; she would never allow them
to kill our kindred in the Republics. Rhodes and
Chamberlain and Milner may wish for it; but Lord
Salisbury has said that they do not want the land or the
gold of the Transvaal. England would never do us this
great wrong.” Fourteen months ago, as you all know,
there was hardly a farm-house in the length and breadth
of the Cape Colony where the picture of the Queen was
not to be found hanging on the walls. There were sad
old memories of Slachters Nek, and the first attack on the
independence of the Transvaal Republic, but Gladstone’s
wisdom in restoring it had healed the wound ; and there
was a strong feeling through the length and breadth of
our land that the speculator-monopolists, and not the
Queen and the English people, were responsible for the
Jameson raid. The faith of our people in England’s
ultimate rectitude and desire to act with honor was, in
spite of many mistakes, unshaken. “Rhodes and the
speculators may desire to take their gold and land from
our brothers in the Transvaal,” said an old Boer to me,
“ but our Queen will not let them. I have known her for
more than fifty years.” Fourteen months ago if any man
had stated to me that it was possible for those things to
take place which have taken place in the Colony under
martial law, and in the neighboring Republics, during
the last year, without arousing a passionate and deter-
mined protest from the bulk of the English people, I
would have laughed him to scorn. That the bulk of the
people of England could sit silent and unmoved while
private houses were burnt down and women and young
children turned homeless into the wilds, in order that
through wounding the affections and sympathies of the
men their arms might be paralysed for further warfare —
while quiet private citizens were forced into trains that
their presence there might guard the lives of English
soldiers at the risk of their own—while the honorable
uniform of the British officer was pawned to civilians,
that masquerading in that guise they might avenge
themselves upon their political enemies—had one told me
that these things could be, and the bulk of the English
nation sit by silent and unmoved I would have regarded
him as one who dreams in a fever. That there
might be war, that battle fields might run red,
that fortified places might be bombarded, these
things I had recognised as possible; but that the
mightiest Empire the world has seen would expend
its gold in purchasing informers ; that in England
itself the right to free speech would be so dead that how-
ling mobs of thousands would attack single individuals
merely endeavouring to express their thought with regard
to a public matter, and life itself be endangered ; that in
South Africa the man or woman who exercised that pri-
mary right of the Englishman, the right to free speech,
would do so with the vision of a manacle at his or her
elbow —this I had not dreamed possible.
Now the bond of a~eetion and confidence that bound
England to South Africa has snapped.
But England has lost more than the lives of her own
brave soldiers, more than the confidence and affection of
the Cape Colonists, more than the alliance and friendship
of the heroic Republics.
England has lost her Honour. ..
To-day England stands naked before the nations, the
mantle' of assumed virtue which she had carefully-
wrapped about her torn open. She, who cried aloud to
the nations, “I am not as thou art, my aims are not as
thine ! ” stands to-day exposed. Russia, Austria, I'iance,
Germany, have not sinned as she has sinned. They never
crossed 6000 miles of sea to find a small, brave people
bound to them by the ties of a common religion and
common Aryan descent: and with the cry, ‘ >ve seek no
gold 1 We seek no land !” to set a knee upon their throats
and clutch with greedy fingers at land or gold. To-day,
in her unlovely and unlovable old age, all the nobler ana
more generous instincts of her youth extinguished, she
stands naked and shrivelled before the nations, with her
name branded upon her forehead —a hypocrite among the
Peoples. ...
The hour of England’s repentance and remorse will
come. But it will be that form of remorse, the most
awful which can visit the soul of the individual or the
nation —the remorse which comes too late.
My friends, the life of the individual is short ; but the
life of a nation is long.
The men who have produced this war, and into whose
hands England has fallen in these sad times, will pass
away. Rhodes, Chamberlain, and Milner may attain
the gold, or the power, or the titles, or the glory to
which they have aspired ; then, they will pass away. But
the young South African people, now coming to its birth
in these days of stress and anguish ; and which to-day
lies prostrate with the blood upon its forehead for bap-
tismal water, weighing down its eyelids—the young
South African people will live and grow. It will wax
with the centuries, and to it belongs the future.
While we, the people of South Africa, hold by the old,
simple, brave ideals and manners of life of the first
founders of the South African race, the future of South
Africa is assured. It is for you, the women of South
Africa, to transmit these ideas to your children. Free-
dom first, and wealth, ease, luxury, last, if at all. It has
been said in South Africa that “ Every man has his
price.” But he lied who said t his. It is for you, the
women of South Africa, to show that the heart of South
Africa is unpurchasable by any gold from blood-stained
hands. The heart of the womanhood of a nation is the
treasure-house where its freedom is stored. A fearless,
indomitable, unpurchasable womanhood ; a fearless,
indomitable, unpurchasable race.
Among all the Shoddy candidates who are
prowling over the face of Australia telling the
public how Britain has prospered under its pre-
sent fiscal system there is not one —not a single
one—who advocates the adoption of Britain’s
fiscal system. Said system is :
(1) Pure Revenue Tariffism ; no surreptitious Protec-
tion on wine, beer, tobacoo, sugar, and other things as in
N.S. Wales; every customs duty on goods which can be
locally manufactured counterbalanced by an equivalent
excise.
(2) Duties on about half-a-doa >n articles only.
(3) The shortage in revenue made up by very heavy
direct taxation. The direct taxation (local and national)
in England is fully five times as heavy as in N.S.W.
(4) No borrowing for unproductive works.
(5) The steady paying off of the public debt out of tax-
ation.
If the Commonwealth adopts Britain’s fiscal sys-
tem (which Reid, M'Millan, Braddon and co.
declare has made Britain rich and great), there is
no need for the Commonwealth to tax anything
beyond spirits and narcotics. The British taxa-
tion system would yield abundance of money.
Why do these people mouth so dreadfully about
the advisability of following Britain’s example
when not one of them proposes following Britain’s
example? If they really believe that example is
so good as they say, then they are every one
traitors to Australia for not urging this country to
adopt the beneficial thing, and there is no gum-
tree high enough to hang them on.
“ Calypso ” writes :
Dr. Creed and architect Cyril Blackett, the other day
at Sydney, deputationised Works Minister O’Sullivan re
cremating the unclaimed corpses which are to be removed
from Devonshire-street cemetery. “The Minister in
replying to the request pointed out that cremation of the
dead appeared to be repulsive to the majority of people
with religious feelings.” O’Sullivan is a queer person
with an infinite stock of insular prejudices. He doesn’t
seem to know that about 150,000,000 of the most religious
people on earth habitually cremate their dead, and he
apparently cannot conceive that anyone outside the
small group of sects around him can have any religious
feelings to speak of. Yet we know that the Greeks, from
whom we have derived most of our artistic, literary and
political ideals, practised cremation on principle. It was
early Christian fanaticism that abolished cremation in
Europe as a pagan rite, in the same way as it abolished
Greek art, literature and philosophy, it also practically
abolished cleanliness because the pagan gentlemen
washed themselves. Really, cremation is neither more
nor less than a measure of sanitary policy utterly distinct
from religion.
How the N.S.W. Old Age Pension Board will
work isn’t hard to foresee when it is borne in mind
that the members are, even without this big extra
job, about the hardest-driven officials in the State.
First, as regards Mr. Coghlan, the St..ti t ; his
hands are so full with the work of running the
most elaborate statistical department in Austra-
lasia, and so extra-full with the preliminaries for
the census, that he can have no leisure for creating
a new department. Then there is the Inspector
General of Police. Quite enough for any one man
to manage the police of the State ; yet for years
Mr. Fosbery has been the real head of the Abori-
gines’ Protection Board, the Fisheries Board, and
the Charity Organisation Society, and lately the
unfortunate gentleman has had added to his other
duties the regulation of the Sydney street
traffic. Now a new straw, and a heavy
one, has been added to his already crushing
load. Finally there is the Director of Charities.
The wonder is how these officials find time to do
the work of so many and such incongruous offices.
There is a reasonable suspicion, indeed, that the
head of a department, instead of looking into
matters for himself, “calls for reports” from his
subordinates, and mostly allows them to dictate
the course to be taken. Thus, where the nominal
control is vested in a board, the real power gets
into the hands of the secretary on condition that
he does not give trouble to the members by too
frequently referring to them.
Again the Brisbane Courier: “But how about
the Kanaka? He is fewer in Queensland even
than the Chinaman ; and he does not compete with
the white man. The notorious fact is that he does
work that the white man will not do.” The state-
ment that the Kanaka does not compete with the
white man can only be branded as a brazen lie.
There are sugar mills on the Burnett where the
Kanaka is employed about the mills, undis-
guisedly in open day. There are, knocking round
the Isis district, scores of Kanakas who fence, fell
scrub, split timber, sink wells, milk cows—in
short, do every variety of farm and plantation
work. There are mills (and some supported
partly by the State) in N.Q., where beyond
a few petty bosses, a white man is not to
be seen. There was, in Bundaberg some time
ago, a Kanaka pugilist who gave highly popular
exhibitions of his skill to an admiring and varied
audience, thereby doing the European bruiser out
of a job. Only a fortnight ago a Burdekin Kanaka
levanted and took with him some hundreds of
pounds held in trust for his brother Kanakas,
thereby emulating the deeds of the Caucasian
pillar of the church, and rendering that gentleman
(to some extent) a superfluity. Why Tommy
Tanna, with a little tnr I and opportunity, is
capable of any white accoi plishrnent.
Several Queensland Govt, papers are just now
boiling with wrath towards the State pedagogues.
It seems the Q. teachers have recently been saying
things about the worthy sky-pilots who are
attempting to meddle with Bananaland’s secular
education system. The schoolmasters allege that,
inasmuch as they have already 25 subjects to
teach in less than 25 hours per week, they really
have no time left for a fresh subject. They
further aver (as Premier Philp pointed out) that
most of them were engaged to teach secular sub-
jects, and that some of them cannot possibly teach,
dogma without a violation of their consciences.
What is more, they draw attention to the healthy
feeling existing between all classes of children in
the State school ; the absence of all sectarian
jarring and party feeling ; and they prophesy that
the moment religious instruction is instituted, dis-
tinctions are introduced and divisions created that
will inevitably damage the existing harmony.
Altogether the pedagogues have a strong case,
and should let their voices be heard on the
subject.

“ Hermes ” writes a great truth :
As a twister of facts and statistics Sydney Daily Tele-
graph is to be admired by all aspiring liars. But when it
gets down to “ ethics ” it makes itself ridiculous. The
other day it rose unsteadily on its two hind linotypes and
solemly propounded the following deep doctrine: “The
money which any man has earned (which is his own)
(much of it is generally his wife’s), he has the inherent
right to spend where and how he can do so to the best
advantage, subject always to the claims which may be
made upon him or it for the purpose of Government, or,
in other words, for the purposes of needful State re-
venue.” Upon this text the D.T. proceeds to preach that
it is “ highly immoral ” on the part of any majority to tax
per Protective tariff an unwilling minority to protect the
industries of the whole community. These be the King-
street ethics, ye gods ! Defence is part of the “purpose
of government,” and all competition is some degree of
war. Actual hostilities are merely the extreme degree of
competition, and if a State is justified in defending its
people’s wealth and lives against a foreign enemy in time
of war, it is also justified in protecting them in times of
peace. That is what Government is for, among other
things. Invasions are of two kinds —military and com-
mercial. It is the duty of Government to protect the
community, and even supposing Protection made things
dearer, which it doesn’t, the community is quite justified
in taxing the whole community to protect the industries
and wealth of the whole community.

It is about time the State Premiers protested
collectively and individually against the proposed
Imperial recruiting in Australia. The country
has been too drunk with militarism for the past
year to see what is its own best interests, and those
who should lead or guide in such matters
have been jumping like so many monkeys-
on-sticks to the pulls of the uncaring self-
seekers at the other end. Already the
gory-jowled Empire has sucked away nearly
12,000 of a five-millions population —a population
vastly inadequate to the country’s needs—and
which three States at least are still endeavoring to
supplement by immigration. And it is mostly the
young men that are taken ; the men who were to
do most towards the appearance of the next gener-
ation, and who should now, in the normal course
of things, be out opening-up, prospecting, tilling
and filling the land. And in return we are to
have our share of the ultimate retribution and
shame. Our sons will return to us disillusioned
wrecks, fit only for mouthing about the deeds
they never did ; and destined, if they breed at all,
to produce only transmitters of their own
enfeebled constitutions. For, so far, Q’land,
Westralia and Vic., have refused the recruiting-
sergeant admittance ; while South Australia with
her vast unpopulated territory, and her boundless
undeveloped resources, opens her arms gladly and
wide: N.S.W., as yet, has said nothing either
way ; but before the advent of those thinly-dis-
guised recruiters, the Duke and Duchess of York,
let us say plainly, “ We have given of blood and
treasure so freely, that we can afford no more.
Let it cease.”

Writes “Fed Tape, Junior” :
N.S. Wales appointed its Public Service Commissioners
in 1895 for seven years, so that their term of office will
soon be up. It is said that Chairman Delohery was
appointed for seven years from 1930, so he will not be in
danger of any reconstruction. And it is nearly certain
that there will be some such thing. In the Service itself
the Board is hated and despised. Hated because of the
many injustices it allows to continue, and despised be-
cause its word cannot be implicitly taken nor its
regulations relied on. Nearly 60 per cent, of the
various officers are desirous of a transfer to another de-
partment, preferring the devil they don’t know to the
one they do. Parliament, by a direct and challenged
vote refused to allow the Harbor Trust and Wharf Re-
sumptions Board to be officered by the Board, as big a
set-down as a public institution ever received from any
Legislature. The present State Ministers are notoriously
out - of sympathy with it, as they regard the Commis-
sioners as minions of George H. Reid. So the dissatisfied
Public Service hopes for drastic changes. And this is a line
excuse. The present Board was appointed to administer
nine Ministerial depts. With the advent of Federation
one, the Post-Office, goes bodily away from its jurisdic-
tion. Of the eight remaining, the Chief Secretary’s
department and the Treasury lose largely, while the
Observatory and other services are lopped off other
Departments. Also the Harbour Trust will take away
from the importance of the Public Works Department in
Sydney. If an office under the Commissioners was to be
reduced like this the Board would reduce the staff, quick
and lively. Consequently it is only reasonable to suppose
that the Commissionerships will be cut down to Two.
We in the Service have no further use for Mr. Barling.
He can retire on the pension George Reid so thought-
fully provided for him and his colleagues.

N.S.W. Labor Leader M‘Gowen, in his capacity'
as a Federal candidate, demands the abolition of
the Braddon clause and of equal State represen-
tation in the Senate. These are the kind of
alleged reforms that can be achieved without
paying any more taxes—the easy, cheap sort of
things that the easy, cheap sort of men make a
name by. But when did the Labor party raise
its voice, except in the most perfunctory fashion,
for the big, difficult reform that this country wants
more than all the rest put together—for the aboli-
tion of borrowing, for national solvency and self-
reliance, for ceasing to be the slave of the foreign
capitalist and the servant of the British three-ball
usurer? The Labor party’s plea is that in this
matter it is no more than other parties—that it is a
democratic crowd which isn’t more Tory than the
Tories —which is quite true. No one expected the
biggest and most essential of all Democratic ideas
to be advocated by any of the old hack politicians.
But when the alleged Democratic party doesn’t
even lift up its Voice on behalf of the best Demo-
cratic principle of all —the one that is worth all
other Democratic principles put together—this
country is truly in a bad way.

The clerical trade-union for regulating the mar-
riage market in the exclusive interest of the
regular sects is likely to lose the influential official
support of the N.S.W. Registrar-General. That
gentleman, apparently on his own sole authority,
removed the name of one Rev. George Hat from
the roll of persons licensed to celebrate marriages,
for no better reason than that he (Hat) had quar-
relled with the governing body of his own particu-
lar sect, and had set up in the clerical business on
his “own.” The Supreme Court saw enough in
the case to call on the Registrar-General to
“show cause,” and so the case stands till next
term. The real question, so far as the public is
concerned, is whether, in a community which has
710 religion “by law established,” any public
officer is justified in interfering in a purely
dogmatic and disciplinary dispute, among the
members of any sect, further than is required to
necessitate a judicial decision. In Mr. Hay’s
case the Registrar-General seems to have simply
gone out of his way in the interest of the regular
clerics as against the irregular non-unionist, and
such of the public as may wish to employ the
latter. The law knows, or should know, no dif-
ference between the regular and irregular cleric as
long as they keep clear of criminal practices.
What does it matter whether the man who per-
forms a marriage ceremony is union or non-union,
if the ceremony is legally valid ?

Writes “ Old Soldier ”: —
Ex-Commander-in-Chief Wolseley and ex-War-Minister
Lansdowne seem to have turned the “ Lords ” into a
laundry to some purpose last week, though neither of
them lias much reason to boast of the result of the wash.
Wolseley began by admitting the inefficiency of the
British army and blaming the “system.” He “advo-
cated the professional control of the army,’’and scored
by pointing out that the only two civilian-controlled
armies are the Chinese and the British. (He forgot the
U.S., though.) Then Lansdowne on his part declared
that Wolseley’s duty was to “ prepare schemes for offen-
sive and defensive operations.” How W. was to find
time for that while pottering with courts-martial, courts
of inquiry, promotions, transfers, exchange, and drill
and discipline, was not explained. If W. had wasted
time on the “schemes” which would have been
promptly pigeon-holed—he might have warned Lans-
downe that “ Ladysmith was not a very suitable military
station . . . and that it would take more than one army-
corps to subjugate the S’African Republics.” But how on
earth was W. to know? The suitableness of a military-
station depends on circumstances. White did very well
at Ladysmith, and it would be very hard to prove that he
would have done better anywhere else. As for Lans-
downe’s other point, how was Wolseley or anyone else to
know that an army corps (30,000 trained and disciplined
soldiers led by allegedly-trained officers) could not knock
the stuffing out of the same number of undisciplined
farmers? If W had dared to hint that, as the event has
proved, i.t would take close on 210,000 men to only half-
do the business, he would have been execrated as a base
slanderer of the empire. Just remember what happened
to Gen. Butler, for warning the authorities that at least
100,000 men would be needed to begin with ! As for the
other things for which Wolseley is said to have been
responsible : “ Control and mobilisation of the army,
utilising the volunteer forces, and for the intelligence de-
partment,” they all involve expenditure, and the C. in C.
can’t spend 6d. on his own authority. He can do neither
good nor harm without the Minister, whose subordinate
he is, and those who fancy Roberts will be better than
Wolseley will be just as far-out as those who thought
Wolseley would be more than Cambridge.

It is a peculiar circumstance that all the N.S.W.
Tree trade speakers have suddenly started afresh
to curse the Braddon Clause in the Common-
wealth Constitution with a great fresh curse.
The circumstances of that clause are complicated.
Reid stumped N.S.W. against the original Com-
monwealth Bill because the Braddon Clause was
in it. He got the Bill rejected (though a solid
majority of the people voted for it) by his 80,000-
minimum fake, and then demanded amendments
—the removal of the Braddon Clause among
the rest. The other Premiers met him on the
matter, and it was suddenly discovered that the
Reid party couldn’t devise anything better than
the clause it had so violently denounced, and
N.S.W. saw the astounding vision of its Premier
accepting, as the best possible solution of the finan-
cial problem, the very proviso on account of which
he had stumped N.S.W. imploring the people to
reject the Bill without hesitation. All the com-
bined intelligence of the Importing Party, with
its Reid and its M‘Mi lean and its B. Smith and
its Pumpkin Smith and its Carruthers and
Brunker and all the rest, when it got on the job,
found it couldn’t devise anything better than the
Braddon Clause, so the party definitely accepted
it. Yet in some inscrutable way the Importing
Party now holds up the Braddon Clause as a
disaster and a reason why N.S.W. should vote
against the Barton Ministry. If it is a disaster
the one man in N.S.W. who is responsible for its
continuance in the Constitution is Reid, who was
offered, at his own request, a full and fair oppor-
tunity to replace it by something better, and
didn’t. And if it is a disaster, as Reid’s crowd
says, so much the more reason to vote against
Reid.

N.S. Wales Judge Owen found occasion last
week, when summing-up in a trumpery libel case,
to emphasise the remarks of Chief Justice Darley
and others as to the frequency with which the
obsolete N.S.W. libel-law is taken advantage of
by hasty suitors’ lawyers in search of costs. Ac-
cording to S.M. Herald report, his Honor said —
That he repeated what he and other Judges had on
several occasions stated in court, namely, that the num-
ber of petty libel cases brought in the' Supreme Court
was a perfect scandal. Every little bit of Billingsgate
was dragged into the Supreme Court, and the public time
and the time of juries was occupied, to the exclusion of
more .momentous matters, in determining whether cer-
tain wbrds were or were not uttered.
The jury, after a short deliberation, returned a verdict
for the plaintiff with one farthing damages, and the fore-
man said that the jury quite endorsed the remarks made
by his Honor in summing-up. He added that jurymen
believed that these cases were not always brought into
court by the combatants themselves.
His Honor: I am very glad you have expressed that
opinion, because the Judges, to a very large extent, are
powerless. These matters rest almost entirely with the
jury, and while juries give large damages in such cases,
I suppose they will be brought into court. If, however,
juries would put their feet down they would soon put an
end to this kind of action.
The Bulletin commends these remarks to the
attention of the public. Many gross abuses in
Australian social life, and many gross and: public
