Her Majesty’s “Florodora.”
Theatre Royal “ In Sight of St. Paul’s."
Bijou Rickards Variety Co.
AtheniEum Hall J. C. Williamson’s Bio-Tableau.
Masonic Hall O. M. Rodney.
The present revival of “ The Christian” at
Her Majesty’s, Sydney, is for one week only,
“ Royal Divorce” following on Saturday. The
present scribe is sorry. “The Christian” is a
profoundly fascinating drama from a certain
point of view—the materialistic point of the vege-
table man who alludes to the Brotherhood of the
Holy Gethsemane as “blokes.” It is the Drama
of Wasted Spiritual Endeavour—the embodi-
ment of pious pessimism. As a warning to
people not to be good it has no equal
on the modern stage. Everybody who is
good in it is miserable and a failure, with the pos-
sible exception of Mrs. Callender, who has a
strong temper despite her benevolence, and
threatens to batter John Storm’s congregation
with her good Scotch gamp. Probably it is this
streak of wickedness in Mrs. Callender which
prevents her being a miserable, telescoped Chris-
tian like the others. John himself is the worst
failure. He abandons a fortune and a good
position to be a preacher and put the world
straight, and he finds that he makes just as much
impression on it as the feet of a cockroach do on
an Alp. He becomes a crazy priest, bellowing at
street corners till he is mercifully put out of his
miserable goodness by ahalf-brickonthehead. The
good girl in the hospital becomes the proprietor of
a highly-improper baby. The prize good girl,
Glory Quayle, leads a wretched life of benevolence
in the same hospital till she flies from it all and
becomes happy and sinful on the stage. And in a
silent sort of way the saddest of all the good
people are the monks of the Holy Gethsemane, in
their monkery in Bishopsgate-street—the long
row of tall, good, black-coated, black-hatted men,
who periodically tramp from their cells to the
chapel and back again, and then back some more,
and say silent prayers, and ring bells, and silently
count each other’s footmarks in the slush and the
grease-marks of each other’s silent heads on the
wall.
• * * •
There isn’t one of the good people, barring
Mrs. Callender, who seems to leave any permanent
benefit behind. John Storm himself is chiefly
instrumental in causing some one, who might
otherwise have been a respectable bricklayer,
drinking his beer peacefully in a public-house, to
go away burdened with the guilt of having slain
a reverend lunatic with a brick. Certainly he was
driven to it through his son having been gaoled
for hollering in a public place about the end of
the world, at the reverend and well-meaning
lunatic’s instigation. The brethren of the Holy
Gethsemane don’t make any disturbance, but only
moulder benevolently in their monk-box, but even
they are an evil influence, inasmuch as they are
silent and purposeless consumers of vegetables
which might otherwise maintain some person who
would do the world active service. Certainly
they serve to hold up 12 black hats which might
otherwise fall to the ground, but when one thinks
of the great men who might have lived if some
very small men hadn’t got there first and con-
sumed their vegetables, he is filled with wrath at
the Brotherhood of the Holy Gethsemane.
• * * *
The present co. is below the standard of
the last set of Christians in three important re-
spects. Tyrone Power doesn’t fill the part of
John Storm as Kingston did. He is a strong,
clever, intelligent actor, but Kingston contrived
to look like a half crazed benefactor who heard
heavenly voices, and had divine visitations, and
knew all about the end of the world. Tyrone
Power, on the other hand, looks severely sane and
intelligent all the time. Also Miss Edith Crane
falls about 2ft. 6in. below Henrietta Watson’s
success as Glory Quayle. And where Hawtrey
made Canon Wealthy a polished, fashionable fraud
with a jewelled hand and a tendency to regard God
as a member of the aristocracy, Arthur Lissant
makes him a screaming piece of mirth. Lissant’s
parson makes the audience laugh twice as much
as Hawtrey’s did, but Hawtrey’s was a splendid
character study, while the present Canon is only
a cheerful low-comedy joke. But the fact that
Miss Eloise Juno, the only lady in Australia who
can play a Scotchwoman as one to the haggis born,
still fills the broad shoes and the angry but bene-
volent gamp of Mrs. Callender compensates for
almost everything. Also Cecil Ward’s Drake,
W. F. Grant’s Father Lamplugh, and George
Majeroni’s Brother Paul (not that Paul has much
to do except be a rather more active moulderer than
the other monks) are all excellent. And the con-
densed cockney who brings round the monks’
vegetables is a gleam of sunshine in a bitterly
cold grey drama. His off-hand way of alluding to
the Holy Gethsemanists, in their black, long-
tailed habiliments, and their visions, and their
midnight prayers, and their dolorous bell-ringings,
as blokes is a gladdening irreverence. This world
is full of holy, dreamful, moss-grown frauds who
are only blokes after all, and who would be much
better for knowing it.
** * *
The “ Ladder of Life ” is in its sixth week
at Sydney Lyceum, and the management adver-
tises that its extraordinary success—and it cer-
tainly is extraordinary—is doubtless owing in a
measure to the “lack of improbability in the
plot.” After that it certainly can’t be set down
to any lack of improbability in the advertise-
ment. The “Ladder” is one of the most pic-
turesque nightmares that ever struck Sydney,
and The Bulletin’s inward conviction is that
most people go to see it a second time to try and
find out what they had been drinking when they
saw it the first time—hence its success. Nobody
could sort out his first impressions of the
“Ladder” without a suspicion that he had been
drinking something. After the second visit most
people will go a third time to try and guess what
the author had been drinking.
** * *
The Queen’s Hall, a small and rather un-
known place of entertainment opposite Sydney
Criterion, is to be occupied for operatic purposes
next Saturday night by some persons who are
also unknown at present, though that is no reason
why they should not be celebrated characters by
next Monday morning. Everybody was unknown
at some time or other, and most of them remained
that way, and most of the others ought to. Still,
this leaves all the more room for George Gard-
ner’s juvenile opera co. (which is to appear in
“Cloches de Corneville” at the time and place
above specified) to rise to well-deserved greatness.
Well-deserved greatness is about the only vacant
billet (except that of general servant) that this
writer knows of at present.
** * *
“ Cinderella,” which brings with it from
its last billet an honorable reputation as the richest
and most gorgeous pantomime that Melbourne
ever saw, is to be staged at Sydney Royal on
Monday. The management states, without fear
of contradiction, that it is going to more than live
up to its Melbourne record, and no city could ask
better than that. The rather limp “ Scarlet
Feather ” will therefore cease trying to hold itself
up in the hat of the somewhat unconvincing
monarch after Saturday night.
#** # v
The “ Convict Martyr ” goes cheerily on
for another week at Sydney Criterion, the fact
that the convict is not exhibited breaking stones
in a quarry, or making a futile attempt to escape
with the funny prisoner, no doubt helping to its
success. In this adaptation from “ Les Miser-
ables ” we see nothing of prison life except the
departure of a laced and knee-breeched Mayor to
his cfell, and his emergence therefrom some years
later with a long white beard and a dismantled
intellect. A play of convict life wherein convict
life is not depicted has always been badly wanted,
and this adaptation is a noble attempt at a great
stage reform.
»* * *
Musgrove's Opera is now ending its season
at Adelaide Royal. The S.A. public within reach
of the city has come very strongly at the new
attraction, and is much pleased with itself for
appreciating “Lohengrin” and “ Tannhauser,”
being probably unaware that these two pieces are
merely Wagner-for-the-young-beginner.
** « *
Hawtrey’s comedy is doing well in its
severalth week at Sydney Palace.
*****
Sydney old-fashioned, remote aiid almost
forgotten Royal Standard theatre has been
re-christened “The Empire” and is to be
opened on Saturday by John Fuller’s minstrel and
variety co. The new management publishes a
programme which looks promising, including a
gentleman who is described as tne “ Sad-Eyed
Shriek.” Sydney is just a trifle short of shrieks
at present, and there should be abundance of
room for this shriek, if he really is a shriek. Mr.
Fuller announces that he has secured a long lease
of the Empire—which may, or may not, be a
happy circumstance, just as things turn out.
Some things are better for being long and others
are not. Having no desire to discourage a com-
plete stranger the writer won’t say which of these
things this one probably is.
** * *
Marie Lloyd, Rickards’ most recent im-
portation, of whom a portrait is printed in this
issue, is thus enthused over by a correspondent :
Marie Lloyd is one of less than half-a-dozen entertain-
ing artists who have gone to Federaland while in their
zenith during the past quarter of a century. Bright
always, succulently plump and shapely to the eye, with a
voice containing various shades of intonation that can
sing “ Good Night ” so as to bring a blush to an English
winter sunset, Marie peregrinates to “ knock the pearls
off ” the Commonwealth’s shirt while she is at her best.
She is the most popular of all the comediennes on the
musical stage easily, and she has the gift of “ wondrous
sympathy” that seduces her auditors to help the
success of her songs. Beady art and a ready
tongue make Marie a dangerous one to bait, and
there is a stage-manager in London who has never re-
covered from sweet Marie’s retort-courteous during a cer-
tain historical argument. Finally, Marie is “ one of the
boys,” has a heart all ears for “ a tale of woe,” which her
pocket backs up, and bar the strange chances of that un-
explained prejudice of some audiences should be an un-
paralleled success down under.
** * *
On Saturday night a sweltering multitude
crowded Melb. Royal as it never was crowded
during the time of the two highly respectable
melodramas with which Bland Holt began his
Commonwealth year. Melbourne prefers the old
Sutton Vane brand of horror, and the revived “In
Sight of St. Paul’s” is seemingly one of its par-
ticular weaknesses. Just here The Bulletin rises
to apologise for having stated that “St. Paul’s” is
full of evil temptations for Walter Baker. As a
matter of fact Walter carries self-sacrifice to an
extreme length in this gaudy narrative. He
charges himself with forgery for the sake of
his best girl’s brother, and he suffers tribulation
in a third-floor back in order that the thin young
bounder aforesaid may continue to lavish the
wages of sin upon an alluring female named the
Panther, who runs a gilded den and gives a birth-
day party to a lot of ladies no better than herself.
Luckily Walter’s aged father discovers the true
state of the forgery case and becomes reconciled
to his offspring by means of a polite idiot. After
the idiot and other virtuous characters have cele-
brated their happiness with a perri winkle tea in
the vicinity of Baker’s humble sofa bedstead the
scene changes to the haunts of giddiness, where
the fragile bounder gets shot by Albert Norman —
Norman being wicked as usual—and the birth-
day party bursts into flames. A decidedly
wooden actor, who was onetime married to the
Panther, wanders into her den and demands res-
titution of conjugal rights, whereupon her faithful
Norman shoots the intruder. And because the
uncalled-for husband is not as dead as the guilty
pair would like him to be, they place him down-
stairs or somewhere among the bottles and leave
him to drink himself madder than ever, with the
result that he sets fire to the premises. Conse-
quently the Panther is burnt alive, in addition to
receiving a fatal bullet from a pistol. The pistol
missed fire on Saturday, but the dissolute adven-
turess managed to perish all the same.
• * * *
“In Sight of St. Paul’s” reflects much
credit on scenic artist Brunton and the property-
man and the management. It has one or more
striking pictures in each of the five acts. Cabs
and omnibusses and costermongers’ barrows en-
liven its course of crime, and the comic relief is
strong in small domestic details of the kind that
delight mixed audiences. Tea and toast are in-
troduced with brilliant success, also the sofa-bed-
stead, and the succulent winkle elicit shrieks of
approbation. In its sinful aspects the drama
loses by comparison with the “ St. Paul’s” of four
years ago. Miss Harrie Ireland, the most natural
of nice girls in broad, light comedy, has no quali-
fications for the Panther, barring a tall, embrace-
able figure, a glorious autumnal-tinted head of
hair, and a yellow-silk dress. Furthermore, the
wooden husband is a lamentable alteration in the
cast, whilst the thin young man seems a poor sub-
stitute for his fleshy predecessor. For the rest,
Walter Baker, Miss Ross, the Bland Holts, Cor-
lesse, and Miss Seymour are well placed, and the
omnibus deserves a special word of praise.
*# * *
Last nights are the rule at other Mel-
bourne theatres. The farewells of ‘ ‘ Florodora ”
will extend to Easter week apparently. Wil-
liamson’s mascotte musical comedy has taken a
fresh impetus from Irene Franklin, who sings
three of the songs which were old before she re-
tired from Rickards’s co., and doesn’t treat them
quite so daintily as at first. Or perhaps the
limelight they shed upon Irene at Her Majesty’s
is a little too strong for the damsel’s buxom in-
genuousness. Anyhow, the difference between
the Irene of now and aforetime is not remarked
by “Florodora” patrons, who, in a general way
of speaking, accept the chicken ditty as a new
item.
** * *
The masherlike Barabbas, of Jerusalem,
was to terminate his early Christian career at
Melbourne Princess’s on Tuesday, and “The
Gates of Bondage,” a drama that might easily be
mistaken for Wilson Barrett’s “ Ben-my-Chree,”
is running to-night (Thursday) and to-morrow if
the advt. has not changed its mind.
** * *
Next Saturday (16th), the Musgrove
Opera Co. returned to the Princess’s for a short
season. New operas and new artists are immi-
nent, but the programme for the first week in-
cludes only revivals of “ Lohengrin ” (3), “ Trova-
tore ” (1), “ Bohemian Girl,” and “ Carmen” —the
popular Spanish story, with the bull-fight behind
the scenes, coming first on the list.
** # *
Con. Rodney, the monologuer, is putting
in his appointed fortnight at the Masonic Hall,
with payable results. The limelight has been
brought to a proper sense of duty, also the tem-
perature of Melbourne is more favorable to an
entertainment which depends largely on the cool-
ness of the man who stands to be criticised
for two solid hours. Rodney is cool and
genial. He talks to the people in front as
though they had come there to show their regard
for himself and the grey-haired young Henderson
at the piano, and he sings to them in the same
strain. One night last week, when his throat
was very husky, he made the audience feel that
they were putting a severe strain on his good
nature and ought to pay extra for the privilege.
* * * #-
The Bio-Tableau at the Athenaeum Hall
has added a view of Hugh Ward’s dance (from
“ Florodora ”) to its films, and the second week of
the return season finds business more than satis-
factory.
George Reid hasn’t mentioned that alleged
Freetrade dog, that was thrown in the water and
learned to swim, for fully three weeks.
Mr. James C. Flynn, M.P. for North
Cork in the Commons, is a sarcastic person.
When the House was discussing the pay of the
Australian contingents in China, said Mr. Flynn:
“ The fact that payment is claimed is in itself evi-
dence of the very high character of colonial
loyalty.” Yes, Mr. Flynn, the Australian may
kill a Chinaman at home for fun, but when he
goes abroad to do it he expects good money, and
plenty of it. That is the usual difference between
the amateur and the professional sport.

The most notable feature about these figures is that New
South Wales has had the biggest month’s increase it has
yet shown this financial year—bigger than the record of
last October. Also that Tasmania lias started its financial
year with two decreases, which appears to suggest that its
glittering boom is over, and that a lean period may be
coming on the island.

For four of the States (Westralia, N.S.W., South Aus-
tralia, and Queensland) expenditure accounts are avail-
able, and it is a subject for solemn reflection that in every
case, except that of N.S.W., the financial situation is
worse than it was at this time last year. In the case of
N.S.W. it would be worse but for the*bold and noble fake
of Treasury-Bill Lyne in reckoning as revenue the receipts
for resumed property which is not paid for, and regard-
ing which the interest on the purchase money is unpaid
and unprovided for. Taking Lyne at his own figures,
however, this is how things stand

What things would have been like in N.S.W.
if Treasury-Bill Lyne had not improved his revenue by
the receipts from the resumed area is another story.
But last year in N.S.W. closed with a deficit of £117,901,
which was artfully disguised and turned into a surplus
by putting some heavy items of expenditure into the
floating debt. And this year there is about £70,000 more
interest to pay on the public debt; the Commonwealth
celebration accounts are not all paid yet; the Duke of
York celebration accounts are still to come in ; there is
further war expenditure to provide for; Lyne is pledged
to pay £250,000 more than he did last year in redemption
of the floating debt; and the interest bill (now about
eight months in arrear) on the cost of the resumed area
must be met some day. Also next year the Old Age
Pensions are to be paid. An independent committee of
inquiry into Lyne’s finances will soon be badly wanted.
South Australia finished last financial year with
a deficit of about £13,459, which was turned into a paper
surplus by a shabby little misappropriation of certain
trust funds. And things are already £32,750 worse than
they were last year.
Queensland finished last financial year with a
more or less reliable surplus of not quite £48,000. Things
are already £457,955 worse than they were last year. Wes-
tralia ended last year with a surplus of nearly £260,000. This
year it seems likely, despite a huge increase of revenue (the
biggest in Australia in proportion to population), to just
about make ends meet and no more. The present year is
commonly called one of returning prosperity. If pros-
perity returns much more four Treasurers seem likely to
go broke.

The Insurance Record of 20th July, 1900,
writes with reference to the Colonial Mutual Life Assur-
ance Society, Limited : “ The Society’s transactions
during 189!) are calculated to afford considerable satis-
faction to all connected with the institution and the
management are to be congratulated upon having brought
affairs to so successful an issue during troublous and un-
settled times.*
* * * *
We are pleased to note that the Melbourne and
Metropolitan Board of Works have placed the order for
the new pumping engine for the Spottiswood Station with
a colonial firm, the Austral Otis Engineering Company,
South Melbourne, being the successful tenderers against
competitors from all parts of the world. The engine to
be supplied is a vertical triple expansion, and has a
capacity of 12,000,000 gallons of sewerage per 24 hours.
The whole work is to be completed and the engine
running in 18 months.*
* * » •
Gentleman having only a limited time at their
disposal can obtain the practical instruction in assaying
and metallurgical work, necessary to qualify them for
obtaining good positions, in the ore testing works of Gil-
fillan and McCreery, Stock Exchange Buildings, Melb.*
* * * *
If you want to borrow money, go to N.S.W.
Mont de Piete Co., Ltd., 74 Castlereagh-street, 74.
Eustace Bennett, Manager.*
Messrs. Plummer Love and Co., 249 George-
street, Sydney, are large suppliers of all mining requi-
sites. They are sole agents here for the now celebrated
“ British” brand of explosives, detonators and fuse ; also
for the Taylor Horsfield Improved National Bock Drills,
Air Compressors, etc., etc.*
* * * *
Since the start of the Broken Hill Proprietary’s
new 5000-ton mill, most of the Barrier mines have been
engaged in remodelling their concentrating plants on up-
to-date lines, and wisely show a preference for machines
which have proved most successful on the difficult fines
and slimes. The machines that can claim this distinction
are the “ Luhrig ” Vanners, as supplied by Messrs. W. &
J. Lempriere, of Melbourne. The South’s new mill will
include a dozen or so, and the Junction Co. has also de-
cided to erect a number of Luhrig Vanners as an impor-
tant adjunct to its large plant. Other mines would do
well to follow this good example.*
* ' * *• *
Norman Godkin, of 31 Queen-street, Melbourne,
is prepared to purchase or develope, with option of
purchase, “big prospecting shows” (either gold or
mineral) in any part of Australia. Properties offered
must be subject to inspection. Cable address: “Option,”
Melbourne.*
* * * *
Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footscray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts, nuts, forgings, andall sorts ofiron-work.*

The management of the Union Bank announced about
five years ago that it might be necessary to provide
£250,000 to cover depreciation of assets, and that amount
was transferred from reserve to contingency fund for the
purpose of covering these anticipated losses. For some
time thereafter the Union Bank had a reserve-fund of
£750,000, and a contingency reserve of £250,000. The
latter was never used, however, for the purpose origin-
ally intended, and as the losses are gradually written
off out of earnings corresponding sums are restored from
contingency-fund to reserve. The two now stand: Reserve-
fund, £825,000 ; contingency-reserve, £175,000.
The Union Bank certainly shows a very
material improvement, though the last half-year’s profits
are not up to the very high level of February, 1900.
Latest profits are at the rate of £6 6s. Bd. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ total funds—an exceedingly
respectable figure in these times and considerably above
the average. Reserves of cash and liquid securities are
no less than £5,554,998, equal to about 32 per cent, of all
liabilities to the public. Also deposits are 15J millions,
and note circulation is close on £440,000. The Union
Bank’s record dividend was 36 per cent, in 1854, but it
paid 14 per cent, as recently as 1891, and 12 per cent, as
recently as 1893. Apparently there is a fair prospect of
getting back to the latter figure before long.

The paper margin of surviving capital which stands
between the depositors and absolute insolvency is grow-
ing rather small nowadays. The gross revenue of the
Sydney and Suburban last year was £2483, equal to
£2 os. sd. per cent, per annum on the nominal value of
the assets. The trouble lies in the fact that it owes about
£97,000 to depositors and mortgagees, and the average
interest payable on these liabilities appears to be nearly
£3 10s. per cent. A concern which borrows large sums
at 3£ per cent.., and can only invest them to earn 2 per
cent., is lost or thereabouts. If the Sydney and Suburban
could call upabout£4o,ooo new capital to pay off its more
expensive mortgages, it couldmeetits other outgoings out
of earnings and save its assets until better times arrive,
but it has practically no callable capital left, and it can
only raise the money to pay interest with by the constant
sacrifice of its assets. The 3 per cent, deposits have
sellers at Bs. in the £, which is probably their full value.
In fact, if the present order of things continues, interest
payments alone will in time swallow up the assets, and
leave no principal at all for the depositors.
* * » «
The Sydney Permanent Freehold Land and
Building 00. (Sydney), which can put in a pretty good
claim to rank amongst the most lamentable building
societies in Australia, showed no sign of improvement in
1900. This institution, in 1895, obtained a reduction of
interest on deposits to 2£ per cent., and in 1896 it made
further arrangements by which it altogether ceased to
pay interest. It is still in that condition, and thus gets
the use of £84,913 of deposit money absolutely gratis;
yet even on these specially favorable terms it made a loss
during 1900. This is the situation in recent years :

In 1897 the co. owed £119,381 to outside creditors
(£104,264 to depositors and £15,117 to mortgagees &c.)
It now owes £94,898 (£84,913 to depositors and £9985 to
mortgagees). The reduction of £5132 in the mortgages
means a considerable saving in interest, but the reduction
of £19,351 in the deposits is the repayment of money
which bore no interest whatever. In other words the co.
has £19,351 less funds to earn revenue with, and no
corresponding reduction in the outgoings, and as a natural
consequence the income keeps diminishing badly. Also
the assets appear to be growing worse rapidly. The total
receipts in 1897 were £2936, equal to £1 Bs. Bd. per cent, per
annum on the nominal value of the assets. In 1900 they
were £1192, equal to only 17s. per cent, on the nominal
value of the assets, though said nominal value has
been greatly written down in the interval. Assuming
that the assets yield even the very moderate rate
of 3 per cent, on their real present value they are
worth rather less than £40,000, at which rate the capital
is wholly lost, and the Sydney Permanent Freehold Land
and Building 00. is only good for about 9s. in the £ of its
remaining liabilities to depositors and other animals. It
is an unlucky thing in Australia to put the word Per-
manent in the name of a building society or land co. The
more Permanent it is called the more temporary it is apt
to be.

Mort’s Dock and Engineering Co. (Sydney) has
now completed four half-years since the co. was enlarged
and re-constituted. The results are shown underneath.
The fact that the increase in the reserves has no accurate
visible relation to the amount of profit remaining after
payment of the dividend is a matter for which the pre-
sent writer takes no responsibility. The reserve-fund of
Mori’s Dock Co. seems to increase by additions from in-
visible sources as well as in the ordinary way. For in-
stance, it stood at £18,491 in December. 1899, and £3COO
was then added to it, which might reasonably have made
it £21,491. Instead, however, it appeared at June, 1900,
as £21,715. The situation may be summarised thus:

The N.S.W. Fresh Food and Ice Co. (Sydney),
whose profits have been declining for some time past, is
unable to pay any dividend for the half-year just ended.
The following figures cover a period of two years
Profits, less . Total
deprecia- Dividends. Reserves.
June, 1899 .. £6,256 8 per cent. =£6,120 .. £32,726
:: 8S S || .
Dec., 1900 .. 587 Nil 32,392
The co. makes its reserves a little larger on paper by .in-
cluding as an asset the very remarkable paper item Re-
construction Expenses, £810.” The real figures are as
shown above : Undivided profit (less the reconstruction
expenses”), £201; reserve fund, £18,750; and redemp-
tion fund, £13,441. The real meaning of the last item
is rather uncertain, but the writer includes it among the
reserves on spec. —presumably it is a reserve of some
kind. The Fresh Food and Ice 00. appears to have bank
overdrafts amounting to over £22,000. They were a little
over £BOOO at June, 1899, but have been steadily growing.
The asset which has been growing fastest on the other
side is “ sundry debtors ” ; it has risen from £lB,OOO odd
at July, 1899, to over £31,000. This may only mean that
the co. is doing a larger business, or it may mean that it
is doing a less safe business, and finds it harder to collect
its accounts. And as the earnings grow less the provi-
sion for wear and tear of plant also grows less ; for the
year ended July, 1899, the amount allowed for wear and
tear of plant was £6OOO, while for the year to December,
1900, it was only £3500. This may be sufficient, but it
looks doubtful in view of the much larger sums written
off when profits were greater. In one way and another
the affairs of the Fresh Food and Ice 00. appear none too
promising at present.
* * * *
The Jennings Centrifugal Dredging Co., Ade-
long, N.S.W., seems to have thrown up the sponge. The
Jennings Proprietary 00., Melbourne, of which it was
originally an offshoot, has proved a disastrous failure.
Lead seems to be firming a little ; but the
Broken Hill co.’s advisers have given up encouraging the
hope that the price will go back to £l6 and stay there (it
went up to £lB last half-year); they talk now about com-
mercial depression, and forecast £l5 as a likely average
figure for some time. Besides, the closing-down of the
Junction and the J. North, the South and North have
been compelled to “ pass ” their ordinary divs. It is pro-
posed to amalgamate the Junction and Junction North ;
the former is by far the better mine, but the latter has a
much-needed good and deep shaft, in addition to modern
machinery. Both the Junction’s shafts are cork-screwed ;
that, indeed, is one of its worst troubles. Amalgamation
ought to be welcomed. The lead slump has wet-blanketed
the Victoria and South Blocks, situated one at each
end of the main lode, revived during last year’s lead
boom; nevertheless, what has been done at the South
Blocks is mightily encouraging. Sydney shareholders
have met and decided to give the North Central “ one
more trial.” Such faith and confidence are really
touching.
• • * *
B.H. Water Supply sold 17,871,000 gals, water
during February, as against 24,933,000 gals, during
January. And yet the rain didn’t fall until February was
nearly half through. Now March has opened with more
rains! The figures show what a mess the present wet
season is going" to make of the co.’s profits.
• * * *
Cobar Gold Mines (N.S.W.): Crushed last year
with a 100-head stamp-mill over 67,000 tons, and made a
gross profit of £22,412. After allowing for depreciation
of plant and machinery and other expenditure incurred,
a net balance £10,368 is carried forward. Mining costs,
4s. 3d. per ton ; milling, 2s. fid. ; cyanide, 3s. Sd. ; in all,
10s. sd. Average assay of ore over the entire year, Bdwt.
2gr. At time of meeting 43,000 tons of slimes had accu-
mulated ; there are now about 50,000 tons —estimated to
contain from 5 to 6dwt. per ton, but the assay average
over the year is 7dwt. After experiments in London and
elsewhere filter-press treatment was determined on ; the
plant, costing over £6OOO, is in course of erection ; will be
completed in a few weeks. Expected that these slimes will
give a profit of 12s. 6d. per ton, and that the co. will then
enter upon a regular div-paying career. Average width
of lode 40ft., and the ore reserves are practically un-
limited. At the meeting compliments were passed on the
managing director in N.S.W. —Mr. T. Bucklaud—and on
the mining manager, Mr. J. Fox, for the good work they
have done to bring about the success of this mine. The
experience of the Cobar Gold Mines should act as a
warning in the opening up of N.S.W. low grade mines.
Before it was taken over by the English Co. a local one,
known as the Fort Bourke, crushed from the property
over 30,000 tons for an average of 6dwts. Extraction
only averaged 50 per cent.—a fact established by
subsequent cyanidation of the tailings. The full
width of the lode was not taken, wherever
possible the best parts were gutted out ; some-
what impoverishing the main body left around them.
Only reasonable to assume that the lower levels, which
have not been worked in this manner, will keep up the
average of last year’s returns. If so, and filter-press
treatment of the slimes is a success, the mine will un-
doubtedly be a steady div.-payer for many years. The
English Co. started with a working capital of £35,000 ;
exhausted it in the erection of a 100-head stamp mill
and the excavation of the largest reservoir in Cobar,
then came reconstruction, and another £35,000 was called
up for the erection of cyanide and other plant and for
mine development. Amalgamation without cyanide
proved a failure, and milling with cyanide proved incom-
plete because of the quantity of slimes, representing about
40 per cent, of the ore—hence the enormous accumula-
tion. This asset may safely be said to be worth £30,000,
and the filter-pressing plant to deal with it will be big
enough to take the slimes from the battery, and to gra-
dually absorb the accumulated heaps. So, after one re-
• construction, and many tribulations, the Cobar Gold
Mines looks like having become an enduring property.
• * * •
Occidental G.M. Co., Cobar, which started with
a very small capital and got most of its now decent equip-
ment out of the ground as it went along, has 50 stampers
in position, and is greatly enlarging its dam-capacity ; at
present, like several other local mines, milling and cyanid-
ing are suspended for want of water. When the battery
again starts it will be fitted with self-feeding gear and
will have the aid of rock-breakers. For the half-year
14,519 tons stone were crushed for 17120 z. gold ; per
cyanide there came 1500 oz. gold. £2lOO were paid in divs.
The prospects of the mine look very healthy indeed.
* * * *
Eleanora (N.S.W.): The main north shaft has
collapsed, and to save the poppet-legs a wire-rope was
placed on top and a charge of dynamite under them—
they came away in a chaotic mass, were smashed to
smithereens, and round the shaft there is a barbed-wire
enclosure with notices warning the public not to trespass.
And this is the mine which was going to do great things
when a change of management took place two or three
years ago. Heavy rain has set in, and the local opinion
is that the mine will cave ; in some places there is not
a stick of timber for hundreds of feet, and the few work-
able places are being gouged by tributers.
• * * »
The water-question is now a very serious matter
at Oobar. The townspeople are in sore straits for a
supply, and large numbers of sheep are being removed
from the district to save their lives. The Great Oobar
Copper Mine has arranged for water with the Oobar Gold
Mines, in whose tank there is about 9ft., which will last
both mines for a month. The Great Oobar must be pay-
ing through the nose for this concession, as manager
Buckland, of the C.G.M., is not likely to risk gratis the
imminent catastrophe of having his 100-head battery
hung-up for want of water.
* * * *
Nearly all the Oobar mines are either shut down
or on the verge of closing for want of water.
Long Tunnel Extended has fallen heavily in the
market, owing to the Long Tunnel’s suspension of work
having led to a big increase of water in the L.T.E.
* * * «
“Pelican”: The Mt. Morgan Co. has never
been “hungry” to employes. Witness, cost of treat-
ment in 1890, over £3 a ton; in 1900, about 12s.
6d., and no reduction in wages. 00. spent its money
in costly experiments and employment of brains to bring
about desired result. Has there been a strike at M.M. ?
Is it not a well-known fact that the M.M. Co., any day it
chose, could lower the wages of men working in open-cut
without being considered a sweater of worst degree ? Re
water supply. This question for the last five years has
been an all-absorbing one with the directorate,
and every endeavour has been made to solve it.
To obtain a supply of water from same source as Rock-
hampton town is supplied—a stagnant lagoon, by no
means inexhaustible —consent of R.T.C. would first have
to be obtained ; and secondly, not only 24 miles of piping
provided, but a huge power-house would also be neces-
sary to force water over the watershed between Dee and
Fitzroy. In ordinary seasons, enough water comes down
Dee at freshes to fill the 220,000,0000ga1. dam many, many
times over ; to this must be added the inflow from Dairy
Creek, which joins the Dee immediately above central
dam.
* * • *
“W.M.8.” from Ebagoolah (N.Q.): On a
former visit to this gold-field in Sept, last I said the
reefs would turn out well and give some handsome
crushings. During the last month, the Caledonia P.C.
crushed 38 tons for 2690z5. ; an average of 7oz. 3dwt. per
ton. This has always been looked upon as a good thing,
and a Melbourne syndicate is after it. Shaft down 70ft.
and reef solid. The All Nations P.C. crushed 121 tons for
600ozs ; May Queen, 31 tons for lOlozs. ; Trafalgar, 50 tons
for 3700z5., and Golden Treasure, 8j tons for 150ozs.
These results make me certain that the future of the field
is assured, though a serious drawback is the distance of
the battery and consequent high cost of cartage and
crushing. Not much is being done in alluvial—about
200ozs. a month. One nice slug I saw weighed 2Jlbs.
Water still scarce, but competition has cheapened pro-
visions to reasonable rates. No use going to Ebagoolah
without 6 months rations.
* * * *
Einasleigh Copper, Ltd, writes a good miner to
Thk Bulletin, promises to be one of the mines of
N.Q. Lately struck the lode at 120 ft. deep, the crosscut
proving it 31ft. thick, averaging over 8 per cent, for the
whole width. As the drive extended south, values in-
creased, that part of the lode carrying the crosscut
averaging 12£ per cent. Is situated at junction of
Einasleigh and Oopperfield rivers, which are dry nine
months of the year except for large pools along the beds
at intervals from a quarter to three miles. Immediately
opposite the mine there is a broken range of hills from
300 to 700 ft. high. On the western bank flat country ex-
tends for miles. The plain is a decomposed rock called
basalt but very high in silica. This basalt has flowed
across the river, completely filling up the old channel,
burying the country over a large area, and has hidden all
traces of the lode outcrop, save in one spot, where there
was at one time a huge knob of ore which was broken off
and washed down by the floods for a quarter-of-a-inile.
Blocks up to a ton in weight are now lying on, or buried
in the river sands, so there was but a small spot 100 ft.
long where the lode outcrop was kt all visible. A shaft
was started to allow for the lode dipping west, but it has
now been found to dip in the opposite direction. A fault
has cut the lode almost fair in two, so that in one place it
hangs together by but a few threads of ore, but no doubt
it will make again as the fault is small. The country
rock on the western side of the lode is worth from 2
to 3 per cent, worth of copper impregnated much the same
as at Cobar walls of the lode are, and, as at Cobar, there
is no distinct parting between ore and country. There is
not much doubt that the ore will average from 12 to 14
per cent, copper, with probably 2 to 3dwt. of gold and 1 to
4oz. of silver. The mine has disabilities. It must pay at
least 25 per cent, more wages than further south, and
probably get at least 25 per cent, less work done for it—
practically an increase in wages of 50 per cent. Stores
cost from 50 to 200 per cent more than N.S.W. mines
have to pay, and the Einasleigh has to pay £l2 per ton to
get them there ! Then there is a wet season of three
months when haulage is impossible. A 5-gal. drum of gas-
tar, for instance, costs 12s. on the mine, it is only worth
Is. 3d. in Sydney ! Gal. iron costs'£36 per ton, bar iron
£26 to £29, fire-bricks from £45 to ' 60 per thousand,
according to the make 1 The necessaries of life are from
150 to 200 per cent, more than Sydney prices ; back car-
riage £8 per ton from Townsville—via Chillagoe, £5 to
the line, and Chillagoe railway rates, wharfage, &c.,
would bring it up to £7, according to the goods. At pre-
sent rates of carriage, mining and other costs, even 15
per cent, ore could not be sent away for treatment with-
out loss. Einasleigh’s only salvation is railway com-
munication, for it is a very sanguine man who will expect
the lode to average 15 per cent, copper. The Einasleigh
is 110 miles from Chillagoe—a survey may bring it down
to 100. A 2ft.-gauge line would cost from £lOOO to £llOO
per mile, not including bridges. If this money can be
raised the mine is a very valuable one; it is worthless
without.

* » * *
Lake View Consols : Sulphide plant, 2600 tons,
36400 z. ; Diehl plant, 6095 tons, 55000 z. ; 180 tons of con-
centrates for 29100 z. Total production, 8695 tons for
12,0500 z. Stated that the Diehl plant was running 35
days. The public may be reminded of the fact that it
was several times announced to have started in Dec. ; it
was supposed to be running in Jan. ; nobody suspected
that it was not running without a break in Feb. ; and yet,
in the second week of March, it is declared that 35 days
represents the total run. Which is it ? Something wrong
with the plant- or the newspapers ? Anyhow, there is a
cleaning-up at last, which is something to be thankful
for, and from the two plants there is the production of a
little over 12,0000 z. we have heard so much talk about.
For months past, the public have been told that the
production of this mine as soon as the Diehl process
got to work would be from 1200 to 1800 oz. a month.
The margin was wide—but that was regarded as quite a
natural circumstance with this particular mine.
At a L.Y. shareholders’ meeting in London six
weeks ago the chairman said “ there was no reason why
they should not have an output of 12,0000 z. per month.”
A shareholder meekly asked ‘ ‘ Fop how long ? ” The reply
was, “ For a considerable time ” —and it seemed to carry
conviction. Chairman also admitted that miners
working in the mine were in league with people work-
ing out of it, that telegraph-operators were not so
reliable as they ought to be, and unless wires were sent
in the most difficult cypher their contents were made
known ; that gold was being stolen—and they had other
troubles to contend with. The cyanide decision of the
Privy Council had been in their favor, and there was no
foundation for the statement that the London and Globe
owes them any money—the mine had nothing to do with
the London and Globe. How much more satisfactory it
would have been if the chairman could have given the
truthful assurance that the London and Globe had
nothing to do with the mine !
One shareholder tried to censure the directors
for taking their commission of £10,274 as provided
by the articles of association in addition to their
fees. That seemed to him dishonest—but it didn’t
seem so to the meeting, as his motion disapprov-
ing of its payment and calling on the directors to
resign was promptly negatived. M‘Kin non, the mining
manager, said when he took charge, now nearly a year ago,
lie thoroughly sampled the mine, and his report was
simply a plain, unvarnished statement of the facts. The
ore-reserves were given as 65,000 tons and subsequently
increased to 71,000 tons. He admitted the quantity was
very disappointing after the figures of the previous year,
but it was entirely due to the cessation of development
work. The mine should have been developed to 1000 ft.
instead of 500 ft. by now. In his opinion they had again
got into a payable zone. Was it ever officially declared
that the mine had got into an unpayable zone? He told
the shareholders that the future of the mine depended on
the results of development-work. He thought it would
live in depth and confirmed the chairman’s statement
with regard to 12,0000 z. per month production.
And in the face of all this, and after a drop of
nearly two millions, this mine has still a market value of
a little over two millions! ! It has not paid a div. for 12
months, and it only paid one of 10s. in the early part of
last year. Its average gold production for the year has
been between 7000 and 8000ozs., from which it must be
inferred that the whole of the proceeds have gone in min-
ing and other expenses, and in the absence of the divi-
dend the modest, unassuming directors have collared
over £lO,OOO in addition to their fees, and an attempt to
censure them is lost by an “overwhelming majority,”
and the directors go on their way rejoicing ! ! Truly
John Bull is a milch-cow with a calf-like disposition.

Hobart cable in S. M. Herald : “A party of
M.sP. and leading local promoters took a trip to see the
works of the Great Western Railway on Friday. Mr.
Palmer, managing director, read parts of Mr. Reginald
Murray’s report on the mineral prospects en route, which
states that the Barnbluff find on one of the company’s
blocks alone has a formation of 8,000,000 tons of copper-
ore-hearing rock. As an estimate he thinks this will go
25 per cent, of copper, and would prove payable under
economical treatment. He concludes that the poten-
tialities as regards mineral wealth on the route traversed
cannot be yet estimated.” Just imagine a paper spread-
ing a yarn to the effect that a man like Reginald Murray
thinks that 8,000,000 tons of %5 per cent, copper-ore will
pay if economically handled ! But, despite this mighty
bloomer, it looks as if the G.W. Shindykitters had struck
what they believe to be an immensely valuable tract of
mineral country in the land grant given them by a legis-
lature many of the members of which, if they don’t de-
serve to be in gaol, ought to be in the Asylum for Con-
genital Idiots.

Ross Flat claim is allegedly the richest gold
spot on the west coast of Maorilavh but for 20 years it
has been in a flooded state, and all attempts to get'rid of
the water have failed, the cost running into many thou-
sands. Now expected that newer machinery and better
appliances will do the work ; total expenditure being put
down at £50,000. Pretty stiff ; but the claim will likely
return between 400 and 500 oz. a week. The money has
been practically secured, and Govt, has promised a sub-
sidy of £15,000.

Parkes’s vain and forgotten Dead House
idea comes up again. The O’Sullivan and Treasury-
Bill Lyne are dreaming about fooling away some
good borrowed money in putting up a building in
Sydney Centennial Park, or somewhere, in honor of
the men who fell in S’Africa. What the building
will be used for in a general way, besides being a
receptacle for dust and rats, is an open question,
yet it will be necessary to find something to put
in it besides a damp, earthy smell —it can’t be
merely four walls and a door and an echo. Still
it is cheering to the live and starving Cornstalk
soldiers who wore out their boots and their hearts
calling a,t the Treasury for their lawful money and
not getting it, to hear that their dead brethren
are to get a building. And if Treasury-Bill Lyne
will put in that structure, where everybody can
see it, a receipt for all the war-expenses, showing
that the whole thing was honorably paid out of
revenue, and not faked into the loan expenditure,
the new Dead House won’t be quite so empty after
all.
* • * *
Writes a correspondent of S.M. Herald,
re the proposed N.S.W. State House “ If the
Government wishes to do something practical,
let it find employment for the soldiers who
have returned from South Africa; and, if
a further suggestion is needed, let it build
a wing of the proposed Consumptives’ Hospital.”
To which The Bulletin says Amen ! And there
are not lacking many other reasonable alterna-
tives. Why worry about the dead who fought for
five shillings a day and to get cheap labour for
Rhodes and co., while the N.S.W. dead-beat poor,
hundreds of them worn-out pioneers of Australian
civilisation, are styed like pigs in inflam-
mable asylums which may any day or night
roast the majority of a thousand men ? And
just look at Liverpool (N.S.W.) so-called Benevo-
lent Asylum, in which hundreds of consumptives
are kept closely within walls so that their spittle
may not be wafted to the people of the adjoining
town—this practical imprisonment within walls
being in utter defiance of what is known to all
intelligent people concerning the requirements of
sufferers from phthisis.

jtte the alleged “Alteration” of the cor-
ridor ears by Vic. Railway Department for the
use of the Yorks. The Commissioner was
authorised to build 50 corridor-cars. Forty-eight

each. Two “Royals” are now being built ( not

day and night to get them finished in time. So
much for the ingenious, economical-sounding
yarn promulgated to humbug the credulous public.
HARD LUCK.
KIND LADY: “ Well, Luv, wot yer crying for?"
LUV: “ Please , lady, our Sunday-school picnic’s
on Queen’s Birthday, en now there ain’t no
Queen ter ’are birthdays.”
A ridiculous impression prevails among
some people that the death of the Queen has
altered the title of everything that bore her name.
In Melbourne, for instance, several prominent
orators (and one of the daily papers) have begun
to refer to the Queen’s Hall in the local Parlia-
ment House (so called from the presence of a big
statue of the late sovereign) as “ the King’s Hall.”
Others have started styling the Queen’s bridge
the King’s bridge,” and one is in hourly expec-
tation of hearing some politician refer to the
Kanaka question in “Kingsland.”
* * * *
When one reads the columns of abject
crawl in Melb. Age each morning re the prepara-
tions for the Duke’s reception, it is well to re-
member what the same said 30 years ago on the
arrival of the Duke of Edinburgh. The Argus in
describing the illumination of the Public Library
said “ Cressets of flame adorned the portals of the
edifice.” The Age report of the same decoration
was : “On the gate-posts were stuck two burning
pots of fat.” By the way, as Australia is gravely
threatened in several directions with another big
outbreak of Plague, very little indeed may be
seen by the general public of the immediate heir
to the throne of England, even should he come to
Australia.
* * * *
A correspondent of Brisbane Courier
pleads earnestly for a revision and amendment of
S. Queensland nomenclature “before-the Duke of
York comes.” Particularly deprecated are the
names “ Boggo Road ” and “ Woolloongabba.”
* * * »
Already in Melbourne, in view of the
coming York gambols, the Presbyterians have
raised the perennial precedence question. They
want the representatives of the different sects to
walk all in a row, or, if that cannot be managed,
that the numerically strongest sect shall have its
man in front. It may cool the ardor of these
alleged followers of Him whose Kingdom is not of
this world to learn that, if it came to numbers of
actual adherents, the Salvarmy would possibly be
on top !
* * * *
All things have an ending—even the
notorious Mercadool land case. Cable tells that
the Privy Council has given judgment in favor of
JST.S. W. Govt. (Minister for Lands), overruling
N.S.W. Full Court. The facts of the case are so
involved that even those implicated in the alleged
swindle will not venture to unravel the skein.
Only one thing is certain—that the P.C. decision
means loss irreparable to the then owners of the
Mercadool station. It is pleasing, however, to
remember that lawyers glutted themselves to the
full over the Mercadool case, the costs for the 10
years over which the case has lasted amounting to
a big fortune.
* * • *
The result of the Privy Council appeal in
the Mercadool case is a victory for the Land
Board and the Law Appeal Court, and a set-back
for the Supreme Court. And the sting is the
worse because some of the Judges, notably the C. J.,
simply snorted over the incapacity of the lower
officials. Mr. Freeman, the Land Board chair-
man, who dealt with the case in the first instance,
was so shockingly worried by it that a break-down,
followed by a compulsory sea-trip to Europe, fol-
lowed ; and even then he couldn’t feel comfort-
able again in the Mercadool district, so secured a
temporary exchange to Bourke.
* * * «
The mills of God grind slowly, but they
generally “ arrive there. ” Quite a number of Vic-
torian women who say they have reached
the pension age, and who most probably
have done so, have to wait several years
yet because their only documentary evi-
dence lies in their marriage certificates,
and away back in the ’GO’s and ’7o’s they
understated their ages, and so put them-
selves out of court. Now, they say with
tears that they were really 30, though they
put themselves down as 25, but the law
refuses to go behind the written testimony.
* * * *
A proposal is on foot, to widen part
of Elizabeth-street, Sydney, by the usual
expedient of stealing a bit of the park. It
isn’t to be a very large bit, but, all the
same, the people’s pleasure-ground is slowly
and steadily vanishing. All round it little
fragments are being gnawed off now and
then—always with the very best inten-
tions. The greater part of Elizabeth-street,
by the way, and the worst part, doesn’t
abut on the park, and it will presumably be
left as it is.
* * * *
The new N.S.W. medical law which
prohibits doctors from trading under two
or more names should inspire a similar law
for money-lenders. One Sydney money-
lender trades under five different titles, and
very few are satisfied with one title. Eng-
land has got ahead in this respect. The
Act passed there last Parliament has torn
the disguises from a host of vampires. Such
a title as “The Bank of Pimlico” was found
to be run by two compositors, and a score or so of
women and men harpies were at the bottom of
three or four or more each of high-sounding
financial institutions.

Brisbane Courier item :
FILTERS IN SCHOOLS.
Several quotations lor the supply of filters for use in
State schools have been made to the Department of
Public Instruction, and the matter has been referred to
the Works Department for report. In view of the stale
of the public finances, however, it is not likely that any-
thing will be done this financial year.
When so much money is wanted for African
farm-burning, there is none left to supply the
school-children, even in plague-time, with drink-
ing water likely not to be dangerous to life.
* * * *
The latest five-bob-a-day contingent from
N.S.W. for S’Afriea sets out under, as usual,
officers drawn from the class which, during the
big labor-strike, arrayed themselves in Bedford-
cord breeches and blue jumpers as amateur
mounted constables—to undertake, if necessary,
the not uncongenial task of riding down the re-
calcitrant bush-workers. And now a lot of these
very bush-workers go across sea to burn farm-
houses in order that Rhodes, Beit, Rothschild and
Co. may be able to squeeze white men’s (to say
nothing of niggers’) wages. As to the political
part of the war, the bush-worker is allowing him-
self to be led by his Pastoralists’-Union officer on
an expedition conceived wholly and solely in
order that squatter Bull might chuck the little
Boer cocky out of the selection into which he had
put so many years’ hard graft.

Rumor among the legal brotherhood has
it that a well-known solicitor, who has been called
upon to “show cause,” attributes all his trouble to
an “ after-dinner ” conversation between him and
another member of the profession in which he (the
attorney in trouble) let the cat out of the bag.
“Gent, one,” in his affidavit (read before the Full
Court), refers to part of the allegations against
him as the outcome of a friendly chat at a social
evening, and not intended.for the general public.

The billeting of the Imperial soldiers on
the worthy burghers of Dunedin (M.L.) was pro-
ductive of some queer results. One Tommy (a
Life Guardsman) was secured by one of Dunedin’s
“unco’ guid,” and spent a most exciting Saturday
evening—tea, prayers, and bed at 8.30. Up in the
morning, more prayers, breakfast, and church.
Dinner, an afternoon at home with the family ;
then tea, church, again prayers, and bed. When
what was left'of the warrior got-away on Monday
it became drunk three times ! Another swaddie (a
Seaforth Highlander) arrived at his temporary
home very late and very slightly sober, and pro-
ceeded to knock things about a bit. The owner
of the things remonstrated, and received a bang
on the jaw that completely “outed” him. The
wife sailed-in to the rescue, and got a box on the
ear that put her out of action, and eventually it
became necessary to send for a picket to eject the
invader. After severe damage to the picket, the
“man in possession ” was removed from the ruins.
One of the Line-men (Cockney to the marrow), in
answer to a question from the writer as to how he
had fared, waxed enthusiastic. “All right !” he
said ; “ why, the bloke took me ’ome and put me
on a spring-mattrass—swrmy-mattrass, mind ver
—and, in the mornin’, they woke me up wiv tea.
Tea at Bin the mornin’ ! Gawd !! 1 ” and his feel-
ings got beyond expression. This same Tommy
paid a large tribute to MaoriJand’s prosperity.
“ Wot gets me dahn, ” said he, “ there don’t seem
to be no paupers—everybody ” (only he didn’t say
“ body ”) “ yer meet ’as got suv’rins.” During the
conversation, a Highlander, who had been in the
Magersfontein disaster, was giving a highly-
colored account of the affair, so we stopped our
chat to listen. Parts of the narrative were so
strong that I asked my warrior if the“ Scottie”
had really been in the scrap. “ Oh, yuz,” he
answered; “’e was there all right. Cawn’t yer
see ’is fice ain't lorst the frightened look yet ? ”
A draftsman, sued at Prahran (Vic.), re-
cently, for failing to support his illegitimate
child, objected to the girl (with whom he had
lived for two years) on the curious ground that
she made mistakes in her grammar, and left her
on that account. He wrote to her : “ You say
‘ I couldn’t have stayed half so long if I hadn’t
of had him.’ Tou should have said, ‘lf I had
not have had him.” The Bench declined to allow
defendant to score on this ground, and ordered
him to pay 20s. a week.
A certain parson having been sarcastic
about the fairy tale of Jonah and the whale at a
Melb. Wesleyan Conference meeting, has been
moved by the resulting scandal to announce his
firm belief in the truth of the story, declaring that
the Lord, if he choose, could lodge Jonah for three
days in the belly of a whale. After all, it was not
so astonishing for the whale to swallow Jonah, as
it is to find an educated man in the 20th century
capable of swallowing both Jonah and the whale.

Titanic Parson Edgar, of Melb. Wesley
Church, broke off short in his sermon recently to
mop himself and announce that, as the day was so
very hot, worshippers might take off anything in
reason. Some elderly males promptly shed their
coats, but the bulk of the congregation was
shocked and hurt deep down, and the ladies
especially sniffed haughtily. The idea was a
failure, a severe black coat being absolutely neces-
sary to a reverential demeanor in church. Writer
recalls a quarrel amongst the elders in the little
wooden “corner of the Lord’s vineyard” at
Waddy. Brother Spendwho hi 1 arrived late
in hissecond-best, confronted half-a-dozen babbling
opponents, red with righteous wrath, but quite
impotent as an orator. “ Ah, ah, thoo be mighty
eliquint, you ! ” he cried ; “ but thoo knows thoo
wouldna dare to face me if I had my frock coat
on ! ”
* * * *
The exceptionally clear complexion and
wholesome appearance of Chief-Justice Madden
would seem to indicate a serious cause for liis re-
cent ill-health, and if The Bulletin were medical
adviser to his estimable Waxellenoy he would be
shipped to Europe or Maoriland without delay.
Men of the Madden kind don’t make small ail-
ments for themselves, like the ordinary whisky-
drinking, heavy-feeding sinner who takes life as it
comes. When the dapper, active Chief-Justice
gets suddenly indisposed, and remains indisposed
for some days, it is time for him to have a long
rest from judicial duties and viee-regal vanities ;
therefore this journal shakes a warning forefinger
at the C.J., and imolores him to flee from the
York to come. If H.E. insists on waiting to be
introduced to the Heir Apparent arid all that sort
of thing—very well, then, The Bulletin will not
be responsible for the consequences.
* * * *
The law went to vast trouble to small pur-
pose in the case of that Castlemaine (Yic.) girl
who registered a birth which, so far as can be dis-
covered, never took place. Judge Holroyd, in
sending her to gaol for three months (in addition
to the three she has already spent there since
committal) remarked that “ if she had enabled the
police to find the child things would have been
much better.” Doubtless, but as the girl appears
merely to have registered a fictitious infant from
a disinterested desire to maintain the birth-rate,
or just to keep herself up-to.date, as it were, his
Honor’s observation seems a bit superfluous.
* * * *
An aged Richmond (Melb.) citizen, whose
wife keeps a shop in that suburb, wandered down
to St. Kilda lately, and being a stranger in those
pirts, and also afflicted with a severe impediment
in his speech and a trifling ditto in his intellect,
was promptly run-in as a vagrant and sentenced
to 14 days—almost before liis wife had missed
him. It was not till four days later that she dis-
covered his whereabouts, and then 48 hours
elapsed before she was allowed to see him. After
enlisting the aid of M.L. A. Bennett and besieg-
ing the Solicitor-General vigorously, the latter
promised the woman her husband’s immediate
release ; but the Law wasn’t going to be bustled,
and tied the matter up with red tape so carefully
that the old chap ultimately served the full term.
The Law’s gradual way of letting the inoffensive
old gentleman out of gaol contrasts charmingly
with its tremendous hurry in getting him in.

A curious yarn was told to the Melb.
police by a man who wandered into the Detective
Office about 3 a.m. lately. He was an importer’s
traveller, and was going home with a bag contain-
ing £175 worth of jewellery, when he became sud-
denly tired, sat down on the footpath, in Exhibi-
tion-street (of all places), and went to sleep.
When he awoke he was astonished to find the bag
gone. The ’tecs told him that the only surprising
feature about the affair was that his hat and boots
hadn’t gone too !
A curious collection of casual persons
asked for divorce last week in Sydney, and told
His Honor the simple tale of how they happened
to get married with no very fixed purpose, so to
speak. A rather sere and yellow German said he
wandered into the holy estate of matrimony after-
a week’s acquaintance. “My wife stayed with
me 19 days,” he said, “except seven days when
she was out every day.” She got £l5 from him m
19 days, and on the eighteenth day, which was
also her seventh day of being out, she smelt
strongly of drink. Then the habit of being out grew
upon her so much that she wasn’t- in for four years,
except once when she sued for maintenance, and
even then she was out. Another man told how,
3Js years ago, he married a girl of 15. They
lived together for a month, and then she also
acquired the habit of being out. They only met
once after that—in a doorway—on which occasion
she burst into tears and said, “ Wait till I go and
put my clothes on.” What she had on at the
time was not described in detail, but while she
was putting something more on, her hus-
band also got into the habit of going away, and
they never met again. Presumably, the Wife
(then aged 16|) came down with ’em all on, and
looked up and down the darkening street, and
found that the habit of being out had spread in
the family. A sea-captain explained that he had
been married 11 years. His trouble was not
drink, or the lodger, or the casual habit of being
out. Six years ago, lie said, his wife began
to back the wrong horse, and thereafter she
spent all her time at race-meetings. More than
50 times he took her wedding-ring out of pawn
where it had gone to raise money with which to
bet on the dead bird that proved to be alive, and
the straight griffin which was curly. At last she
ran so far after some horse or other that she never
found her way back, and is now pursuing the
straight griffin in parts unknown. A fourth man
had a shorter and simpler tale to tell. He was
simply a eontingenter whose wife went off with
the lodger while he was away burning down the
Boer woman’s house. A whole lot of contin-
genters found, when they returned, that their
wives had gone away with the lodger, and when
they have finished collecting their back pay from
a very tough Goverument a good many of them
propose to do something about the matter.

It used to he said that “ the
man who is his own lawyer has a

recent Sydney exposures, the
man is a fool nowadays who

A Melbourne J.P. who the other day
fined a ruffian £10 —in default three months’ im-
prisonment—for having bitten his wife’s face, and
walloped a lot of her into a jelly with a chair—
inflicted exactly the same penalty on a lad of 15
who had used “ langwidge ” and broken a window.
And yet these awful gratis-beaks were insulted
because Turner wouldn’t trust them with the
administration of the Old Age Pension Act!
At Gatton (Q.) College of Agriculture all
the arrangements for the student-boarders are
attended to by men. The Govt, probably thinks
that women on the premises might lead to another
Gatton tragedy and some more strange stories of
police tactics.
The haughty dam.es who run Melb.
Women’s Hospital—when recently requested by
the committee of the Victoria Hospital for
Women to reconsider their intention to name a
new ward the “ Victoria*” as it would clash with
the name of the other institution —loftily informed
the “upstart crowd” that they reserved the right
to give a ward any name they liked. So there
now!

There are no Nos. 13 used on the White
Star liners—a fact said to be due to the loss of
on© of the eo/s vessels on the 13th of the month on
her 13th trip. Some of the larger cabins contain 24
hunks, but owing to the omission of the 13 the
numbers run up to 25.
Science has declared that the small toe of
the human foot is bound to disappear in time, its
tendency to curl up and grow
inert being indicative of its
speedy absorption into the rest
of the anatomy. Writer was
informed by a Sydney boot-
maker as solemn fact, however,
that when the long, narrow
shoes came in lately, at least
one fashionble lady anticipated
the progress of evolution by
amputating the small toe so
that she might compress the
foot into the narrowest pos-
sible dimensions. Now that
broad shoes are in once more,
that dame is in a quandary as
to how to fill up the vacant
spaces in her shoe-leather.
* * *
Business-trips inland are
now arranged for on behalf of
Cairns Jap. ladies. An ad-
vance-agent goes through from
Cairns to Normanton via
Georgetown and Croydon, not
forgetting pastoral holdings en
route. Orders taken and dates
arranged for. Then the caval-
cade of equestriennes proceeds
along the allotted route , duly
fulfilling engagements ; when the
trip is over the horses are sold,
and the troupe, with side-
saddles, etc., is shipped back
from Norm an ton to Cairns,
picking up stray crumbs
en route. Sound commercial
principles !
ROUGE ET NOIR.
With ruddy lips and cheeks aglow
The widow flaunts her garb of woe.
Upon the black her money tosses,
Determined to recoup her losses.
S.S.
Mr. Mafchieson, the retiring Chief Com-
missioner of Yic. Railways, is surely one of the
luckiest of men. The circumstances under which
he took the billet made it fairly easy for him to
keep his natural enemies, the politicians, at bay.
Tiie failure of predecessor Speight provided him
with an excellent foundation for success as an
administrator. The name he had made in Queens-
land gained vast importance in Victoria, and when
he took it for a business-pleasure trip to England
and America, the astute Commissioner was seem-
ingly working a line for further self-advancement.
The result of that recent six months’ absence on
full salary is Mathieson’s appointment to the
chief command of a big English railway—the Mid-
land. He will say good-bye to Victoria early in
May, leaving a subordinate officer to look after
the traffic during the visit of the Dook o’ York—if
the Duke of York does pay Australia a visit—and
ride upon the royal engine of the royal train when
H.R.H. journeys to and from Ballarat or else-
where. Mathieson will not even stay to run the
risk of being duly hanged by a hysterical Govern-
ment in the event of anything going wrong with
the Dook whilst the Railway Dept, has charge of
him.
* • * *
Be the manner in which the evidence in
the case of Mrs. Smith, the woman under arrest
in connection with the death of Patrick Conway
on board s.s. Talune, from M.L. to Sydney, was
anticipated by Sydney D.T. : Isn’t it about time
that a law was enacted specifically prohibiting
such newspaper doings ? The woman’s case was
monstrously prejudiced before even the inquest
opened—in the minds of thousands she was already
convicted. If the State chaperoned innocence as
ardently as it pursues crime, its accredited officials
would, without the spur of application, put self-
appointed newspaper juries in their due place.

The second trial of the Sydney libel-action,
Limon v. the Evening News (the paper having
been misled into printing an announcement of
plaintiff’s supposed marriage, he being already

damages. Seeing, however, that plaintiff has had
to pay his own costs in the former trial, it is ques-

Memo, for travelling Australians. At
San Francisco lately the authorities refused to
allow a man from British Columbia to land, on the
ground that he was suffering from a contagious
disease —consumption to wit.
* * * *
A small child in Balmain recently swal-
lowed a penny. She was hurriedly taken to the
local cottage hospital in a state of collapse from
fright. No trace of the coin could be found, and
after a week had passed the hospital people grew
anxious, though the girl seemed all right. A
penny was too dangerous to leave alone, so she
was sent over to Sydney Hospital to undergo a
Rontgen-ray examination. The X ray easily
located the missing coin in her throat, where it
had stuck in some extraordinary fashion. An
operation soon recovered the “brown,” and the
child seems little the worse for her experience.

The chief mechanical engineer of N.S.W.
railways gave some interesting evidence at the
Sydenham accident inquiry. He said there were
in N.S.W. 18 engines of the type of the one
attached to the wrecked train. They were
“badly proportioned.” In the wrecked engine
“the centre of gravity was not good, and even if
it was moved forward the engine would never be
easy on the road.” He regarded it as safe at 30
miles an hour, but “if I saw the time-table sug-
gested a'.speed of more than 40 miles an hour, I
would object to it with that class of engine.”
Tliere had been eight cases when engines of this
type went off the line, but No. 363, which made
the Sydenham disaster, seems to have been
specially bad, or especially disastrous, having
four out of the eight accidents to its own
separate credit. Engineer Thow pointed out
that the 17 engines of this type had done
among them about five million miles, which was
at the rate of 643,000 miles for each accident, and
he urged that, with such a record, they could
hardly be called dangerous. This is reasonable
enough as regards the class of engine, but No. 363
seems to have been a specially disastrous locomo-
tive, with faults, or ill-luck, which didn’t belong
to its type generally, and the fact that 13 engines
of the same type kept on the rails, and four more
only went off once each, doesn’t alter the fact that
its own record was not at all good. When a
man’s black horse falls on him four times it is
poor satisfaction that 13 other horses of the
same color haven’t fallen on 13 other men. No. 363
appears to be a pretty aged source of trouble, its
first accident being as far back as 1887. It would
be interesting to know if any special attention
was given to this unfortunate steam-implement,
and if the driver knew —what the engineer says
he knew —that it wasn’t regarded as safe to make
over 40 miles an hour with vehicles of its class?

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
