The old “Rhyme of the Duchess May,” by
Mrs. Browning, might be brought up to date in
accordance with May’s cable to Australia about
wnat she intends wearing for us :
Society has retraced its beautiful feet from the
mountains and is crowding back to town, shop-
pmg, shopping, with the frenzied desporation of
females who are preparing to meet their Queen-to-
be. I wish somebody would tell the Duchess that
Sydney isn t buying nothing else but black—but
that it s laying in nut-brown race-frocks, and
giddy-coloured toques, choking off its conscience
with one black gown, to be worn only when the
x orks are looking.
Society of Artists asked Women to their Smoke
JNight on Tuesday for the first time. And the first
shall be the last probably —may be, I ought to say,
as L speak without authority, merely as a scribe.
The rooms looked friendly and inviting, just the
haunt for a talker. Nice big chairs meandered
about in a careless unpremeditated nearness and
aloofness. Walls hung with pictures, electric
fans, and two distinct supper rooms, one with and
one without music, and unusual heaps of men.
And do you know, in spite of all this, one brainy
lady was observed making towards the lift about
eight-ten. “I’m going home,” said she, “I can’t
stand this. A lady artist endeavoured to dis-
cover the cause of her excitement, and at last
extracted a reason the strangest, funniest
queerest, topsy turvied reason anyone ever gave
for going away from a smoke-night. “ I’m going
home because they’re smoking ! ” “ But the card
says a Smoke Night.” “ But they’ve no right to
smoke ‘ But you must have known there would
be smoking.’ “But I didn’t expect it—anyway,
iMi jP tbink they would smoke till after supper
till after* we had gone.” She wasn’t angry exactly,
but she was grieved, very grieved, and she wanted
badly to go right home.
The lady-artist, however, talked down the lady-
writer y es > }t’s true, though I confess it sounds
unlike anything but fiction of the cheapest kind—
and persuaded her that her ways were error and
that men usually smoke on a Smoke Night,’ and
that this was no deep-laid plot to outrage feminine
susceptibilities, but only a commonplace desire to
see the air turn to a blue-grey haze. To cut a
long story short, a compromise was hit upon The
injured party decided to stay, but went back to
the dressing-room first, and put on her hat. She
then came equably back to the rooms and mingled
with the crowd, effectually protected against the
whiffs of nicotine and apparent lack of chivalry
and burst of masculine selfishness by a large
black hat on her previously bare head. Strange—
and true—are the ways of women, at Smoke
Concerts.
How to dress for Smoke brought out various
explanations and apologies. Miss Thea Proctor
looked tall and calm and artistic in a black even-
mg gown, with her long arms bare. Miss Haidee
de Bissa was demi-toiletted. Mrs. Pleming, the
clever little amateur actress, was in black, with
her dark hair tied up with cherry ribbons. George
JLambert’s charming mother was there to contra-
dict that touch of hardness in her son’s portrait of
her, hanging on the wall.
A certain white-edged politician stirred up youth-
ful recollections of Daniel in the lion’s den. You
should have seen him leaning up against a pallid
intensity of a picture, all sand and stunted trees,
and casting a long-lashed eye around in search of
someone who could listen. Ever since a certain
dinner Art has had a deep affinity for politics.
Miss Alice Muskett and Mrs. Pauli wore muslin
walking-dresses. A plump musical man looked
gloomy in black because he had been likened to a
large snowdrop when he last wore his favorite
white suit, and because he was managing the
musical programme. Said programme included,
by the way, a song from a brother of Henri
Brownlow, a large, fair man ridiculously like
Wallace, except in the voice and way of singing
and oh, my brethren, what a fall was there !
I hear that a very popular operatic singer leaves
her co. and returns to England shortly because
her manager has objected to raising her terms to
the extent she demanded.
At a little tea given by Madame Darto last
week at the Australia, Nellie Stewart’s small
daughter Nancy was much in evidence. Nancy
is as unlike her pretty mamma as possible—a red-
haired, thin-faced child with freckles, but gifted
with a delicate, sweet voice and charming expres-
sion. Along with Monsieur Darto she sang and
danced in a childish way, “ When we are married
what will we do,” acting like a born little artiste.
She gabbles her French as easily as English, and
convulsed the genial Joe Tapley—going bald,
alas ! with an account of how she tried to make
him laugh from her box the night before when her
mother wasn’t looking.
Mr. Hawtrey’s managerial venture has turned
out a prominent success, and the number of pretty
Sydney girls walking the Palace boards nightly in
u J e £? all t frocks and hats give point to a new
theory that local talent need not always be ’Ome-
branded before its returns in the box office amount
to something profitable.
Out of the Hawtrey 00. there is one actress
from London—Miss Ruby Ray—to ten from
Sydney. The acting of these ladies is not a bit
brilliant, you understand, but they wear their
dresses very nicely (these gowns, by the way, are
an inspiration of a well-known Sydney pen woman,
who exercised her aesthetic artistic soul in
choosing hats and frocks to meet Hawtrey’s excel-
lent taste) especially Misses Winnie Austen, Roxy
Barton, and Madge Hope, and are just as success-
ful as the average English peroxide, with a made-
up street skin, who talks of deah London, which
never heard of her except in the provinces. Also,
the local actress’s friends come in crowds to the —
but hold ! Perhaps that is a different story from
the one I’m making out, and in enumerating the
advantages of putting our own girls before the
footlights it might be better not to include their
sisters and their cousins and their aunts.
Certainly Hawtrey’s plays have leaned heavily
on the men who act them, rather than on the
women who dress them. Herbert Ross caught on
from the first night. By the way, I don’t know
whether “catching on” has anything to do with
it, but the little grey-eyed Londoner is an ardent
fisherman, and may be seen returning to town on
Monday morning in a dishevelled suit, a big
white Panama hat, and the light of an angler in
his histrionic eye, accompanied by the fair, smil-
ing Mrs. Ross, in a neat black dress and black
toque, as spruce as if she and hubby had been
looking into shops instead of the blue “upsplit-
ting ” sea.
Speaking of fishermen, Trilby Crane and her
husband, Tyrone Power, are housekeeping on the
quiet at Watson’s Bay. Their ’Murkan tastes
urge them to cast aside all conventionalities, and
the burly Tyrone haunts the beaches of the east
shore without any shoes and in the ordinary get-
up of a fisherman. To the weary theatrical this
sort of thing is an immense relief, and on Satur-
day afternoon at Rose Bay the petite Madame
Darto may be seen paddling along the beach while
her smiling husband saunters on the grass with
her miniature foot-gear in his hands.
Tyrone Power, by the way, stepped into an ad-
venture the other day. In his usual bare footed
way, he went into a hotel at Watson’s to
wash the salt from his larynx. A private indi-
vidual, also removing salt at the bar, cast his eye
over the fisherman, and, in the usual drinky
friendliness, ordered a wash for him. “ Here’s a
drink, fisherman,” said he. The actor drank it
on m fine dramatic spirit, respectfully answered
the gent’s questions, and told how the fish were
biting. Once an actor, always an actor. Even
when he returned the shout and called for a bottle
of gold-top, and the stranger gasped, and looked
volumes of interrogation points, Tyrone went on
dramatising, and kept his fishy secret to the end
of the seaside act.
“ The Christian,” at Her Majesty’s, shows the
large, slow Edith Crane wisely not attempting to
follow the small, lithe Hetty Watson’s methods
too closely, and the “famous apostrophe,” as a
daily paper calls it, is whispered rather than
shrieked. Whether Miss Crane’s Glory is as near
Hall Caine’s Glory as was Henrietta Watson’s the
fair Edith scores more heavily as far as looks go.
She looks the most charming creature in extremely
abbreviated skirts which show so much tan
leg that I wonder whether it was Hetty’s
old dress she was wearing or whether it
was just realism to go with her remark that John
would find everything the same after all these
years—Glory in the frock that used to fit her then
among other things.
By the bye, Miss Watson (Mrs. Radcliffe) has
lately written to her friends in Sydney to an-
nounce the advent of a baby—little Hetty—but
rumours of her returning shortly to Australian
footlights continue to circulate.
Poor “ Woody ”—or otherwise Mr. F. R. Wood-
house, the well-known, fair-haired tenor—has been
having a bad time lately. The breaking of a
blood-vessel m his lung, followed by a severe ill-
ness, may prevent him singing in public again,
and his doctors order a long sea-voyage. As
everyone who knows anything knows “Woody”
of the fluty voice, the Profesh. Musicians have done
well to call a meeting to consider how to best re-
cognise his services for Art and Charity. The
meeting is timed for Thursday, at 4.30, Vestibule
of Town Hall, P.B.P.— otherwise, Please be
present; and bring any friends who would like to
help with you. Mayor Graham, Rivers Allpress
and R. Kohlblanch are running the show so far.
Poor young “Sep.” Harte’s death last week was
a sorrowful affair. The bright, boyish organist
was bathing with his friend, Sydney Hoben, when
he struck his head diving in too-shallow water, and
fatally injured his spine, dying from paralysis,
after great suffering, four days later. He was just
20 years of age, and showed fine promise. At 18
he was made organist at St. Francis Church, and
two years later he took Auguste Wiegand’s post
as organist of St. Patrick’s. Sydney Hoben has
now been appointed his successor at the latter
church.
At Colonel and Mrs. Paul’s “at home” on
Saturday night the Artists’ rooms were gay with
red coats and military uniforms. The fair-haired
Mrs. Paul received, in a charming gown of palest
mauve silk, with a big shower bouquet of yellow
roses. Somehow, one meets the same people over
and over again at all these art parties, and it was
quite an excitement to see among others a well-
known danseuse and her hubby, the lady
exquisitely froeked in filmiest white voile
over white glace silk, swathed round her
lithe form in serpentine fashion with rows of
heavy white silken fringe. With this the
renchy little figure wore a pink, Paris hat, and
an opera mantle of subtlest rose-pink brocaded
panne, lined with a low-tinted green. Another
wrap this lady always arouses envy with, is a long
theatre jacket of leaf-green panne lined with dull
blue satin, the- long sleeves puffed out at the
wrists with little blue-satin puffs.

tures without going home to think it over. This
was our new Admiral, who seems a different breed
from the usual naval dignitary, with his eye full
of water and precedence;
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Toorak gasped last Wednesday (6th) at the
sumptuousness of the knot-tying that made as
one a wealthy pair—Miss Gertie England and the
stalwart Mr. Jack Wallace (of Wallace will fame).
The local Church of England was the chosen
tabernacle. The young bride, who made her
debut two years ago, is tall, very fair, very dark-
eyed, and seemingly very indifferent' to a big
share of good looks. It was the bride’s gorgeous
gown that made tired Toorak bustle into an active
fire of admiration. A satin foundation heaped up
with chiffon found eminence as distinct as a
steeple or a tower, in mountings of priceless
Venetian lace. ‘ Regal ! ’ gasped Toorak, and
went forth from the ceremony to bray the news
abroad over Melbourne.
“ Eour bridesmaids waited on the stunning
beauty—in white silk muslin garnished with egg-
yolk yellow, the neck and sleeves of the top-storey
transparent. Picture hats repeated the nice
blend, and streaming posies added to the golden
look of things. Signor Rebottaro sang the two
wedding hymns —the groom having arranged this
innovation as a surprise for the bride. His gift
to her, by the way, was a superb diamond tiara.
Mrs. England’s house, on St. Kilda-road, flew a
hundred flags, and held high festival generally
after the wedding.
“The fortune that young John Wallace shares
reminds me that two capital old Irishmen —the
late Managhan, of property fame, and the late
Pat O’Brien, of Kinkora repute —have left a host
of grandchildren in very affluent circumstances.
Both men built up colossal fortunes carefully in
the days of young Victoria, and their grand-
children are reaping the harvest now. The ele-
gant widow of the ‘ Kmkora King ’ is in London,
having as good a time as her sense of decorum
will permit. Rumor always had it that Mrs.
O’Brien and the late Sir Anthony Brownless were
on the point of marriage. This persistent lie
greatly interfered with the platonics of the pair—
but the lady has survived it.
“Word has just come that another of the
■ wealthy Wallaces —the stalwart Romeo of the
family—was married in London last week, on the
same day as his brother.
“Col. Tom Price took on a paternal air last
Tuesday, and trotted chubby daughter Loma all
over the bird-cage. Said Lorna compromised
with the god of Fashion by striking out in grey.
Mrs. Walter Coldham, just out of bona-fide
mourning, showed forth in an attractive lavender.
Another bright splash of color was supplied by
Miss Irene Miller in a heliotrope scrolled blouse
and a black skirt.
“ The crowd at Flemington on Saturday was a
hot and interesting one, and illustrative of several
social resurrections. Mrs. Everard Browne, lost
to sight for nearly two years, bloomed forth at
this meeting, large, matronly and handsomely-
groomed. In fact, her purple turn-out, with its
hyphens of black and white lace, was the most
remarkable on the terrace. Miss Muriel Wil-
liams, broad of hip and double-chinned in the
fashion of' all leading beauties, showed up after
her globe-trotting, in white silk and a pert gold
and black striped toque, a bold black bow
perching in front. Lady Clarke, the brightest
of all the bees on the terrace, shimmered in
white silk with a butter-lace zouave and bands on
the skirt. A rustic, inopportune, mushroom hat
of black-and-white straw flaunted two aggressive
bows atop. All the fashion of Saturday’s meeting
seemed to be concentrated in the millinery. The
old familiar picture-hats were there in force, so
were the tilted toques (as worn by the Madden
girls in conjunction with black frocks, white bibs
and tuckers), mushroom hats, simple coarse straws,
and the new double-rimmed fly-aways, uniquely
covered like verandah-ledges in tulle and spread-
ing black foliage. The latter kind were worn by
two remarkable fashion-plates—Mrs. Claude Mac-
donald and Mrs. Phillip Russell.
“ A South Yarra honeymoon couple appeared
at all the races last week in brand-new grey out-
fits, and black arm-bands. So did they allay their
ecstacy.
“Commissioner Mathieson, with his chest as
tight in its coat as a gooseberry in its skin, and his
beard a-bristle, had a final strut and cigar on the
Terrace last Saturday, and smilingly allowed con-
gratulations on his big English billet.
“ All Sassiety has taken Grace Palotta to its
heart. The Maddens, the Clarkes, the Millers,
surrounded her in turn on Saturday, and ab-
sorbed her playful Holyrood tricks delightedly.
The scion of the Madden family played the de-
voted aide-de-camp, when other attendants yielded
a place at her elbow. Miss Palotta’s manner is alive
with stage-charms; hereyesroll, twinkle, flash, turn
up, hide behind a dark fringe of lashes, and play
perfect Tarantelles on her companions, while her
hands keep time with her talk. Two more viva-
cious women than Lady Clarke and Grace Palotta
could not be found in all the tiers of Sassiety.
“An interesting group at one time of the after-
noon was made up of S’Rupert and Lady Clarke,
Mrs. Lawson and Grace Palotta. It soon broke
up, the long-absent one darting away to speak to
three of her fellow-passengers. The other two
frisky frockers (who are sworn friends) came to-
gether. Grace wore a commonplace black and
white foulard, and a hat strewn with wistaria,
very much ‘ Florodora.’ Mrs. Lawson brought
back to knowledge her white flouncey ‘ Cup ’
toilette, with which went a black toque.
“ ‘ Florodora ’ still goes strong. Its only weak-
ness is that everyone’s trying to be funny, and no
one’s content with looking pretty and sweet.
Grace Palotta’s Lady Holyrood grows quite a
loud, gagging female who knocks off men’s hats,
&c. Surely the Palotta doesn’t mean to part
with her trump-cards—stage refinement and grace.
As for Carrie Moore, bristling with energy and
go, her Dolores is not the girl to crack the hearts
of all the men subjugated by her in the book. A
Manilan belle would be passionate and fitful, per-
haps, but Carrie makes her comic certainly—
otherwise a strident, hard-faced vixen who doesn’t
attempt to look attractive, and habitually hen-
pecks * followers.’ Formerly the charming little
Moore lacked pepper. Now she veers to the
other extreme. A pretty girl in a comic-operatic
temper should look as spiteful as an angry kitten
—not as scowlingly melodramatic as Porthos in
the ‘ Three Musketeers.’
“Irene Franklin is a welcome relief these
Florodorean times. Her arms are so round and
white, and though her figure looks mature enough
to be the mother of its advertised age, still there’s
something quiet and girlish in ‘the Dainty’s’
demeanour, and her voice, nasal though it be, is
pleasingly soft and gentle. The Palotta ankle-
peep methods seem over-pronounced in compari-
son with Irene’s, and where on earth did Lady
Holy rood get that loud, harsh laugh ?
“That wicked man, Marshall-Hall, is making
use of his free leg now that Prof. Peterson, the
new, convention-fearing music-maker of Ormond,
has landed in Melb. The other evening the
author of Hymns and Hers, Ancient and Modern,
delivered a rash, fervid lecture on ‘ Joy ’ to a push
of absorbed disciples of both sexes, and preached
a lengthy sermon on the wisdom of chasing
pleasure as she flies, and grabbing her with both
hands. The * very best ’ have recognised this ser-
mon as desperately heathenish, and there is evi-
dence in respectable circles of the profound regret
of the Mrs. Grundies in skirts, trousers, and
Bishops’ breeches that there remain no more
effective bricks to aim at the head of the solemn
scoffer. Meanwhile Hall’s conservatorium is
going well, and will probably rake in every musi-
cal student with a ha’porth of artistic sense, leav-
ing to the clergy-ridden institution of Ormond all
those who desire to play a little and sing a little as
part of the equipment of the domestic drawing-
room.
“Franklin Peterson, the new Professor of
Music for Melb. University, made a favorable
first impression by announcing himself as a
believer in many sorts of classical works, includ-
ing ‘ The Mikado.’ Questioned as to his Con-
servatorium plans, with direct reference tothe Mar-
shall-Hall party, Mr. Peterson said that, if Mel-
bourne was only half as musical as it considered
itself, there was absolute need for more than one
Conservatorium in the city. Berlin supports a
dozen Conservatoriums ; why shouldn’t a city of
400,000 people supply pupils enough for two ?
“A Melb. weekly affirms that sculptor Mac-
kennal has been offered for his Circe by the trus-
tees of the National Gallery ‘four figures or over.’
Well, over four figures would be five—£lo,ooo at
least; though that is not what is meant. But
Mackennal has not been offered anything for the
statue. The trustees have no money, and would
have to get a special grant from the Government
if they wanted to buy it.
“ Just deceased —the wife of John Thofhas
Smith. This was the remarkable woman who
consented to keep her marriage a secret for nearly
20 years owing to a bit of prejudice (wholly with-
out foundation) on the part of J.T.S.’s mother.
On the death of that wealthy old lady, the
marriage was made known and the notice cropped
out in the papers. (The lady was a Miss Stubbs,
daughter of the old-time Melb. auctioneer.) The
pair then took up their abode at ‘Ascot,’ on
Flemington-road, and added to the rich furnish-
ings some choice bronzes and bric-a-brac. At a
sale there a few years ago all Society assembled to
secure some of the treasures. The couple lived
very quietly at ‘ Ascot,’ and afterwards at St.
Hilda.
“Interest in the Coningham case has caught
England by the ears. Two Englishmen, who
travelled out by a German boat recently, had
written to a prominent Sydney solicitor asking
for reserved seats at the second' trial. They con-
fessed to fellow-passengers, on the way out, that
they were actuated by two ambitions in coming to
Australia—to hear the Coningham case and see
the Federal Parliament launched.

“Everybody still talks and scribbles re the
servant question. A local washerwoman com-
plains that her nine-year-old daughter, inflated by
consciousness of a sought-after future, has been
neither to have nor to hold ‘ since all the girls
went to Sydney for the Federation and boom
wages and can’t afford to come back again.’ In
answer to motherly reproof the thankless child
chirps 1 I’ll take a situation and go on a merry-go-
round every night, see if 1 don’t.’ Sure enough
she has found an employer glad even of her
skimpish help. ‘Missis’ in this case is a
‘plugger’ who works like a Chow behind the
scenes but faces the footlights as a lady of leisure.
This Spartan cooks and ‘dishes up,’ and then sits
in full war-paint entertaining the guests while the
hired atom, waiting at table, trots in and out with
only her face and boots showing from behind big,
smoking dishes and highly-decorative covers.
“It’s quite archaic to hear the old fashioned
mistress of another very small suburban villa say
to her supercilious help, ‘ Look out, Maud, and
see if your master is coming.’ Poor ‘Master’!
He looks such a meek little old hack, and his
alleged vassal the most skittish of high-steppers.
Yours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULI.
The courtly and kind-hearted ex-N.S.W.
District Court Judge David Grant Forbes, who,
a few years ago, retired on his pension of £750 a
year, died suddenly, at Sydney, last week, aged
82. He was a man of most charming manners ;to
the very last he retained the spirit of a
boy, and was a universal favorite with young
and old. Though called to the Bar in early
life, he spent several years after his return
to N.S.W. farming and squatting. His loyalty
was pronounced—as if to compensate for the
rather heterodox sentiments of his father, the first
N.S.W. Chief Justice. The Chief Justice was a
native of the West Indies, and carried with him
through life a strong admiration for American
Republicanism. His tomb in Belmore Cemetery
was opened the other day for the necessary trans-
fer of the remains.
N.S. Wales collects, under alleged Free-
trade, 19s. 10d. Customs duties per head of popu-
lation ; Victoria’s frightful annual burden amounts
to £ll2s. 9d. If Victoria applied as much loan-
money to revenue purposes as N.S.W. does,
however, its Customs burden could be re-
duced to £1 4s. lOd. per inhabitant annually.
As a further offset to this crushing ad-
ditional impost of 12s. lid., the Victorian, accord-
ing to Coghlan, enjoys slightly higher wages and
cheaper living, and the advantage of absolutely
free education for his children. On this item
alone, the Vic. breadwinner with two children
saves £1 Is. per year, allowing for holidays. Send
along the Juggernaut of Protection !

Let your chapeaux and your trains
And your mouchoirs and your brains,
And your little satin sack,
All be bound in black.
Ladies, ladies everywheah,
I shall be in black.
En princesse, brocaded gauze,
Long train finished for applause,
With designs of leaves and flowers,
Yes, but all in black.
Over this I mean to weah
Such a stunning cloak 1
Black Chantilly, rarest rare,
Draping such a velvet snare,
Long and elegant and black,
Smart but sad and very black.
Then there will be other gowns
All supremely black,
Elegant as elegant,
Worth, or Felix, or Lissant,—
Trimmed and moulded—oh, but black,
Lovely, killing, mournful black.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Ernest Favenc, who has long been ill, has
gone for a trip to the South Seas.
A. B. Murray, head of the many-branched
family of S.A. sheep-breeders, is still alive, though
little heard of now, aged 87.
The two surviving ex-Judges of N.S.W.
District Court ruled the Bench—Dowling for 38
years and Wilkinson for 33.
If we have the Plague in earnest, we shan’t
have the Duke of York. Now which is it
better for us to have —the Duke or the other
Plague ?
The first man elected to the first Federal
Parliament of Australia was John Forrest, re-
turned unopposed for the Swan division of Wes-
tralia last week.
N.S.W. Premier Bill Lyne should be
known as Treasury Bill. Having floated three
millions’ worth of them in about a year he has
well-earned the name.
The long arm, again ! The re-hearing of
the Coningham divorce case was fixed for March
11th, which date is the anniversary of the marriage
sought to be dissolved.
South Australia and N.S.W. have each
had five Chief Justices. S.A. : Jeffcott. Fielding,
Cooper, Hanson and Way. N.S.W. : Forbes,
Dowling, Stephen, Martin and Darley.
Judges on N.S.W. pension-list : W. J.
Foster, ex-Judge of the Supreme Court, £lB2O
per annum ; ex-Judges Dowling and Wilkinson,
of the District Court, £750 per annum each.
W. J. Ferguson, selected as Protectionist
candidate for Bathurst (Macquarie), began his
acquaintance with that town as a prisoner—-it
was to the gaol there that he was committed for
his share in the Broken Hill miners’ strike of ’92.
The man who had A. W. Ware tossed out
of the Adelaide Mayoralty because he described
himself as “ gentleman ” instead of “ brewer,”
nominated against Ware at the second election.
And that time he himself forgot to fill in the date !
What looks like a very cheeky attempt to
creep in under pa’s great-coat is being made by a
quite unknown S. Australian youngster. While
the well-known and able R. Chaffey Baker runs
for the Senate, this unknown, H. Chaffey Baker,
runs for the House of Representatives.
Sister Joseph, one of the Band of Mercy
Nuns who nursed the British wounded in the Cri-
mean War, died last week in the Convent in Bris-
bane. Florence Nightingale, Sister Joseph, and
a couple of other surviving nurses were sent tokens
from the late Queen Victoria a few years ago.
W. T. Ferguson, M.L.A, who is running
as Bartonite candidate for Macquarie, has an
epitaph for G. H. Reid’s political tombstone :
Hated by some.
Admired by others.
Mistrusted by all.
At the last moment, S’langdon Bonython,
of the S.A. Advertiser, decided to run for the
Representatives instead of for the Senate. He
didn’t think the electors would stand four knights
for one House ; and Downer, Baker, and Symon
were almost certainties foy the Senate, while there
wasn’t a single man with a handle announced for
the other Chamber.

The recently-deceased Mrs. Anna Maria
Henty (Vic.), relict of the late Edward Henty
accompanied her husband to Tasmania in 31 and
to Portland (Vic.) in ’34, when he became the
founder of the future province, preceding Hat-

18 months later.
Robert Reid, the Melbourne importer .who
wants to get into the Senate, sadly informed a
Maryborough meeting that lie was the last T ree-
trade candidate left in Victoria. Reid s claim
seems to rest mainly on the fact that it he is
returned he will be available as a curiosity a sor
of Last Man, so to speak.
According to a M.L. paper, Judge Martin
has written to a friend complaining of the way m
which Maorilanders generally have treated him
since his elopement, but adding that “he has
made his bed and must lie on it.” The paper
adds, rather cruelly, that he didn’t make his bed,
but annexed somebody else’s.
When J. G. Ward, as acting-Premier of
M.L., was about to proclaim that the King had
come, he donned a Windsor uniform to do it in,
but put on white knee-pants instead of the regula-
tion mourning black ones. The effect produced
was something like that of a fat woman going to a
funeral in a low-necked dress.
The political star of Charles Hardie Buza-
cott has apparently set for ever. Buzacott
aspired to Federal honors, but out of 40 Conven-
tion League delegates recently assembled in
Rockhampton he failed to secure a single sup-
porter. Rampant Jingoism isn’t always the safe
card some people suppose it.
Portraits of Prof. Franklin Peterson,
Marshall-Hall’s successor in the Ormond chair of
music, flutter about in Melbourne papers, and be-
tray the new Prof. as an eminently “nice” man
with a somewhat lackadaisical expression, calcu-
lated to strongly impress bishops and gentle old
ladies who work in wool.
A pathetic sight, writes a Bulletin
correspondent, is good old Tommy Dalveen ad-
dressing a meeting in favor of his candidature for
the Federal Parliament for the Canoblas.
Apparently he hasn’t the remotest idea as to what
the functions of the Federal Parliament are likely
to be. The fact of his having (together with his
partners) paid away large sums in wages to miners
is in his opinion the all-sufficient and only qualifica-
tion necessary to ensure election. Of course they
pull his leg everywhere he goes, many free drinks
being the result.
An old friend, Capt. Slocum, in his book
about his voyage round the globe alone in the
cutter Spray, mentions that he
was introduced to Kruger by
a gentleman who said “Here
is the captain who has sailed
alone round the world.”
Kruger: “You mean in the
world. The world is flat.”
An American version (to
get real English news you
must go to America) : —
The Duchess of York is not at
all pleased about the trip, for she
is a very bad sailor, and the sea is
no respecter of persons. I don’t
think H.ii.H. will impress the
good people of Australia favor-
ably. She was a very pretty
child; she grew into a rather
nice-looking girl ; but she has
developed into a plain and very
disagreeable-looking matron. I
don’t suppose she is happy, but
when she married the Duke of
York she was perfectly aware of
certain little domestic affairs of
his, and has no right now to put
on such a scowl when she faces
her husband’s future subjects.
She gives herself tremendous airs
—the airs of a parvenu, which
she really is, for, though her
mother was a granddaughter of
George III., her father was illegiti-
mate. She has no idea of the
dignity that belongs to persons
to the manner born. Her mother,
who was the quintessence of bon-
homie, never allowed you to forget
that she was a descendant of the
Georges, but she' did it with such
a grace that you thought it was
you who recognised the fact. On
the other hand, the Duchess of
York appears always to be im-
pressing you with the idea that,
God willing, she will be some day
Queen Consort of England. Now
the Australians are intensely loyal,
but they have got Socialism down
to the finest point, and I imagine
the sight of the Duchess of York
will be'a shock to their loyalty.
The Bulletin’s London correspondent :
Charley Pinhey, late N.S.W. Registrar-General, now
residing in London, received a pleasant surprise-packet
recently in the shape of a letter from N.S.W. Govt,
stating that a pension had now been assigned him. When
the Reid-Coghlan Govt, passed an Act suspending the
pension clauses of the Superannuation Act for 12 months,
Charles A. T. Pinhey was thrown out with a gratuity of
some thousand pounds. The Lyne Govt., recognising the
iniquity of the Act, decided to restore the pension rights
of the unfortunates who had receiyed gratuities in place
of the pensions due to them, and O.H.T.P. now unex-
pectedly receives a pension of some £4OO a year, together
with a substantial lump sum for arrears due after de-
ducting the amount paid him as gratuity.

, ustice Stephen told his brother Judges
the other day that he was in favor of continuing
Circuit Courts, although they did make great
holes in Judges’ time. His reason was that Cir-
cuit Courts gave judges agreeable excursions into
the country. Apropos, Judges are paid extra for
every day they are on duty outside the metropolis.
Melba’s father, David Mitchell, has dis-
pelled for the present the old, old rumour of her
“ approaching visit to Australia.” The old man
has made known something that daughter Nellie
actually did say in a recent letter—“l wish I
could manage to go to Australia. Perhaps I shall
some, day; 1 hope so.” So the old, old rumour
will get a 12 months’ rest, anyhow.
Recently-deceased Thos. Loader (Melb.
merchant) was a member of the Heales Ministry
in ’6l, when he had for a colleague J. H. Brooke,
of Occupation Licenses renown, and was on
friendly terms with Wilson Gray, the celebrated
advanced democrat of the day. There was no
payment of members in those times, so Loader
quickly retired into commerce, Brooke trekked to
Japan (where he established a newspaper, and is
still flourishing), and Gray accepted a judgeship in
M.L., where he died some years ago. In'later
days Loader was chairman of the perished City of
Melbourne Bank, bossed by Colin Longmuir—the
Man That’s Dead.
Clement Wragge, cried up to the skies some
years ago as the meteorologist par excellence, has
fallen upon evil days. Queenslanders growl at his
indefinite forecasts and say that anybody knows
that when the weather is sultry at Townsville and
the glass stands at lOOdeg. in the shade, there’ll
probably be rain. Also that any jackass could
prophesy casual storms between Cooktown and
Bowen in February, and fine, dry weather at Bar-
caldine in August. Fact is, Wragge is a victim
of the drought. What Queenslanders want is a
man who will prophesy three feet of rain over the
Bakblox from Croydon to Cunnamulla, and see
that his prophecy is verified.
“Demos”: Looking over all the London
newspapers I find a surprisingly cold-blooded
dissection of the Queen. This comes of pigeon-
holing, keeping the cagmag years and years. A
warm-blooded young journalist had to tickle up
yellow paper left by the great Noah Arkbottom,
variously embroidered by Methuselahs and Smiths.
“For God’s sake,” he says, “let me pitch aside
this mummified rubbish, and wire in.” “ N-n-no,”
says the trembling Editor,. “ If you won’t do it
properly, somebody else will.” So in goes the
patchwork quilt, a douche to the rushing enthu-
siasm of the public. Contrast this with the live
notice in the Argus, where I take it for granted
that Willoughby allowed Fitchett to take the bit
in his teeth and bolt.
George Jenkins, now scheming to get the
clerkship of the Federal Senate, would have been
Black-Wednesdayed by Graham Berry but for the
intervention of Charles Gavan Duffy. Jenkins
was then assistant clerk of Yic. Leg. Assembly.
“I went to the Government and remonstrated,”
relates Duffy in his “Autobiography.” Subse-
quently, in the House, he called attention to the
proposed sacrifice of Jenkins, and Berry
relented. It has been related in recent
years that the last words of the great
William Pitt were not the famous and
fabulous “ Ob, my country !” but “l am sorry I
have not anything for Jenkins.” Jenkins was
Pitt’s butler and told the story himself. Let
Prime Minister Barton beware that his last words
be not: “I am sorry I did nothing for Jen-
kins ! ” Apparently people who neglect Jenkins
die.
Statement by Colonel Newall, a returned
M.L. contingenter, on the alleged “murder” of
Lieut. White, ofN.S.W. (brother-in-law of Colonel
Kenneth Mackay):—
A retiring convoy was seen and chased by the Aus-
tralians. ' Unfortunately Lieut. White and an orderly
alone were able to maintain the pace, and on overtaking
the convoy, rode up and ordered the Boers to put up
their arms. There are such things as intrepidity and
braveness, also as foolhardiness and stupidity. This
action belonged to the latter kind. Two men could not
possibly take ten, and the Boer cornet did not think it
good enough to put up his hands, so he wiped the two
Australians out. I have since seen in the Graphic a
picture of the Australians, round the graves of Lieut.
White and the orderly, vowing vengeance on the ground
that they were shot while showing the flag of truce.
That was untrue. The two men rode up and got shot.
Everything that was stated about the Boers must not be
believed.
Mr. Arthur Lloyd Windsor, just-retired
editor of the Age, will visit his old friend, Gavan
Duffy, in the south of Europe. Windsor ran
the Age for all it was worth in support of
Duffy’s Ministry—now nearly 30 years ago.
“The only politician who ever atteinp-
ted to infuse sweetness and light into
the dull, confined, parochial, com-
monplace spirit of colonial states-
manship,” was how Windsor wrote
to and of Duffy. In return, Duffy
writes of Windsor’s hook of essays
now rarely procurable - that
it exhibited “ the luminous style,
the wide knowledge and govern-
ing sense which distinguished
Macaulay.” Admirable back-
scratchers !

Extraordinary Verdict.
It is supposed that as a rule verdicts given are
just and reliable, but we sometimes hear of one
that is proved to be entirely erroneous by subse-
quent events. This was the ease with the verdict
on Mr. S. E. Richards, Mining Engineer of
“ Ontario,” Nicholson Street, Burwood, N.S. W.,
who, in a letter dated Bth March, 1000, says:—
“On the Ist Sept. 1898, I became overheated,
then suddenly exposed to a piercing cold wind. I
caught a chill, the result being that I was deprived
of robust health, and for about seven months my
life was despaired of. During this time I had been
through the hands of eight medical men in private
and public hospitals. The majority of them held
out no hope, some of them declaring that I had
kidney, liver, heart, and lung troubles. In the
Prince Alfred Hospital, Sydney, my medical
attendant said I had Bright’s Disease of the
kidneys, that he could do nothing for me, and that
I had better be taken home, as I could not recover.
Rather a dark prospect this considering that, in
addition to the troubles enumerated above, I had
dropsy to a fearful extent. I had been
tapped in the lower part of the abdomen five
times, several gallons of water being drawn off,
but all to no good purpose. In a few days after
being tapped, I swelled up in my legs, abdomen,
and stomach, larger than before. The dropsy
began to make its appearance in my hands and
face. My body swelled to that degree that I
sickened to look at myself, and many times wished
I could die. Friends who visited me sympathised
and bid me, as they believed, a last farewell. But
an angel of mercy came in the shape of Warner’s
Safe Cure. A lady friend strongly recommended
me to give up hospitals, doctors and medicines,
and take only Warner’s Safe Cure. On the 3rd
April, 1899, i began to take Warner’s Safe Cure.
As soon as I began to take this medicine my
appetite began to return. At the end of four
weeks, when I had drank four bottles of Safe
Cure, the water began to come away. I reduced
in circumference around the abdomen from
inches to 41-| inches, and my legs reduced in
proportion, t felt like a new man. My spirits
returned. I began to get regular sleep, still
reducing in size until not a vestige of lung, kidney,
liver, or dropsy trouble could be seen or felt. I
am now in the best of health, and am firmly con-
vinced that Warner's Safe Cure has, in God’s hand,
been the means of restoring my lost health and
strength. I would gladly write, talk, or sing the
praises of Warner’s Safe Cure, but will conclude
by stating that I shall be only too happy to
answer any questions, either by letter or otherwise.
I feel I owe a duty to the world at large in
making known the almost miraculous cure
effected in my case by Warner’s Safe Cure. I
also feel a deep debt of gratitude to the discoverer
of Warner’s Safe Cure, and trust this humble,
unsolicited testimonial may be made public for
the benefit of suffering humanity. I am well-
known all over the Australian Colonies, and my
old mining contemporaries will rejoice to learn of
my restoration to health.”*

Aboriginalities.
“Trotter” sends a North Queensland
picture:
Halifax (N.Q.) is the true home of the alien. Except a
tew soul-weary, nigger-driving Scandinavian sugar-
ooekies, the population is composed of Italians, Syrians,
Hindoos, Ohows, Japs, Malays and Kanakas, and the
rising generation is a piebald medley. As I write two
Dagos are fighting in the billiard-room, two Syrians are
disputing in the bar, a lot of Ghows are yabbering in the
front, while a Jap. has charge of the back. Coming along
saw 20 Japs repairing the tram-line on one of the plan-
tations, and saw a Malay driving the motor. The Kanaka
drives about this district in his dog-cart accompanied by
dusky love ; and the Ohow is the boss store-keeper
An English ex-army officer runs a pub., assisted by the
brother of a well-known diplomatic official in Peking.
There s a Ohow quarter, a Jap quarter, and a Dago and
. yrian quarter, redolent of maocaroni hovels, tomato stew
opium, and the omnipresent banana. In the last-named
quarter a Malay runs a bagnio, with two white women, to
one of whom he is married. The other day a small
coastal steamer carried in the fore-cabin (12x10) S Japs, 2
'u r7 S ’ a a Syrian and a white man bound for
i.,A aaa' G le Queensland Govt, spends something like
LlO 000 a year for rations for 40,000 men to tramp this
continent from one end to the other and the work is
given to the alien). A large, grey beetle dwells here also
—the rum of the cane—and costs the cocky 6d. a quart to
extirpate. When at Halifax in the early part of January,
the temperature averaged 100 in the shade for the week
—sometimes it stood at 116. To pass a summer here is to
develop a chronic N.Q. thirst.

Writes a W.A. man to The Bulletin re
the recent shooting of J. J. Durack, of Denham
station:—
•n' W ? C J r - three years ago J. J. Durack handed a loaded
rifle to Ins black boy, and (it was claimed) instigated the
atter to shoot a native camped not far from the' station,
located on the Denham River, which flows from the west
mto the Old river, about 80 miles south of Wyndham.
The murderer was really only a mere boy, probably
scarcely 15 yeais, and belonged to the Mt. Dockrel tribe,
near Hole s Creek, the old E. Kimberley goldfield more
than 100 miles further south. At the trial in Perth he
was condemned to four years’ imprisonment on Rottnest
Island, and the indictment against Durack was quashed.
Mr. Connor, a partner of the deceased, has been returned
for the vast E. Kimberley electorate on 60 odd votes •
Doherty, another partner, sits for North Fremantle ; and
Pennefather. the Attorney-General, is of their party. It
is questionable whether .deceased enjoyed the highest re-
putation, even among the not over-sensitive population
in E.K., but he belonged to a powerful coterie, who rule
the roost in the district. From a blackfellow’s standpoint
Durack got his deserts, and if he had been wise he would
lave kept away from the place after the shooting of the
larkey. He must have been sufficiently acquainted with

* * * *
A Northern .lap tells a fearsome tale of
how he dived into the Barwoii to mend his fish-
had secured the heathen, having bitten into the
sides of his head. The Jap, in a flurry, drove his
fist into the voracious monster’s mouth and was
at once set free, Sounds apocryphal but the
little brown man is in the local hospital, and has
the teeth marks (close to the eyes) to show.
* * * *
In a Northern court last month Chinese
Li Lap charged Malay Abdullah with assault and
battery. Li Lap presented a piteous spectacle,
his face being covered in every getatable section
with sticking-plaster. The case was dismissed,
and next day Li Lap removed his sticking-plaster
and presented an unscarred phiz to the Mon-
grelian heavens.

“ Suffolk ” : “Silurian ’’ (8., 2/3/'o> „
footprints in carboniferous sandstone
behStKpbed of thediXict co/ftfwhh
a Tertiary deposit. One impression is of the foot of some
huge creature with three toes, the imprint being two
inches deep by 12 across. Another imprint has four toe-
marks, and is Bin. across. There are also bird-tracks like
those of an emu, Jin. deep and 7Jin. long. These speci-
mens are now in Moree (N.S.W.) School of Arts library,
along with the human foot-track already referred to.
Other tracks on these rocks are of smaller animals with
thumbs—either bears or opossums. The writer surmises
that these rocks were a refuge to men and beasts whilst
terrible convulsions of nature were in progress —the sur-
face of the rocks having been subjected to the action of
fire for a period—and that the living creatures thus tak-
ing refuge were probably consumed.

* * * m
How the kanaka improves in years and in
wisdom! The latest development re Tommy
Tanna concerns his unwillingness and absolute
refusal to pay his debts, at the same time that he
puts a handsome sum in the savings’-bank. Isn’t
it time he got a vote for Thrift ?
* * * *
The Bulletin has often asseverated that
the Kanaka makes progress. A few weeks ago a
Polynesian on the Lower Burdekin, who held
several hundreds of pounds in trust for the other
“ boys” (until they should return to the Islands),
collared the loot and departed. If Billy Mallicolo
had only been a church member and a pillar of
the sanctuary the thing would have been complete
and beautiful.
* * • *
Tommy Tanna takes his own peculiar view
of the various functions of civilisation. If a
policeman arrests him when rowdy or drunken —
well and good. Tanna bears the officer no grudge
—it was only the man’s duty. But if a civilian
assists the police (when called upon) in checking a
Kanaka riot, then the Polynesian regards the
citizen so meddling with undying hatred, and
works off his spleen at the very first chance. .
* * * •
Governor Broome once visited the mission
station at New Norcia, W.A., and whilst
rambling through the grounds he came across one
of the blacks belonging to the station, and spoke a
few kindly words m broken English. Much to
the Governor’s astonishment, the black answered
him in Latin—a language with which H.Ex. had
only a “bowing” acquaintance. The Gov. never
fully recovered from the shock. Some enterprising
W.A. scribe should tell us more about New
Norcia.
* * * *
“ Peter K.” : H. J. Mac. (B.
3/11/’00) is wrong in saying that the
Australian aborigines hold to no
hard and fast rules of ceremony in
connection with the burial of their
dead. The Kolor or Mt. Rouse tribe,
the Kuurn-kopan-noot tribe and the
Peak Whurrong, or Hopkins River
tribe, have always adhered to the
following ceremonies in connection
with burials : Children are rolled in
bark and placed in a hollow tree till
dry, when they are removed and
burnt. An ordinary member of a
tribe, if he dies under ordinary cir-
cumstances, is rolled in an opossum-
rug, placed between two sheets
of bark, and buried together with
all his spears and ornaments. If,
however, the weather is too warm to
permit of the full burial ceremony,
the corpse is burnt. A chief is
always burnt and some of his bones
are carried about by his widow for a
certain time.
“ Brew.” : Re puppy-suckling
women. Humboldt saw a South
American Indian woman suckling a
small pig—a practice common enough
also among the Maoris when an
infant died. The Esquimaux rear
puppies in this way, and the wives
of dog-loving Lancashire lads are
credited witli doing the same should
“ t’awd beteh ” peg-out inopportune-
ly. While upon mammarv subjects,
it may be mentioned that multi-
plicity of breasts (polymazia) is fairly
common in hurnans. In ’B6 a Ger-
man woman was found by a leading
Berlin surgeon to possess ten well-
developed mainline, and present
writer has seen a Bcugainvil’e Island
woman with four. Such super-
numerary organs occur sometimes
in curious places—as in the armpit,
shoulder, thigh, and even the face.
The peculiarity seems to depend
mainly on heredity, but primarily
it is a simple enough instance of
atavism (reversion to an earlier type
of the race).

“Yril”: To “Yale” (8., 26/l/’01). I
have spent hundreds of nights sleeping out in the
open, and have watched animals clean through
stilly nights, but cannot say I ever noticed the
phenomenon referred to by Stevenson. In the
fall of the year, however, about an hour before
dawn, symptoms of unrest often appear in human
beings, and sometimes in animals. This, I think,
is due to the chill of approaching dawn affecting
animal life when it is at its lowest point; that is
furthest away from the vivifying influence of the
sun. Of course the dawn chill is apparent all
through the year, but it is most pronounced in the
fall.
“Peepar”: He hares getting
inside enclosures which they could
not jump out of (“RWS,” 8.,
27/10/’O). I have often experienced
the same thing ; but, after mature
consideration, concluded to avoid new
whisky !
“ P.” : Re “ Mac.’s ” inquiry
(8., 13/10/'0). I once had a flying-
squirrel which was, to all appear-
ances, a hermaphrodite, and was
otherwise deformed, the hinder limbs
being missing below the joint where
the ■‘wing” or web joins the leg.
When walking it used to draw the
hind-quarters after it.

“Daw”: Can any zoological
reader tell me why some birds hop,
while others walk or run? I used to
think length of leg had to do witli
the matter, but I have noticed that
while the “Twelve Apostles” pad
the hoof, bower-birds, quite as tall,
hop as if in a sack-race, and the tiny
bush-doves, with legs like wax vestas,
run like lamplighters.
“ Yril ” : Strategic mis-
takes are not confined to
humans. Last summer Peak
Downs (C.Q.) district was
specially favored by rains,
and grass being abundant,
the inevitable plague of grass-
hoppers supervened. Mil-
lions strong the army moved
northwards on its devastat-
ing tour until it struck the
drought - affected country.
For two days it pressed on-
ward, but on the fourth day a
retrograde movement set in,
and continued all through
the fifth day. Riding north-
erly a few days after, I
found corpses in millions.
Another Retreat from Mos-
cow !

“J.S.D.”: A certain
walla by-hunter in Singleton
(N.S.W.) district averages
£5 per week during winter.
He has located along the
mouutain tracks a series of
running wire-loops, hanging
close to the ground, which
catch the marsupials as they
bound up the mountains to-
wards morning from the
water-holes. In the morning
the snarer goes his rounds, skins the captives, and
re-sets the traps for the next night.
* * * *
The M.L. Govt, is taking the Maori
census. After two days’ work one of the
enumerators on the west coast of the North Island
had only got three names. The tribe he was
visiting unanimously refused to give names,
stating that the Govt, only wants the information
so as to know how many Maoris it would have to
fight. There is likely to be still more trouble
further up the coast, in the Te Whiti country.
* * * *
“Wandering J.” : Re malarial fever and
mosquitoes: I have seen both whites and abori-
ginals dying from fever where there were no mos-
quitoes. At the Comet River, C.Q. railway, 20
years ago, after a heavy shower of rain at night,
the ground in the morning would be covered by a
film of greenish jelly, £-inch thick, which disap-
peared as the sun rose. This was the cause of the
fever—the germs are vegetable, not animal. Also
there are plenty of mosquitoes at Klondyke, and
other cold parts, but no malarial fever. The
annoyance of mosquitoes, however, makes a per-
son worried and weak, and, consequently, more
subject to any infection that may be about.
[The recent experiments of Ross, Manson, Celli and
others have definitely proved the causative agent of
human malaria to be a species of mosquito (anopheles).
Without the anopheles there is no malaria. No race of
men possesses any hereditary immunity from malaria, and
blacks are as readily affected by it as Europeans, although
both by evolutional habits may adapt themselves to
escape infection. The Australian black has not had time
to evolve any such means, for the introduction of the
anopheles and its coincident disease has occurred subse-
quent to the colonisation of Australia by Europeans.
The stated non-existence of mosquitoes in the locality
named by “ VV J.” must be due to a perhaps excusable
lack of observation, if the disease were true malaria. The
anopheles does not hum, and is not nearly as annoying as
the ordinary eulex, hence its presence maybe easily over-
looked. The mosquitoes oft Greenland and Klondyke
belong to the culex class, and do not convey malaria to
man, although they are capable of doing so to birds.
The actual “germ” of malaria is distinctly animal, as
may be proved by a visit to any up-to-date laboratory,
and the cycle of development in acute cases of disease
has been demonstrated many times within the last
couple of years.—Ed. B.J

COULDN'T SCORE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ I was fishin’ in the Fitzroy,” said Magee, “ the other
week;
Billy Jonsin, he was with me, it was just by Splitter’s
Creek,
An’ me dog he was a-swimmin’ when he gives a kind of
squeak,
Disappears so bloomin’ sudden that we hadn’t time to
speak ;
An’ I looks at Bill in wonder,
Bill he says to me, ‘ By thunder,
In the name of seven devils, what has took that ’ere dog
under ? ’
“ An’ there was a cuddy drinkin’ just along the bank a
bit ;
Strike me han’some, ask Bill Jonsin, but I nearly had a fit,
When I sees a bloomin’ reptile, an’ you’ll hardly credit it,
Come an’ snatch that cuddy under just as easy as you
spit;
I ain’t no prevaricator,
For I likes the clean pertater,
It was thirty foot or more an’ was a whoppin’ alligator.”
Here he paused and told the pubby that he thought he’d
irrigate.
“ Score another drink to me,” he says, “an’ that ull
make it eight.”
But he met a stern refusal from the whisky-potsntate,
Who implied that alligators couldn’t score upon his slate ;
“ You’re a blanky fabricator,”
Said the boose-manipulator ;
“ When you want another drink from me you bring that
alligator.”

The fruit crop is very good this year, and
the prudent housekeeper is busy looking after the
year’s supply of preserves and filling up shortages
in Jam Jars. There is but little satisfaction in
using a common, badly-fitting, badly-made jar,
which, moreover, is liable to break when the fruit
is poured in. Some new patterns have been intro-
duced this year by M‘Lean Bros, and Rigg, Ltd.,
Melb. and Sydney. Notably the Gilchrist —this is
a large-mouthed jar, with porcelain immerser ;
and the Canton jar, a clear, white glass, with easy-
fitting spring top showing the contents to the best
advantage.*

SCHWEPPE’S
DRY GINGER ALE
La Nature gives, as well-authenticated,
the following extraordinary instance of the dis-
semination of snake-poison:—
A Brazilian mulatto, having been bitten on a finger by
a venomous snake (Lachesis rhombeata), promptly
chopped the digit off. After three days (during which
he experienced no ill-effects) he went back to bury the
severed finger, but found only the bare bones of it, and a
multitude of large black ants, locally called “ sard-sard,”
still busy on them. The ants attacked him, bit him on
the neck and arms, and made him retreat. The same
night he developed all the characteristic symptoms of
snake-poisoning - , and, despite medical aid, sqon died.

It was during the Franco-Prussian war.
A drover had ridden ahead to the township, and
on returning to camp in the evening he was be-
sieged for war news by his men—especially his
German cook. “The war is practically over,” he
said, “ and Germany is demanding a big indem-
nity. Terms are being arranged by that German
chap—Bisgig or Fisgig, or whatever his dashed
name is.” “ You vas not mean Yon Bismarck ? ”
asked the cook in horror. “ Ah, Bismarck, that’s
the name,” said the drover. “ Bismarck—Bisgig,
Fisgig,” said the cook, furiously ; then, suddenly,
“To hell mit you and your tarn sheep! 1 nod
shtob von meenit lonker mit you ! ” And he loft.

“Pelican”: To “H.S.” fB., 26/l/’Ol.
Port Curtis (Q.) niggers also “plant” their
niggers on an elevated bier, but do not live there-
under. By the way, what about blood-poisoning
if the gins—cut and bleeding, as “H.S.” avers—
got any corpse-juice into open wounds ! P.C.
aboriginals also go in for ground and tree burial.
In the latter case they climb up a hollow tree to a
fair height, cut a hole into hollow, jam in cadaver
feet-foremost, and let it slide. This is considered
rather a swagger style— certainly dingo-proof and
supposed to be devil-proof.

“ Pelican ” : Biking up
Clermont (C.Q.) way I noticed
seven emus on the track. Curio-
sity held them till I was about
ten yards away, then they started
off "ahead as pacemakers, just
keeping their distance. I didn’t
try to shake them up till we got
on a fine piece of road with a
slight down grade. Then I went
for them, got right up and set
mv bell going. Result, a mad
scatter in all directions.

A SHORT MEMORY.
“ BRISBANE, Saturday.— Fourteen returned Queensland soldiers, all invalided, reached
Brisbane by mail train on Friday night. There was much comment on the fact that no official
of any sort was at the station to meet them. All were strangers to Brisbane except two, and
the men only possessed between them a few shillings. They were compelled to seek shelter
for the night in boarding-houses.” —Daily paper.

ONCE AGAIN THE DEAD HOUSE.


The Straits Times on the S’African war :
Until now blood has been running only in tiny rivulets.
In the interests of peace it will in future have to run in
streams. There will be no peace in S’Africa until the
Boers have been exterminated, and no sound is heard on
both sides of the Vaal but the lamentations of women
weeping for their fallen husbands, sons, fathers, and
sweethearts. Complete suppression without mercy, a
curt refusal of each request for negotiation, relentless
attacks and persecution should be henceforward Eng-
land’s policy. When the Boers fall back into Zululand
we should allow the Zulus to enter the Transvaal terri-
tory, where our own troops cannot be employed, and
u'hen the Boer women have to suffer from this, let them
remember that it was they who forced the men to fight.
When we have first brought the Boers to submission, the
British army in the two Republics must exterminate the
Boer vermin, and soak the soil with their blood that the
grass may grow the quicker.
Again the fearsome Melbourne “honor-
arium ! ” A suburban butcher, who had for some
time diddled an employe by paying him 30s. a
week while his competitors were paying the legal
minimum wage of 45s. was cordially sympathised
■with by the local Mayor, himself an employer, who
remarked: “We will be legislated out of this
country soon,” and regretfully fined him one shil-
ling without costs. Which is only another argu-
ment for the abolition of the honorary-magistrate
scandal. How enthusiastically would this magis-
terial apologist for sweating have jumped on the
employe had he stolen fifteen shillings’ worth of
his employer’s meat for several consecutive weeks !
It would doubtless have been months “without
the option.”

Issue of £1,000,000 Debentures
Being- part of a sum of £2,374,213 authorised to he raised by the QUEENSLAND GOVERNMENT
LOAN ACT, 64 Viet. No 24, 1900.
To be in addition to, and to rank pari passu with QUEENSLAND GOVERNMENT LOANS already existing.
Repayable at Par, Ist January, 1951.
but may be redeemed on or after Ist JANUARY, 1926, upon twelve months’ notice being given.
Interest being at the Rate of JE3J per Centum per Annum,
payable from Ist January, 1901.
THE TREASURER of the STATE of QUEENSLAND invites tenders for £1,000,000 QUEENSLAND GOVERN-
MENT DEBENTURES bearing interest at the rate of £3J per cent, per annum, repayable at par on the Ist
JANUARY, 1951, but which may be paid off at par on or after the Ist JANUARY, 1926, upon twelve months' notice
being given by notice in the Government Gazette and in the principal newspapers circulating in Australia.
These Debentures will be in addition to, and will rank pari passu with the Queensland Government Loans already
existing.
The Loan is secured upon the Consolidated Revenue of Queensland and is part of a loan of £2,374,213 required
for: —
Railways_ .. £1,624,393

Marine Department

A full six
Dividends
The Debentures will be issued free of Stamp Duty, and will be made out payable to the holder.
The Dividends on the Debentures will be payable half-yearly, on the Ist JANUARY and Ist JULY,
months’ interest calculated upon the nominal amount of Debentures will be payable on the Ist JULY, 1901.
will be paid, free of exchange, by the Banking Agents of the Government at Brisbane, Sydney, Melbourne
Adelaide.
Tenders may be for the whole or any part of the Debentures in multiples of £lOO. No tender must be for less
than £lOO. Each tender must state what amount of money will be given for every £lOO of Debentures. All Tenders
must be at prices which are multiples of sixpence.
Tenders must be addressed to the Treasurer and delivered at the Treasury, Brisbane, before 2 o’clock on TUES
DAY, the 19th MARCH.
The Treasurer retains the right of accepting or rejecting any Tender.
A deposit of £5 per cent, on the amount of Debentures tendered for must be paid at the time of the delivery of the
Tender, but the deposit must not be enclosed in the Tender. Where no allotment is made the deposit will be returned
and in case of partial allotment the balance of the deposit will be applied towards the first instalment.
The dates at which the further payments on account of the Loan will be required are as follows
On Thursday, 28th March, 1901, j so te ." der .®f , for ea s; h hundred Pounds of Debentures a»,
J { when added to Deposit, will leave Eighty Pounds (Sterling) to be mid •
On Thursday, 25th April, 1901, £3O per cent. ;
On Monday," 27th May, 1901, £3O per cent. ;
On Thursday, 27th June, 1901, £2O per cent. ;
but the instalments may be paid in full on or after the 28th March, 1901, under discount at the rate of £2 per eent.
per annum. In the case of default in the payment of any instalment at its proper date, the deposit and instalments
previously paid will be liable to forfeiture.
No further issue of this Loan will be made within the Commonwealth until after twelve months from this date.
Brokerage of ££ per cent, on the face value of the Debentures will be allowed upon accepted Tenders to members
of recognised Stock Exchanges of Australia.
The only Loans authorised and floated in Queensland by Local Authorities are those issued by the Municipalities
of North and South Brisbane amounting in all to £490,000. All other Loans to Local Authorities, including those for
the construction of Waterworks and for Harbour Improvements, are made by the Government and are included in
the general debt of Queensland. There is no floating debt represented by Treasury Bills.
Applications must be made upon the printed forms which may be obtained at the Treasury, Brisbane; the
Queensland National Bank, Limited, Sydney ; the National Bank of Australasia, Limited, Melbourne" and Adelaide.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Australia can’t have Freetrade, and she
can’t have Protection, but she must have ME.”—
Gr. H. Reid.
Red-tape hasn’t yet made up its mind
whether N.S.W. is “state” or “colonv.” The
Govt. Gazette of the “State ” is issued ; but the
proclamations within refer to “state” and
“ colony ” indifferently.
“P.W.C.”: As a name for the Federal
capital [ would suggest “ Ellimatta,” a melodious
native name, meaning “our home,” or “our
residence.” And how would “ Waratah ” do as a
substitute for “New South Wales”?
Intelligent remark by Sydney Morning
Herald
It (Protection) is a policy which has, in almost every
case, been adopted by a fluke.
What a lot of flukes there must be in this world !
the clock moves on. Q. Works Minister
Leahy announces that some of the new railways
will be built under the direction of Govt, engineers
by day labor. Shade of John Robb ! What is
Banaualand coming to ? And this under the
Phi lp Government !
G H. Reid says he believes in removing
not only black but all colored labor from
Australia. Promises, promises, promises ! But
what did G. H. Reid every really do ? He was
Premier and had a chance to shut but the Hindoo
influx. Barton hadn’t.
As Col. Lassetter is the avowed champion
of the N.S.W. head-quarters military staff—a
crowd whose business it is to establish the nearest
approach possible to a military tyranny in Aus-
tralia—be will have the support of all intelligent
democrats in his Senatorial candidature.
Once again (at Newcastle this time) the
Labor Federal candidate is, not the brainy, cul-
tured democrat, hut the diligent departmental
log-roller—the man who has never said “801 ” to
the local party bosses, no matter what they did,
or neglected to barrack for a new culvert.
The M'Eacharn is truly iu a tight place.
As a big importer and shipper he lovesFreetrade,
vet he promises to support Barton’s Protectionist
policy. Also he believes in a “ White Australia,”
yet he asserts that “the employment of Kanaka
labor is essential for the sugar industry.”
ri N.S. Wales is still waiting to hear Sydney
Teleghaph say something about the immorality
of giving 50 per cent, protection to the N.S.W.
peany Freetrade paper by charging Jd. postage
on Queensland and Victorian and S.A. papers
while carrying the local one at the suffering tax-
payer’s expense.
“ The fallacy of protection is illustrated,”
says Mr. Milieu, “by the fact that after3s years’
assistance the Victorian manufactures still require
bolstering up.” Also the fallacy of eating and
drinking is shown by the fact that a healthy man
wants his breakfast this morning, although he
had breakfast yesterday morning.
Freetectionist Joe .Cook’s policy (as he
gave it to the electors of Parramatta electorate
recently):—
A Voice : “ Give us your policy.”
Mr. Cook : “ My policy is anti-Barton.”
Holy Joe Cook also said that Barton had no
policy. So Cook’s only policy is the policy of
being against the man that hasn’t any policy.
From Sydney D.T.
“ If it is to make the people rich, why,” asks Mr. Reid,
“do the Protectionists clamor for only moderate. Protec-
tion.”
If it was to make the people rich, why, asks The
Bulletin, did Reid and Co. give the people only
moderate Freetrade ? Why didn’t they wet their
Dry Dog !
Two pars, that kick one another on the
same page of J. Haynes’ organ :
Mr. John Haynes, M.L.A., yielding - to the advice of
many friends that his services are invaluable to his elec-
torate in the .State Parliament, has retired from his candi-
dature for Robertson in the Federal Parliament.
Mr. Haynes has retired from the Federal contest, being-
disinclined to incur the heavy expense and the risk of loss
to his journalistic business.
Sydney Telegraph :
It is reported that the banks in Queensland threaten
to stop all advances to sugar-growers if black labor is dis-
pensed with.
In which case there will be so much the more
room for a State Land Bank, like those already
at work in Maoriland, S.A. and Victoria. Even
the nigger has a silver lining.
At Kingston’s opening meeting in Ade-
laide Town Hall, although-three of his old col-
leagues, Cordon, Jenkins and O’Loughlin, were
present, Premier Holder was conspicuously
absent. There was much laughter when King-
ston demanded to know if there was a genuine
Freetrader in the audience, and a weak voice
replied “ Where’s Freddy Holder?”
The present sensation of Q. politics is the
flitting of Philp to S’Africa “for a much-needed
holiday.” There is, of course, something beneath
the distracted dry dog owner.

Federal candidate:
I have advocated the employment of kanakas in

Some men are so peculiarly constituted that they
would be proud of having been in gaol 20 years, or
of having been dead for 35 years and six months.
Democratic H. B. Higgins, the most respectable

show for northern Melbourne at the Fed.

Barr —not known.
Cabled that Kitchener is coming to peace-
terms with Botha—Steyn and De Wet standing
out, irreconcileable. Peace is what the fat
burghers of the towns have prayed for; but to
fight on “ for ever and for ever and for ever ” —to
the last gun, the last meal, the last man— that ,
whether discreet or indiscreet, expedient or inex-
pedient, strikes the heroic note which touches
humanity longest.
The ‘“official” Labor instruction in N.S.-
Wales seems to be to vote for Smith and M‘Don-
nell, the two Labor nominees, and throw away
the other four votes on the most hopeless candi-
dates offering—for choice, the Socialists. And if,
through neglecting to give Liberals a lift, six
Tories top "the poll, how will the Labor “ad-
visers ” feel about it then ? And how will the
fool-voters who took that advice ?
Sydney D.T. printed a wire stating that a
speech made at Parkes by “ Mr. Thomas Brown,
M. the unknown person who is being run for
the Canoblas against Wise by the Sydney Shoddy
party with its tongue in its cheek—“ created a

That thankless post, the leadership of
N. Opposition, is going begging. Joseph Cook
was first thought of, but, as he thinks he has a reason-
able prospect of winning a seat in the Federal
Parliament, he passed the billet on to Carruthers,
who also refused. Ex-Evening News editor j’.
A. Hogue, M.L.A. for Glebe, is now considering
the offer, and may, on the political demise of
Lyne’s State colleagues, become N.S.W. Premier.
About two-thirds or three-fourths of the
Federal candidates are reaching out for the votes
of both parties. They say they are Freetraders
by conviction, but are going to vote for a heavy
tariff in order to produce revenue. When the
numbers are up it seems as if the Federal House
of Representatives would consist of about 30 men
who will vote for Protection because they believe
in it, and 45 who will vote for Protection because
they don’t believe in it
Proportionately to its numbers N.S.W.
Labor Party runs the largest number of State
legislators for the Federal Parliament. The
Freetraders have nominated W. M. Hughes, J.
Thomas, and Brown as their candidates. M ‘Goweii
(Redfern), Watkins (Newcastle), J. C. Watson
(Bland), and Ferguson (Macquarie), are selected
Bartonites, and promise to give his Government
a show. Besides these W. J. Spence and S. Law
are running as Labor candidates, while others are
still doubtful starters.

w or t h ■ keeping.
Perth corporation,
like most Australian
municipal bodies, has only one eye, a dim and
filmy optic set in the back of its head. Wherefore
the Council never realises anything in time, and
is hardly even quite aware yet that Queen Anne
is .lead. It has a vague idea, however, that that
estimable lady begins to show signs of breaking-up.
Premier Lyne’s backdown about putting a
political pal in the N.S.W. commercial agency ’
billet in London was a painful exhibition of
trapeze contortions. The Board of Exports
plumply let him know that it would resign in a
body if a man whose qualifications were merelv
political were appointed. Other persons and
bodies spoke as resolutely, and at last the political
appointment was cried off. It was a clear case of
making Lyne climb down.
Now that the 125 N.S.W. State electorates
have been readjusted according to population, it
may be assumed that Treasury-Bill Lyne has
indefinitely postponed all idea of reducing the
Assembly. When the subject crops upthere will
be the inevitable wail: “ We have just gone to
the expense and trouble of re-adjusting the elec-
toral boundaries and getting new rolls prepared.
If we cut down the Assembly to 70 or 80 members
all this money and labour will be wasted.”
“ S’mawlcum ” M‘Eacharn’s chance of
scoring against democratic little Dr. Maloney
in the struggle for the Melbourne seat looks
rather poor for divers reasons. His weak and
timid efforts to win grace with the democrats-
will drive the commercial men who have the op-
tion of voting for their business or for their
suburban residences into choosing the latter, and
Melbourne Federal electorate absorbs large
samples of the surrounding radical suburbs, giving
the labor vote command of the situation. The
third candidate is unknown, so far as the
majority of the electors are concerned.
So far, Southern Melbourne is the most
rushed of the Vic. Federal constituencies. Seven
candidates are after this seat, and not a strong
man amongst them. David Gaunson, in his old
form, would walk in, but David arched his. politi-
cal back when S. Melb. discarded him, years ago,
and since then his opinions have become dull, flat,
and respectable. A local parson, Rev. J. B.
Ronald, is opposing David. Godfrey, M.L.C.,
and “ Aileckzander Sootherland, M.A.,” the
literary professor, are other candidates. F. C.
Gray,_ex-pietist who, as M.L.A., raised a hurri-
cane in a tea-cup over the subsidiary suburban
wives of hon. members, also cordially invites S.
Melb. to put him on to £400 a year.
Millen, of N.S.W. Upper House, told the
people of Newcastle last Saturday that N.S.W.,
through following the enlightened policy of Free-
trade, had for 35 years escaped the heavy Customs
taxation to which Victorians were subject. It is
a pity the Foreign Importers’ party can’t decide
which yarn it will select. It claims that N.S.W.
has had Freetrade for 35 years, and at the same
time it claims that George Reid is entitled to the
eternal gratitude of the people because he estab-
lished Freetrade five years ago. Now, either
Reid didn’t establish Freetrade in 1806, in which
case all the boasting about him is a lie, or else
N.S.W. hadn’t Freetrade till 1896, in which case
the statement that the progress it made before
that date was due to Freetrade is a lie. Which
lie does the party propose to stick to ?
About S.A.’s Federal candidates :
In all 28 candidates have nominated for S.A. Federal
seats, 17 for House of Reps, and 11 for Senate. Of the
former 7 are Protectionists, 5 “Revenue Taritfists,” 2
Freetraders without reservation, one a single-taxer, and
two others unknown. As Holder has declared for a 15
per cent, tariff, and announces that “ Protection is
inevitable,” he is included in the Protectionists. For the
Senate, there are two unreserved Freetraders, six Pro-
tectionists, and three Revenue-Tariffists. Of the 28 can-
didates, not one comes from the country, two alleged
farmers being Caldwell and Copley, M.’sP., city men.
For the seven seats in the popular House, Kingston,.
Holder, Glynn, Solomon and Price are considered certain-
ties, and for the six in the Senate Raker, Downer,
Playford and Symon will probably be far above any
others. After the nine candidates named above electors
have mainly mediocrities to select from. Only one mer-
chant has nominated, although nearly all the big Adelaide
commercial men have been tipped at different times.
Editor Sowden, of S.A. Register, although much boomed,
didn’t face the music when the time came, and Bony-
thon, of the self-ADVERTisER, had to be whipped up to
the post. Symon is considered to have made a great mis-
take in nominating for the Senate, but he refused to
listen to his friends, urging that the Senate would least
interfere with his immense legal practice if elected.

BRISBANE.
NEWCASTLE.
The indecent haste with which N.S.W. State
Cabinet has resolved to carry out the late Pakkes’
precious State-House scheme—the memorial stone
of which is to be laid by the “ Jook ” —apparently
as an excuse for another drunk —ought to finally
seal the doom of Long Lyne and co. As for the
notable idea of decorating the building, to begin
with, with memorial tablets in honor of those who
fell in the Boer war, how does the Cabinet know
that before its fancy dead-house is half-finished
the very names of S’Africa and the Empire will
not be abhorred by the people ? The chances are,
anyhow, that the thing will never get beyond the
foundation-stone stage. There is in Sydney
Domain another foundation-stone, laid years ago,
for a new Parliament House; yet the State Legis-
lature still meets in a disreputable old rookery
which keeps on getting patched up and tinkered
with at great public expense. The dead and gone
contingenters will probably have to remain un-
commemorated —when Lynk’s lot have had their
York drunk—till the Day of Judgment.
*
Lately cabled that
Boer women detained in the British camps on reduced
rations are those whose husbands are on commando.
If this means —as it apparently does—-that certain
Boer women-prisoners have been placed upon re-
duced rations because their husbands are still in
arms, The Bulletin doesn’t know whether to
laugh at the unconscious humor of the British
Lion or to snort at the contemptible meanness of
that much-lauded beast.

G. A. Oarver). Life in itself was simply a burden. I was
attended to by doctors in Wagga and Sydney, who were

It was recently rumored (falsely) that
Halifax, the High Church English peer, had
’verted. A sample of the letters he received :
My Lord,—l was heartily glad to see the announcement
of your joining the Church of Borne. Like Judas, you
have gone to your own place. Dante says that in hell
Judas is shunned by all. When you enter hell, Judas
will no longer be shunned by all. You and he will be fit
companions- -two of the choicest tools the devil ever had.
One Godfrey, a Vic. criminal with a repu-
tation, is a student of human nature, and turns
his discoveries to account. When liberated from
Geelong gaol t’other day, Godfrey made for Mel-
bourne, and drifted into a “ ’appy ’ome ” at Wind-
sor. To the lady who met him on the mat he
said : “I just seen your boss up Chapel-st. with a
pretty girl. They was goin’ in to be photy-
graphed.” The lady immediately fell into a con-
dition of great excitement, and, dashing on a hat,
fled in the direction of Chapel-st. When she
returned from a vain search, she found that various
articles of jewellery and some money had escaped
from her bedroom during her absence. Now Mr.
Godfrey is asked to explain how the missing
jewellery came into his possession, which seems
an easy one for Mr. Godfrey.
“ H.8.T.” : Australian shippers of live-
stock upon Eastern contracts will do well to be
cautious when catering for the Kaiser’s “loot
grabbers ”in China. The s.s. Den of Airlie left
Sydney (?) loaded with sheep and bullocks to be
delivered at Taku before the port was closed by
ice. This she failed to do, and on arrival the
German military authorities declined to take
delivery of the animals, either at Taku or Chefoo.
After hawking the cargo in various parts of Chow-
land, it was finally auctioned off at Shanghai to
an attendance of three Chow buyers, who got
prime Australian cattle at 22 taels per head and
sheep at 4 taels (local exchange rate is about 2s.
lid. per tael). In the ordinary course of events
the freight alone on cattle runs to close on £5 and
on sheep 10s. This is what comes of the delusion
that anything with two sticks and a smoke-stack
is good enough to ship stock on. A little more
speed in this case would have made all the differ-
ence between dire loss and a handsome profit.

Mr. James Morgan having retired in his
favor, Mr. J. D. Fitzgerald, barrister, will make a
very strong run for the Robertson (N.S.W.) seat
in the House of Reps. Mr. Fitzgerald is a good
speaker and debater, a travelled and well-informed
man, and an ardent Australian.
The silliest Freetrade hoax up to date,
from Sydney Truth :
But probably the most infamous and audacious item in
Barton’s vicious Victorian policy is that seriously imputed
to him and his colleagues—an intention to impose an
Excise Duty on Goal at the pit’s mouth.
That would naturally raise the price of coal, and
the Victorian manufacturer—Victoria being the
greatest manufacturing State in the Common-
wealth —would pay a bigger share of the increase
than the manufacturer of any other State. So the
probability of this as an item of Victorian policy
sticks out like a Christmas snowball at Broken
Hill.
