SPORTING NOTIONS.
, 1 e was interesting from start
to finish, though not quite satisfactory to backers of
Clean Sweep. Boy Richardson rode the Melb. Cup win-
ner as though he had instructions to wait upon La Cara-
bine. VVhat time Paul Pry was leading the field by 40 or
50 lengths Clean Sweep and the mare ran close to-
gether, with Advance bringing up the rear. When Paul
Pry began to collapse it was Advance who went out to
overtake him, and at that critical point young Clean
Sweep dropped back behind La Carabine. Turning into
the straight Advance took the lead, La Carabine,
under the whip, was chasing the Maorilander, and
Clean Sweep seemed to drop further behind. Advance
for the moment had the race won to all appearances.
The flustered jockey (Jenkins) gave one the impression that
he had involuntarily eased his nag. Meanwhile Clean
Sweep was certainly going faster than La Carabine, and
when the mare had finally won the race by two lengths
the young Melb. Cup winner was only three-quarters of a
length behind Advance. The colt, carrying 161 b. less
than an3' of the others, was never so near the front as
when his stable companion passed the post. In a sloW-
run Champion the light-weight winner of a fast-run
Melb. Cup was the only one of the party that never held
the lead—he came back to the birdcage the least dis-
tressed of the four. Ijad the jockey been instructed to
send the colt along at a sound pace from the start, trust-
ing to its light weight and its proved staying power, Clean
Sweep would probably have won the Champion.
* * * »
La Carabine’s Champion Stakes’ win stamps her
as absolutely the best w.f.a. nag in Australasia. Since
’65 only two other mares (Romula and Quiver) got to the
front in this event. They were 3 and 4yr. olds respec-
tively ; La Carabine is 6. Of the four starters three came
from one stable —Scobie’s. Time was, compared with
figures previously put up, rather slow, as it exceeded the
fastest (when Quiver and Wallace dead-heated in ’96) by
13sec., though the winner finished 9sec. inside the limit,*
which saved a reduction in prize-money. Field has only
been larger once in the past six years.
* * *
Dreamland’s winning of the Australian Cup
recompensed the trainer in a measure for the disappoint-
ments he suffered with the son of Trenton and Nightmare
long ago, when his luck was as notoriously bad as it now
is conspicuously otherwise. For some years Dreamland
was Jim Scobie’s pet weakness. A more experienced
trainer would have seen the absolute necessity for giving
the colt time to pull round after the attack of strangles
which spoilt its Derby and Cup chances, but Scooie
didn’t begin to be wise in the Spring of ’94. Dreamland
was the crack horse of his stable ; it had won Maribyrnong
Plate as a 2-year-old, and had been specially saved for
classic honors. To scratch it for all engagements would
have meant a cruel sacrifice of sanguine hopes, as Scobie
persuaded himself and the owners (Bailey and Orr) that
the colt was making a complete recovery from
its illness, and would be all right again by
Nov. Result, Dreamland .ran nowhere in the Derby and
Cup of ’94. Next year, he had 10 races—“ nowhere ”in
nine of them (including another Melb. Cup), and second
in the other. Following year, he scored three noivheres
(including a fourth failure in Melb. Cup). In ’99, Dream-
land scored a win—the second win in nearly' seven years
of racing—after a dead-heat with La Carabine, then re-
garded as a common, jaded mare. But the veteran also
achieved some more nowheres (including a fifth Melb.
Cup defeat). By this time Messrs. Bailey and Orr were
weary of Dreamland. They sold the ‘handsome old
identity to S’Rupert Clarke, and the new owner puc him
to the stud for a year or two, and the new life suited
Deamland so well that he was put back into train mg, and
the training enabled him to win last week’s Australian
Cup for his singularly lucky owner.
* * * *
After Gosport had beaten Dreamland at Caul-
field, handicapper Dakin predicted (to the owner) that he
would win the Bond Cup and Australian Cup. The mis-
hap which caused Gosport to be scratched for both events
was not thought to be serio.us at the time, but his per-
formance in the Autumn Handicap last Thursday, when
he couldn’t raise a gallop, suggested that he was suffering
from something more than the effects of ten days’ con-
finement in his stable. If the Portsea horse conies on
again he should be worth considering for any long-
distance handicap at a reasonable weight.
* • » *
Dreamland’s win of Australian Cup recalls his
last previous victory years ago, in Maribyrnong Plate, so
nicely accomplished that he looked like being the best of
his time. He was foaled 10 years since. Season after
season saw him shape so badly that he got into last
week’s big race with the feather of 6.12 up. Only twice
before has the Cup been won by nags carrying lighter
burdens—Nathalie (6.8) in ’64, and Pollio (6.7) in ’B2.
Sybil humped a similar weight (to the oz.) home in ’77.
Aged prads won seven Australian Cups up to and in-
clusive of ’BO, but since only Lochiel (’B9) and Dreamland
have passed the post first.
* * * »
Mo’s yelp that some remark made by Rupert
Clarke the evening before encouraged the impression that
Dreamland was a doubtful starter in the Australian Cup,
thus causing the odds to be stretched a bit, is distinctly
good coming from such a source. How beautifully he
gives himself away, too, to those who took the lengthened
odds !
• • * *
Bookmakers and punters alike begin to wish
that when three or four candidates from Scobie’s stable
go for the same race their respective owners would not so
invariably fancy one or more of the other chances in
preference to their own chance. This alleged conflict of
opinion is not always in keeping with appearances. For
instance, Rupert Clarke has stated that he didn’t much
fancy the old stallion for the Aust. Cup. He thought The
Bride would win.. All the same a stranger watching the
business of saddling-up Dreamland, Malster and The
Bride for said Cup, might easily have come to the con-
clusion that the old un was the stable elect. And there
were previous circumstances to account for Tiie Bum,e-
tin’s belief in the horse that started a better favorite than
The Bride and beat her.
* * * *
The Wakeful and Dreamland double has been
spoken of as a strange coincidence, but the coincid-
ence ” is explained by the close blood relationship of the
two horses. They were suitably named after their
respective dams, Insomnia and Nightmare.
Said now that Wakeful (winner of Newmarket), *
prior to money going on, ran the big sprint distance
(with ordinary shoes) in 1.16 J, and a mile in 1.44, thus
wiping out everything previously done at St. Albans by:
Aurum, Bobadil and co., in their time.
“ Gun ” : Been stated, in places, that Wakeful
was the hottest favorite ever known to win a Newmarket.
Not so. Bungebah went out at 2to 1. Biggest outsiders
among successful nags were Churchill (33 to 1), and
Laundress and Forest (100 to 2 each).
* * * *
The Loch Plate, last Saturday, provided an in-
teresting study in Ikey’s odds, and a good race, as it
happened, between the two “cracks” from Seobie’s
stable. Betting at the outset was 7t04 on La Carabine,
and 7to 4 against Clean Sweep. Ten minutes later prices
began to fluctuate, and presently it was “even money”
between the two. Then, for three or four minutes the
books took odds about the colt and laid odds against the
mare. Finally the market resumed its original form, and
La Carabine went to the post at 7 to 4 on, whilst Clean
Sweep was quoted in some places at 2to 1 against. The
race confirmed a general belief that La Carabine might
have been beaten by Clean Sweep for the Champion if the
youngster had followed close on the heels of old Paul
Pry. He is a quick beginner and he stays quite as well
as La Carabine, who is rather slow to get going. In the
Loch Plate the Melb. Cup winner went away at a good,
but not a cut-throat, pace and put himself a couple of
lengths ahead of the mare. He didn’t come back to her.
She had to recover the two lost lengths, and the effort
tired her. At the distance La Carabine had her head in
front, but the colt was not quite spun out. With both
jockeys whipping their hardest Clean Sweep won by
half a head. The Loch Plate has a reputation for great
finishes. It was over the lf-mile course that Auraria and
Wallace (Melb. Cup and Derby winners) ran their famous
dead-heat, when the thousands in the Grand-stand stood
up as one individual and unloosed their feelings in a
mighty roar.
» * » *
San Fran at. 6.12 in Sydney Cup looks attrac-
tive after his third in Australian Cup, provided he doesn’t
incur a penalty in between.
* * * *
The doubt as to the real capabilities of Maori-
land horses Advance and Boreas in competition with Aus-
tralia’s best remains to be settled at Randwick next
month. The heavy appearance of the pair suggested that
they were not quite fit, yet they showed no particular
signs of distress after their gallops. The riding, of Jen-
kins was condemned, nevertheless the failure of Advance
in the All-Aged Stakeslooked due to want of courage in the
horse rather than lack of grit in the jockey. All things
considered, it seems probable that the Maorilanders were
rated too highly by the Australian handicapper, and
equally likely that they were not in condition to do full
credit to themselves or the much-criticised little man who
was expected to make them win.

* . • * *
Safety’s capture of Bourke Handicap (7fur.)
last.week was a painful contrast to the mare’s running in
Hawksburn Handicap at Caulfield ten days previously.
In the Caulfield race (6fur.) Safety carried 7.7, started at
20 to 1, and finished seventh. At Flemington the same
Safety, carrying 7.2, was backed down to 6 to 4, and the
“ good thing ” came off. Interesting to know why the
despised and much-beaten outsider of Feb. 23rd was con-
sidered a dead bird for Bourke Handicap aforesaid on
March sth.
* * » *
Mo whined himself sick after the last race at
Moorefield (Sydney) Saturday. Teazer (late Crystal) a
whispered red-hot thing, was heavily backed down to a
very short price and went out first favorite. Zero,
absolutely unsupported, appeared to some to win by a
short head, but Judge Olliffe saw it differently. Teazer’s
number went. up. Mo. squirmed and squeaked as he only
can when things are not to his liking, and wouldn’t have
disgorged a cent had it been possible to get out of it.
Result made a huge difference ; had Zero scored it meant
a “ skinner” all round but as it was punters made a big
dip into most of the satchels. Bongara, Zero and Teazer
passed the post almost in a line. Anyone of the trio
might have been declared winner without bringing
censure upon the responsible official, except from Ikey,
who would have been suited best by Zero, hence his wail.
* * * *
A remarkable bit of bad luck for a jockey hap-
pened at recent Hawkesdale (Vic.) races. In the Hurdle
Race, Addition got rid of his rider at a jump, and then
set sail after the field. Getting on terms with History,
ridden by Lyons, Addition ran close alongside, and the
jock’s log got through the dangling reins unnoticed by
him. At the next hurdle Addition pulled back and
brought History’s rider nearly off. Fortunately the jerk
broke the bridle, and Lyons made a splendid recovery,
but his chance in the race was spoilt.
Heavyweight mill at Golden Gate A. C.
(Sydney) last week between big 1 Armidale maintenance
m in M’Ooll and Australian Artillery Bandsman Thompson
bristled with hot situations. The pair punched, ’rastled,
and roughed like men possessed, threw themselves at each
other, and mauled and floundered as if there was no such
thing as science in the game. Two strong navvies would
at least have shaped as well. M‘Coll proved the stronger
and better-conditioned of the pair. Thompson went
down floored somehow or other) half-a-dozen times, but
still kept coming until a lead-laden jolt on the jaw finally
stretched him out Many of M‘Coll’s thumps had the
force of mule-kicks. When Australian premier Doherty
trained in Sydney last year he boasted that no man had
ever been able to lay him out. M‘Ooll tackled the task,
and, getting a fair opening, landed a right hook that sent
Doherty star-gazing for more than the allotted lOsecs.
Return bruising bout between negro “Ike”
Stewart and half-caste Maori Ruenalf at Golden Gate A.C.
(Sydney) Saturday night brought a big house. Ruenalf
shaped like a winner till he knocked up his right on Ike’s
iron head. The thing then degenerated into an old-
womanish scramble with Ruenalf doing-all he could to
keep as far away as possible. Though the blacker of the
two often had his opponent in dire trouble he didn’t seem
to know it, and ultimately the agony ended in a draw.
* * * * ...
Clem “ Cribb ” (bruiser Otto’s elder brother) is
in Sydney, and may fight shortly. He looks more like,
the boxer than Otto, being tall, long-reached and lanky-
limbed, but brother Arthur’s drawback (though not quite
so pronounced) is there—Clem’s nose will always be at
least an inch nearer the enemy than it is expedient it
should be, or than Otto’s ever was.. The .new-comer’s
previous fighting experiences have been with bare
knuckles—be knows little or nothing about glove-work.
There are six in the family. Writer, so far, has only seen
three, and is satisfied that there’s not more than one Otto
among them.
* * * *
News from U.S. is that Martin (“Buff”)
Costello wants to tackle fighting again ; he has been out
of it (running a pub at Montreal) for years. Will be re-
membered that Costello had “ The Lounge,” Bourke-
street, Melh., up to perhaps 10 years ago. “ Buff ” came
to Australia with “young Mitchell” and both fought here
many times. Former was reckoned the trickiest at the
game we had ever seen. Costello, during his stay in this
section, met all our best men—Peter Jackson, Mick
Dooley, George Seale, Billy M’Carthy, &c., and fought a
stiff battle with Paddy Slavin “ in the Open ” near Albury
(N.S.W.) He was a good-natured, popular fellow who
got rid of money »s fast as it came to him. Two big Vic.
sports (both since dead) stood to the Yankee pug well—-
one, particularly, supplied him with large sums on several
occasions.

Tom M‘Kibbin is still the sensation of Adelaide
club cricket. The ex-Bathurst man followed up his 8 for
81 against E. Adelaide by making 68 not out against the
bowling of Travers and co. By the way, Ernest Jones is
now working hard as a lumper hand for Adelaide s.s. 00.
at Port Adelaide.
* * * »
“ Watchem ” writes: “ Second round of Mel-
bourne club cricket, just concluded, puts East high on
top, and the only interest in the competition will be
whether Melbourne or Hawksburn is second. Stuckey,
M'Allister, Howlett, Godby, Horan, M'Miehael, Laver,
Officer and Carter are an excellent batting combination,
but East owes its success in a great measure to its
bowlers, Collins and Laver, who have been irresistible
this year. Saunders, the crack bowler in inter-State
matches, makes no show in club cricket, his average
being very poor. On t’other hand, Alf. Carter (North),
who failed lamentably in big cricket, has a marvellous
club average, his 15 wickets against Richmond being the
season’s record. His brother Jack, now playing again
for North, who always bowls well in Saturday cricket,
never made a show in inter-State, although often tried.
The Vic. certainties for next Australian XI. are Arm-
strong, Trumble, and Graham-(Melb.), Saunders (Carlton),
and Stuckey (East).”
* * * *
Seventh round of Sydney senior grade cricket
ended Saturday. Bats had'a good time. Vic. Trumper
(213 not out), for Paddington, topped the doings. Oppos-
ing 11 had only made 227, hence Trumper nearly accoun-
ted for the whole side himself. Sydney squelched
Central Cumberland by close on 100, and Redfern broke
its long spell of non-success this season through triumph-
ing over Leichhardt-Balmain. North Sydney Closed its
innings at 6 for 351, and, getting Burwood out .for 154,
won by 197. Apropos Trumper’s big total, it was his first
century in club cricket so far this season—previous being
94. A year ago he notched 260 not out for Paddington
against Waverley.

Crack svvimmer Fred Lane has cut his. “per-
manent ” retirement short. Six weeks up country made
him a new man, and ’tis announced now that he’ll be in
the thick of it again ere long.
* • * •
Q. people thought they had in young Frank
Gailey (son of a Brisbane solicitor) a rod in pickle for boy
“ Dick ” Cavill, who visited the Northern State solus to
swim for the local sprint supremacy on Wednesday last
week. On what Dick previously did in public the
Bananalander’s prospects looked at least fair, but the all-
distances Australasian top-notcher rose to the occasion in
great style, and put up quite the best time (1 2 1-5) ever
achieved by him over the 100yds. journey. Gailey and
Hogan (both ex-champions) were second and third
respectively—the former 4yds. away. Australasian best
(1.1 3-5) stands opposite “ Mud” Bishop’sijname, and was
created on a straightway course at Cockatoo Island
(Sydney) in ’9B. Present scribe doesn’t take much stock
in this record, as it was accomplished with a gale of wind
behind, and is away ahead of anything done by the same
man before or since. Further evidence of “Dick’s”
exceptional form jtist now comes in the fact that Satur-
day last saw him, at Brisbane, equal Fred Lane’s world’s
fastest for 120yd5.—1.16 3-5.
* * * •
N.S.W. coursing season opened Saturday at
Belmore (Sydney). Belmore Park Club ran off three
stakes. Weather was far too hot and ground much too
hard. Dogs suffered severely, one or two seriously.
Enthusiast Jack Rowan supplied all the winners—the
biggest (Pupil) only having been put in to fill up through
a disappointment. Arrangements for preventing ringing-
in, doping, &c., were up to date, and a stipendiary
steward was in evidence for the first time, noting dis-
tinctive marks, &c. Most hounds were a heap off in con-
dition. Pratt’s slipping was a revelation to many, and
easily the best seen in Sydney for years.
v * * *
R.S.Y.S. Gascoigne Cup was sailed for on
Saturday by Isea and three 30-footers, Aoma, Heather
and Petrel. A fresh nor’-easter and a good sea “out-
u cll ” ® u PP lied a fair test. Masterful handling by
Stan ’ Stevens brought Aoma home the second year in
succession. As conditions demand that Oup shall be won
three times straight off, Aoma’s prospects look rosy.

Latest re sculler Towns r efforts to make Canuck
Gaudaur line-up for world-supremacy is that Jake still

Yankee mail tells that Shoalhaven (N.S.W.)
sculler, Jim Wray, was in Boston (U.S.) last month with
poor prospects, as expected position as coach at Harvard
arsity wasn’t available. Had Jim whipped Towns
things might have been different.
• * * •
Vic. Chess Championship was this year won by
R. L. Hodgson, a city accountant, and (after Esling, and
possibly young Watson) the State’s strongest player. He
thus becomes first holder of S’malkum M‘Eacharn’s £25
Challenge Cup, said Cup having to be won twice running
or thrice in 10 years to be annexed outright. Only six
competed, Hodgsdn winning easily. The Melb. Chess
Club, which managed the tourney, also ran what it called
a Junior Championship, for players under second-class,
the most noticeable feature about which was the charm-
ing unanimity with which most of the competitors with-
drew directly they had sustained a couple of defeats.
These young chessists are as proud and careful of their
alleged reputations as a young mother of her first baby—
and with just as much reason.

At Burwood C.C. 10-miles Road Race, held
on Saturday last, Red Bird riders finished first and
second over the line. There were 93 starters—largest
number ever started in a N.S.W. road event.

“Kiwi,” from Maoriland, re the Federal
Commission, now sitting (and which is on its way
to Australia) • ■'
. . . „
ootts."
in the larger centres, where the bulk of the testimony,
being chiefly that of manufacturers and trades-unionists
who oppose Federation, has been distinctly unfavorable.
Generally speaking, the farmers favor Unity, and most of
the witnesses who desire its consummation have been
farmers. Curiously enough, the Commissioners passed
through Oamaru and Timaru without taking evidence,
though public opinion is more pronounced at these
Them hal P Wn anywhere else in M.L.
inconsequence. Of course, my conjecture mav tewrong
—I hope it is—but I have a strong suspicion that the
Commissioners are generally hostile; in fact, those of
them who are M’s.P. declared themselves disunionists
long before the Commission was set up. Politicians take
sides here on the question quite irrespective of party
lines, but the most pronounced disunionist at heart is the
Premier. The idea that Maoriland is governed by the
People is all humbug. Seddon has been our autocrat for
years, and he is so now to an extent undreamt of before.
If by any means he could be “ squared,” say by the pro-
raise of a big office (perhaps King) under (that is to say,
over) the Commonwealth, I have no doubt that the nuptial
knot could be tied in a very short time.
At Poverty Point.
•' “ Histrio” to The Bulletin, from London A turn
round some London theatres. Drury Lane, “ The Price
of Peace ” —all mounting, with acting equal to a back-
block show, always excepting old-man Neville. “ Herod,”
superbly mounted and dressed, but Tree very disap-
pointing. He is essentially a picture-making actor; make-
up, looks, attitudes, very . artistic and telling ; but his
elocution terribly monotonous, lecture-ish, and unim-
passioned. Rignold would have struck fire where Tree
was wooden. Curtains were coldly taken and calls were
outrageously faked. The Lewis Waller “ Three Muske-
teers ” was a well-rehearsed show, not nearly as well
mounted as Williamson’s, and Lily Hanbury is no better
than Henrietta Watson. Forbes Robertson, at
Peekham, in Bernard Shaw’s “Devil’s Disciple”
was a treat. F. B. very reminiscent of Scot
Inglis, but Scot had the finer face and voice,
though the Englishman’s magnificent voice can be sub-
dued to light, humorous touches unknown to Scot. The
play—cleverly Shaw-esque—went well, and one line, “ I
never expect a soldier to think,” brought cheers from the
alleged military-loving Britishers. Also saw F.B. in
“ Hamlet”—an ideal performance in many respects. ■ He
retains Fortinbras, dies in the King’s chair, and is carried
off on the soldiers’ shields. He has since married his
clever little leading lady, Gertrude Elliot, sister to the
massive Maxine. Charles Hawtrey, in “ The Message
from Mars,” is the perfection of the unconscious—unlike
anybody in Australia. Seems to think his part more than
say it. Titheradge scores as the impressive Messenger
hoisted skyward. Saw Mrs. Pat Campbell in “ Mr. and
Mrs. Daventry”—a very ordinary drama on the fleshly-
husband-and-spirituelle-wife motive. Her personality is
tender and clingsome, awakening pity and the desire to
protect her, as does her voice—low, sweet and searching.
Her emotion is genuine, but in “ Magda” she must have
been Nance O’Neil’s antithesis. “Mrs. Dane’s Defence ”
at Wyndham’s has a fine act where the woman-liar is
bowled out by the lawyer. Wyndham is the finest actor
I have seen—he brought tears with a look, and his stage
management is sharp-cut—every move an idea. Noticed
at the farcical comedy, “ In the Soup,” that the British
public stands “broad” lines and business that Australia
would not tolerate in second-rate variety shows.

Walter Raynham has four pretty young daughters ap-
pearing together in a song-and-dance turn at the London
halls, under the title of “ The Four Trees.”
Haddon Chambers’s new play, “ The Awakening,” deals
mainly with the emotions of a hero who allowed his
moral nature to lie dormant whilst he was having a lively
time. Sounds as if suggested by “ A Message from Mark.”
“ Melb.” : Went to a popular Saturday-night entertain-
ment much patronised by pious people who object to
the wicked theatre. Here were items on the programme
as oerulean as at London Pav. First, the inevitable bed-
post. A young lady sang about a married couple having
twins, and so there was no room in the bed. Second,
another young lady sang about “ The Shadow on the
Blind,” wherefore he had to marry the girl. Third, one
of these young ladies sang “ Wliat’s the use of a pair of
trousers without a man inside?” Fourth, a gentleman
sang about a lady sitting on a tack and thinking it was a
bee.
“Melb.” : Vic. Board of Health iscommendablyexigent
as to the exits of new buildings. Old ones go as they
please. Attending the Pleasant Sunday Afternoon at
Wesley Church, I am horrified to notice how long it takes
a crowd to get out. Apparently half-an-hour. What
about fire ? The Lonsdale-st. porch of St. Francis’s R.C.
church ought to be absolutely broken down and removed.
It’s a death-trap ! Do we want a Santiago ? These are
only samples of the old places. Inasmuch as the Bijou
will be unoccupied soon, let me emphasise its extreme
danger ; it is all upstairs.
J.A.C. :, A correspondent (16/2/’l) takes exception to
your statement that Arthur Sullivan was of Jewish
origin. I did not know the late composer personally, but
I knew his brother Fred, the original judge in “ Trial by
Jury,” (“and a good judge, too,”) and he was of most
pronounced Jewish type. In the early ’7o’s he and
D’Oyley Carte ran a musical and dramatic agency over
Carte’s, senior, musical-instrument shop, at Charing
Cross, London.
Dear Bulletin, —Desiring to witness the crowds that
the London papers said were reading the bulletins of the
Queen’s condition, I went to Buckingham Palace the
evening before her death. With some difficulty I found
the little slip of paper signed “ Arthur Balfour,” in front
of which was the “ loyal crowd ” —consisting of two dirty
East-Enders and an old lady selling matches. The old
lady asked me for a penny, and the East-Enders went off
singing a pantomime chorus. Thoroughly disillusioned,
I spent the evening searching for “ loyalty.” I went back
to the Strand, and turned into the Alhambra. The papers
had spoken of the illness “casting a gloom over audi-
ences,” &c., but I never saw these magnificently put on
ballets played with more vim—to a packed and delighted
house. Next day- the day of the death —I saw opposite
the Mansion House bulletin about a tenth of the crowd
that would be around an Argus cricket announcement;
and a peep into the Exchange revealed only much furious
pencilling and whispering among the excited bell-toppers.
I found some loyalty among the Strand drapers, who dis-
played whole windows full of evening-shirts with black
ribbons across. Some, I was informed, were quite sold
out of black ties and gloves the previous afternoon, two
days before need arose. Also the stationers showed piles
of mourning notepaper. On the evening of the death I
visited a suburban music halt, and two “ turns ” had been
done when the music fizzled out, and the stage manager
came on and announced the news, and the consequent
“ having to close.” A drunk in the stalls uttered a very
audible “Hooray,” and no one jumped on him. The
audience filed out, and at the bar a portly evening-dress
wearer remarked, “ Well, we had a drunk on Mafeking
night, let’s have one now.” Meantime, outside, round
the stage door, the minor artists were wondering how
many nights’ salary they were going to lose.— Stroller.

VICE-REGAL JEWELLF9S,
Illingworth, leader of Westralia’s Opposi-
tion, announces a shadowy democratic policy, and
proposes to fight Premier Throssell thereon. He
advocates one man one vote, and an equitable dis-
tribution of seats, but the rest is mostly fog and
generalities. Illingworth holds that “ our loans
must be put on a sound financial basis ”; also,
that “our revenue and expenditure must have
most careful attention.” He says that agricul-
tural development “demands close attention,”
and that Old-Age Pensions and about a dozen
other questions also “require attention.” The
man whose policy is mainly a long list of things
that require attention is an unsatisfactory person.
The two great troubles of this world are the things
which haven’t been attended to and the things
that were attended to the wrong way.
In one of its wilder moments Sydney
Telegraph, of 6th March, published a list setting
forth that in certain articles (clothes, boots, hats,
cement, butter, candles, cheese, hops, bacon, con-
densed milk, nails, vinegar, blankets, soap, and
starch) which come free into N.S.W., but are
taxed in Victoria, N.S.W. manufactures are so
backward that the State has to import £2,201,000
worth annually. And, on the other hand, Vic-
toria is so much further advanced in manufactures
that it only needs to import £312,000. The im-
porters’ paper complained that these manufac-
tures, which found so much employment for the
people, are really a curse because they make so
much less imports for the Federal Government to
tax and get revenue from. The trouble is that if
the Federal Government taxes these imports for
revenue purposes the imports will largely cease, as
they have done in Victoria, And if it lets them
go free, so as to - keep up the blessed im-
ports and keep down the sinful local manufac-
tures, then it can’t get revenue. Therefore, a
certain amount of confusion supervenes. The
theory seems to be this : —
If you tax imports, imports diminish as shown by the
horrible example of Victoria.
If you don’t tax them they increase, as shown by the
blessed example of N.S.W.
Therefore by not taxing imports there are lots of im-
ports for the Federal Government to tax for revenue pur-
poses.
By this process it appears that it is advisable, not
to eat your cake so that you may have it in order
that you may eat it so that you won’t have it.
Some of the Victorian Judges have lucid
intervals. Judge Williams recently .had before
him a man who couldn’t find the bail required for
his re-appearance, and who seemed likely to spend
his remand in gaol. Prisoner then suggested to
the Judge that he should be permitted out on
condition that he reported himself daily at a cer-
tain hour at the Detective Office ; and his Honor
actually accepted the proposal, and let the man
out on these terms.
Dear Bulletin. —The “Man from Eng-
land ” (8., 23/2/1) may be right, or wrong, in his
allegations against the Gordon Highlanders ; but
the fact remains that the corps has, up” to date,
gained five V.C’s. in the Boer war —the record for
the campaign.— A Mon frae Scotlan’.

“ Melb.”: Demise, Scott, the chief second-
hand book-selling expert ; almost the only one,
for Cole took, up the line late in life. There is
only room for one Scott in Melb. nowadays.
Formerly Dwight and Bennett were the rivals.
Dwight left a lot of money to Melb. University.
Bennett could Stay as long as he liked in London
buying books for his clever wife to sell. Their
twin sons are familiar Melbourne citizens.
The name of vanished lawyer Grey
cropped up again at Melb. Law Courts last week
in an appeal case involving one of Grey’s little
alleged deficiencies —a trifle of £lO,OOO or so.
In this case Attorney-General Isaacs appeared for
a defendant who had not had the misfortune to be
a partner or a co-trustee with Grey aforesaid.
But the question as to why the Vic. Crown jLaw
department didn’t issue a warrant for Grey’s
arrest is still unanswered.
The other night a contingent 300 strong
from the notorious Freeman-street, “ Crutchy ”
and “ Rats ” pushes of Fitzroy and Carlton
(Melb.) marched over to Brunswick to attack the
local commandoes. On the way they tore off
pickets and palings wherever handy, and broke
every street-lamp along the line of march. On a
vacant block of land in the centre of Brunswick
the battle raged fiercely, while the citizens raced
all over the place for the police. These eventually
arrived and captured two small boys, who were
next day duly fined 20s.
E. D. Millen, N.S.W. Freetrade candi-
date for the Senate:
The adoption of Freetrade in N.S.W. had not been fol-
lowed by that 'avalanche of disaster which they were
told would accompany it. . . . As freetraders who
had an active, live faith in their creed, they believed that
a Freetrade policy would prove of as much benefit to
Victoria as it had to N.S.W.
What a fine political three-card shark and confi-
dence-man the average Freetrade orator is,
to be sure! The person who can describe
as Freetrade Reid’s mongrel policy of heavy
Protection on beer, wine, sugar, tobacco, cigars,
cigarettes, biscuits, confectionery, newspapers,
&c., combined with the creating of artificial
prosperity by the heaviest borrowing in Aus-
tralasia, is indeed a daisy. Isn’t there one man
among all the unspeakable gang of political
impostors who call themselves the N.S.W. Free-
trade party who has the courage to propose Free-
trade or real Revenue-Tariffism ?
Telegram last week from Maoriland, the
State with a strongly democratic Government and
almost the heaviest Protectionist tariff, in Austra-
lasia : .
The Postmaster-General (Mr. J. G. Ward), speaking last
night, said that last vear remissions of taxation had been
made to the extent of £325,000, yet the revenue for the
11 months of the present year amounted to £5,112,200, as
against £4,859,700 last year, an increase of £252,000, with
another month to go. He ventured to prophesy that at
the end of the present financial year the surplus would
amount to £400,000. The finances of the colony bad
never stood better. There was a plethora of money in
New Zealand to-day, such as never before existed in its
history. The income-tax to February this year should
show an increase, compared with 1892 and 1893, of
£105,000, and by the end of March it was calculated that
the increase would amount to £159,000.
To quote the words of a lecturer who dropped in
at The Bulletin office the other day, after jour-
neying through Maoriland : “You can’t see any
poverty over there. The people look as if they
had more money than they want.” Ruined by
Protection, no doubt.
One thing now seems certain about that
big divorce-case : that when all is over somebody
or other will be in gaol for either forgery or
perjury.
From an M.L. contingenter’s letter :
It is fascinating work this man-hunting, especially as
we have orders to take no prisoners, but to shoot first and
challenge afterwards. Nor are we to take notice of the
white flag, as the Boers have abused the use of it so often.
According to a returned M.L. trooper :
There were times when the class of warfare waged was
somewhat barbarous. Prisoners were not desired, and
consequently the men were ordered not to hamper them-
selves with' them unless a departure from that course
would involve cold-blooded slaughter. .
Either this (and many other returned swaddies)
are arrant liars, or
From a M.L. contingenter’s letter :
One day a cablegram from Seddon to Kitchener was
read on parade. It was a favourable reply to a request
that we should remain and have numbers made up, but
it was very unfavourably received, and 001. Cradock and
Major Jowsey replied to that effect. Most of the men
hate this country, and would prefer M.L. at a pound a
week to £4 a week here ; in fact, there is no encourage-
ment to remain. When vacancies among_ the officers
occur they are made up by men from M.L., instead of by
promotions of capable men who haved served through the
war.
History will yet contrast, in fitting colours, the
heroic patriotism of Seddon, sitting in his arm-
chair, and sending words of cheer to
Kitchener from far-off Maoriland, and the un-
worthy response made by those whom he had sent
forth to do battle for the Empire.
N.S.W. ex-District Court Judge M'Far-
land (he was 30 years ago on the W.A. Bench)
died on Monday afternoon at Sydney, aged 77.
N.S. Wales has thus lost two veteran pen-
sioned minor judges within a week.
The Federal Govt, has leased, as offices,
the premises in Macquarie-street, Sydney, erst-
while occupied by the old Reform Club—the
famous institution which “ drank itself out of
debt.” The Bulletin sincerely hopes that Mr.
Barton and his colleagues will take whatever
steps may be necessary to lay the ghost of old
Jack Robertson, which still sits in its accustomed
place, in a big chair just inside the front window
of the ground floor.
The brilliantly-stupid E. D Millen, of
N.S.W. House of Permanent Fossils, at New-
castle :
The fact that after 35 years of Protection the manufac-
turers of Victoria still demanded to be protected was to
his mind the strongest argument of the fallacy of Protec-
tion.
What a mind E. D. Millen must have ! Victoria
has tried about 25 years of Protection, and is so
satisfied that that policy gives better wages and
cheaper living that it wants to continue it. That
proves Protection is BAD. Britain has had Free-
trade for about 50 years, and Millen says it is so
satisfied with it that it wants to continue it.
That proves Freetrade is GOOD. The fact that
Victoria, after giving Protection a serious trial,
finds it a success and wants to keep it clearly
shows, to Milieu’s mind, that Protection is ruinous.
But if Victoria, after giving Protection a serious
trial, found it a failure and wanted to abandon it,
that would also prove, according to Millen’s mind,
that it was ruinous. Millen ought to sell a mind
like that. It is a dreadful thing for any man to
have about him.

Federal House of j|epresentatives
To the Electors of
The Melbourne Electorate.
GENTLEMEN—
I have the honor to solicit your vote and interest, and,
should you elect me as your representative, I shall give my active support to the
following measures :
(1) The Tariff Policy of the Barton Ministry.
(2) White Australia, reserving to Queensland Sugar Planters the right to
employ, as long as necessary, colored labor, on condition that such labor is returned
to lts na five shores at stated times, and under no circumstances is allowed to settle
in the Commonwealth.
(3) Federal Defences. Adequate maintenance of the fortifications and
defence forces of the Commonwealth. Increase of citizen soldiers and rifle clubs.
Any increased expenditure necessary for these purposes to be met out of revenue
and not by means of loans.
(4) Industrial Conciliation and Arbitration on lines that are fair alike to
employers and employed.
(5) Old Ago Pensions subject to conditions mentioned in my public
addresses. J r
(6) Uniform Factory Acts for the Commonwealth.
(7) Nationalization of the State Railways by the Commonwealth on
condition that the status of employes is not lowered.
(8) Federal Territory not to be alienated. The size to be considerably
increased beyond the 100 square miles provided in the Commonwealth Bill, and the
site to be within easy access of a seaboard.
(9) Federal Public Works. All unproductive Public Works in connection
with the Federal Capital, the Post Office, Customs, and Defence Department to be
paid out of Revenue and not from Loans which will be a burthen on future
generations.
I am, Gentlemen,
YOUR OBEDIENT SERVANT,
M. D. McEacharn.
Central Committee Rooms : Stock Exchange, Melbourne.
Ist March, 1901.

WHY WE ADVERTISE!
Because Advertising Is the legitimate means vouchsafed to enlighten people
to acquaint each other with their avocations and wants. If a man has a
Speciality, and has devoted years of his life and expended large sums of money in studying that Speciality and in
purchasing the necessary appliances, etc., for treating certain diseases, it would be absurd for him not to announce
the fact, and disseminate tne knowledge universally. We have no apology to offer for informing the world through
this advertisement and otherwise of the means of relief we can afford to sufferers who need our help. Therefore,
however opposed to your own preconceived ideas and to those of your medical advisers, our method of treatment may
he the fact still remains that it is successful, and this is verified by the thousands of testimonials received from
grateful patients. We make no secret of our mode of practice. We only claim to have utilised in a common-sense
way the result of scientific research and long experience. If you listen to the counsel of your sceptical friends and
jealous physician you may omit consulting us, but remember if you allow the disease to ryji riot in your system, you
are guilty of culpable negiect, and are alone responsible for your future pitiful condition.
Nine Diplomas and Certificates of Qualification and Registration by Government
Medical Boards of New South Wales, Victoria, south Australia,
Great Britain and America Open to Inspection.
Late Consulting Staff, Homoeopathic Hospital,
Late Medical Staff, British Army.
Melbourne;
home treatment
-If you live out of town and cannot visit us, this need not deprive you
of the benefits of our wonderful treatment, because in the new system
of combining medicine and electricity a complete Electro-Medical home treatment is provided for. Scarcely a day
passes that the news does not reach us of some wonderful home cure of a person living so far away that a personal
visit could not be made. Write a plain statement of your condition, explaining your feelings and symptoms as clearly
as possible, and from it we will be able to accurately diagnose your case. It only costs you the trouble of writing,
and twopence postage to get the opinion and advice of Australia’s Leading Specialists.
DO NOT BE DRUGGED TO DEATH BY QUACKS
When there are so many quacks and impudent pretenders in the field of Medicine, who arrogate to them-
selves the monstrosity of giving counsel and advice in matters of which they are profoundly ignorant, and who
have nothing to recommend them but their audacity, sufferers cannot be too careful whom they consult.
Although liberty of choice belongs to all, discrimination is necessary. In consulting THE FREEMAN AND'
WALLACE ELECTRO-MEDICAL AND SURGICAL INSTITUTE patients may feel assured that they shall
receive the advice of those Btted by superior skill and education, the result of special study. Our reputation and.
unparalleled success as Specialists entitle us to your confidence.

Public Notices.
“ WOODITB.”
TT having come to the knowledge of the Woodite Oom-
pany of Mitcham, Surrey, England, that some persons
and manufacturers have been making spurious rings,
Sheeting, packings and Woodite gauge glass rings, and
selling the same as “ Woodite,"
NOTICE is hereby given that any infringement or
piracy of the trade mark “ Woodite ” will be proceeded
against, and persons are warned not to purchase or Ideal
with the same. All Woodite rings and moulded goods
have the name “ Woodite ” cast on them ; none others
are genuine.
A reward for information will be paid on conviction of
any person or persons pirating or infringing the trade
mark “ Woodite.”
WOODITE COMPANY,
January 21st, 1901. Mitcham, Surrey, England.
Government Notices.
Department of Public Works,
Sydney, 27th February, 1901.
DEVONSHIRE STREET CEMETERY.
I\JOTICE is hereby given that the Department of Public
Works is CREATING a NEW CEMETERY at near
LA PEROUSE in a beautiful position for the RECEPTION
of the UNCLAIMED BODIES, and other property now
lying within the DEVONSHIRE STREET CEMETERY.
In this new cemetery the remains will be reverentially
re-interred, and all existing monuments, tombstones,
headstones, footstones, and iron railings re-erected over
the respective graves by skilled workmen.
Owing to the greater portion of the stone work and
iron railing now in the Devonshire Street Cemetery being
unsuitable for admission into any of the other Metropoli-
tan Cemeteries (except the Presbyterian Division of the
Necropolis), persons who have already made application
for translation to other cemeteries may, if satisfied to
leave the work in the hands of the Department, withdraw
their applications with the assurance that their interests
will be safeguarded in every way compatible with rever-
ence, decency, and skill.
The new cemetery will eventually be connected by
tram, and is now connected by good roads. It will also
be beautified and kept up in an orderly manner.
ROBT. HICKSON,
9 c 21 Under Secretary for Public Works, and
Commissioner for Roads.

Federal Elections.
THE ELECTORS OF THE STATE
OP
New South Wales.
Gentlemen, —
I beg to state that I am a candidate for election to
the Senate of the Commonwealth. Many electors have
represented to me that the experience gained in my 34
years’ connection with the railways of New South Wales
in different positions, latterly as Traffic Manager, would
be of value in dealing with the Inter-State Commission
and with the Federation of the Railways of Australia,
which must be the question of the near future. Care
must be taken whilst guarding the financial interests
of the parent State, that its commercial interests,
especially with reference to our chief port, Sydney, are
also safe-guarded. If elected, I would give the present
Government an opportunity of bringing forward its
measures. It is plain that whatever Government is in
power must bring in a tariff sufficient to raise at least
eight millions of money. I am a Protectionist. There
are industries in our midst which require fostering. Most
prominent amongst them is the iron industry. We have
in New South Wales the very best of iron oresin unlimited
quantities, coal, lime, and every article necessary for
making the best of iron and steel. Surely this is an in-
dustry (and it is only one of many) worth placing on its
feet. Should I gain the confidence of the Electors I can
promise faithfully and conscientiously to discharge the
duties required of me.
Yours faithfully,
W. VERO READ.
North Sydney, 6th March, 1901.
JJJLECTORS OE QUEENSLAND.
I beg to announce myself as a Candidate for the Senate
in the Commonwealth Parliament. Owing to the limited
time available, it is impossible to explain my views in all
the chief centres of population. During my twenty years’
service in the Queensland Parliament I have ever striven
to foster the interests of my Electorate and of Queens-
land, and that my efforts have been appreciated seems to
be shown by the fact of my continuous return.
If I am elected, while I shall consider it my duty to
watch the interests of the State, as I realise that under its
provisions “State rights” will demand constant and in-
telligent supervision, I shall nevertheless not forget that
the interests of all Australia are in the keeping of the
Commonwealth Parliament.
1. If elected I will advocate and support the creation
of a system that will secure to the Commonwealth an
adequate land and sea defence by means of citizen sol-
diers and sailors.
2. The adoption of a revenue tariff that will produce
sufficient for the requirements of the Commonwealth and
of the States without destroying existing industries.
3. I am in favour of the exclusion of Asiatic and African
races, and am opposed to the employment of alien labour
but in regard to the sugar industry I believe in leaving
the settlement of the Kanaka problem to the people of
this State. It is pre-eminently a State matter, and the
people of Queensland may be trusted to do what is right.
4. I believe in an old age pension scheme, and, in fact,
in all measures of a liberal and progressive character.
If elected, I am prepared to give the present Ministry a
fair triai; but lam pledged to no party. I shall look
more at the policy than at the politician.
JOHN HAMILTON.

E D EE A L
Elections.
EL EOT lONS.
The Senate.
To The Electors of Queensland.
Fellow Electors, —Having represented the electors of an
important State constituency in the Assembly since 1896,
I feel that I am not unknown to the public, and therefore
offer myself as a candidate for a seat in the Senate of the
Commo: wealth.
The future prosperity of the people of this vast Com-
monwealth will be largely dependent upon the intelli-
gence, the wisdom, and the patriotism shown by its re-
presentatives in Parliament.
It will also be the duty of our representatives to guard
carefully the control and administration of the internal
affairs of the Commonwealth.
Amongst the first duties to be undertaken will be the
determination of the future fiscal policy of Australia, the
immediate settlement of which should not be delayed. It
therefore behoves electors to assure themselves of the
fiscal faith of every candidate seeking election.
Although a Protectionist, I consider that a modified
tariff could be arranged which, besides bringing in a
revenue, would prevent the free influx of cheap labor pro-
ductions, while at the same time assisting the agricultural
and manufacturing interests.
The large Customs revenue that will be necessarily
raised, and the undesirability of having to resort to any
form of direct taxation, renders the exercise of economy
in the administration of the Commonwealth finances of
paramount importance.
I favour the exclusion of all "coloured races, but that
sugar be produced under present conditions pending the
appointment of a Royal Commission, who shall investi-
gate and report as to the advisability or otherwise of em-
ploying Polynesians in the canefields. It is not the
Kanakas (who are decreasing in number), but the other
coloured races, that are a menace to Australia. The
white citizen workers of Australia should demand their
prompt and absolute prohibition.
I consider that a scheme should be initiated without
delay for the consolidation and conversion of the debts
of the States, and so attain the ideal of one people with
one interest and one debt.
Considering that our frozen beef, mutton, &c., suffers
great deterioration on the Thames, I will advocate better
methods of handling, storing, and distributing our pro-
ducts in Europe, and that a central depot for Australia
be established in London.
I will assist in formulating a policy for developing the
interior of our vast continent by railway communication.
I consider the defence of Australia requires our deep
consideration, and, therefore, consider it is necessary
that a Council of Advice should be established between
the home Government and her dependencies for the
better distribution, arrangement, or equipment of forces,
and who would advise in any matter connected directly
or indirectly with defence.
I favor the establishment of old-age pensions. Pending
legislation, the operation of the system in different parts
of the world should be carefully watched, as many valu-
able lessons will be afforded for the guidance of the Com-
monwealth Parliament when dealing, as eventually it
must, with the subject.
I am a believer in conciliation and arbitration, and also
adult suffrage. As it will not be possible, in the limited
time available before the date fixed for the elections, for
me to visit more than a few centres of population, I must
rely upon my past record in public life commending my
candidature to the electors of my State.
Should you honor me with your support, it will be my
aim to assist all Liberal, Democratic, and Progressive
measures for the promotion of the interests of Australia
and its people, irrespective of class, creed, or conditions.
Yours obediently,
JOHN BARTHOLOMEW.
O THE ELE 0 T O R~S
OF
NEW SOUTH WALES.
Gentlemen, —I beg to offer myself as a Candidate for a
seat in the SENATE of the COMMONWEALTH PAR-
LIAMENT.
lam a native of NEW SOUTH WALES, and keenly
alive to the interests of the STATE, and would resist to
the utmost any attempt to deprive us of our just rights.
<■ DEFENCE.
Recent events have proved that it is quite impossible
for any Minister for Defence, with his many political
duties and responsibilities, to be thoroughly conversant
with the administration of the Military Forces, conse-
quently many mistakes have been made and sinecure
offices created ; these I wish to see remedied and abolished
at the earliest possible moment, and no time is more op-
portune than the present, when the forces of the different
States are being blended together in one Commonwealth
Army. I regret to say that there are already indications
of a movement, in at least one of the other States, to
supersede not only the General and Permanent Staff, but
the whole of the New South Wales Forces. Any endeavors
in this direction require to be firmly opposed. lam of
opinion that much extravagance has been displayed in
the past, and greater efficiency could be gained at less
expenditure. lam a strong advocate of the principle of
a Partially-Paid Force, which was inaugurated by General
Richardson, an officer who has done so much for New
South Wales, and whose system has become a model for
the rest of Australia. I have had many years’ experience
in the Defence Forces, having commanded the Mounted
Rifles for a period of 9 years, and served in the Imperial
Army for 13 years. I am, therefore, in a position to
recognise the weak spots in our system.
TARIFF.
I am opposed to the imposition a TARIFF which would
ENRICH bounty-fed manufacturers of the STATE of
VICTORIA, preferring duties which would be mainly
REVENUE BEARING, having due regard to the interests
of the FARMERS and PRODUCERS. I am strongly in
favor of Reciprocal Trade between Great Britain and her
Colonies, and am prepared to vote for the granting of
Preferential Duties to British Manufactures, in return for
favored treatment of AUSTRALIAN WOOL and PRO-
DUCE.
I regret it will be impossible for me to personally visit
you to further explain my views, as I am under orders to
leave for South Africa.
If elected to the honorable position as one of the Rep-
resentatives of NEW SOUTH WALES in the SENATE,
my services on my return to Australia at the conclusion
of the War will be at your complete disposal.
I have the honor to be,
Gentlemen,
Your obedient servant,
H. B. LASSETTER.
Sydney, March 7th, 1901.

Federal
FEDERAL SENATE ELECTION.
TO THE ELECTORS OF NEW SOUTH
\ T^Tpa
Fellow Citizens, —
As the Candidates seieuieu uy tne Political Labor
League to carry the banner of Labor in the coming con-
test for the Federal Senate, we propose to lay briefly
before you our views upon the leading Federal questions
of the day.
THE FISCAL QUESTION.
While regarding the Fiscal Question as of minorimport-
ance, yet as papers and politicians have combined to force
it to the front we would at once state our position in
regard to it.
To raise Revenue through the Customs, as both Barton
and Reid propose, means imposing upon New South
Wales a burden of a million and a half more taxation than
she now pays, and all this that the other States may keep
up their present expenditures without having recourse to
direct taxation. Seeing that the States retain their lands
and railways, we refuse to admit that they have any
right thus to overweight N.S.W. through the Federal
Treasury. The Federal Government has quite enough to
do to set its own financial house in order. If Barton were
an honest Protectionist and Reid an honest Freetrader,
instead of being the revenue-tariffists their policies pro-
claim both to be, they would advocate a certain measure
of direct taxation, to mitigate the burden a high revenue
tariff is upon the great masses of the community.
As the Tariff Question is, however, a minor one to us
compared to Industrial Peace, to a White Australia, to
the Abolition of Minority Rule in the Senate, which
stands in the way of the fusion of the peoples in the
several States into one Australian nation, we would vote
with perfect impartiality for any leader, no matter what
his fiscal policy, who would give us the largest measure of
these reforms.
WHITE AUSTRALIA.
Not fisealism, but a White Australia, is the all-important
question. Coming from the stock we do, with the quality
and variety of soil and climate and extent of territory we
possess, we have the opportunity of making our country
one of the first among the nations of the earth. One of
the first essentials is to keep it white. The Asiatic and
Kanaka must be excluded, and at once ; and not only
these, but all other inferior races. In many parts of the
continent the Chinese, the Japanese, and the Hindoos are
an even greater menace than the Kanaka. They lower
our standard of living, they lower our moral tone, and
they cannot be assimilated with our race. No considera-
tion of trade or of treaty should be allowed to stand in
the way of their exclusion. We are satisfied the British
Government will realise this to be our national duty, but
in any event we should do so.
CONSTITUTIONAL REFORM.
Each citizen of the Commonwealth, wherever situated,
has an equal interest with any other upon all matters
that the Commonwealth has jurisdiction upon. But, as
the Constitution stands, should he live in Tasmania, he
has ten times the representation in the Senate—through
which all laws must pass—that he would have if he
lived in New South Wales. This at once destroys majority
rule —the sheet anchor of Democracy—and perpetuates
that narrow provincialism it should be our aim to abolish.
Mr. Barton now tells us the Constitution is in danger.
Before the Constitution was adopted he was one of the
loudest in proclaiming his readiness to trust the people.
Surely if the people could be trusted to decide the ques-
tion of adopting it, they can be trusted to decide if any
amendment is necessary. We are prepared to leave such
a question to the people every time.
COMPULSORY ARBITRATION.
Industrial Peace is essential to the progress and de-
velopment of the nation. No reasonable measure of this
can be secured except by legislation. We therefore stand
for Compulsory Arbitration.
NAVIGATION AND SHIPPING LAWS.
Navigation and Shipping Laws will receive our earnest
attention, and we will exert ourselves to secure encourage-
ment to Australian shipping by the enactment of laws
preventing unfair competition from foreign subsidised
and manned vessels.
NATIONAL BANK.
We strongly favor the establishment of a National
Bank, with the sole right of note issue. This would
minimise the danger of banking crises and reconstruc-
tions, as well the power of private institutions to thwart
progressive legislation to benefit private interests. The
profits arising from the note issue alone would be very
considerable.
COINAGE.
We claim for the Commonwealth the right of Coinage
of all the metals in our currency. The profits on silver
alone amount to a very large sum, and should belong to
the Commonwealth.
ADULT SUFFRAGE.
We are firm believers in Adult Suffrage. It can never
be truly claimed that our laws are “ broad based upon
the people’s will ” while women are debarred from having
an equal voice with men in the creation of laws.
MILITARISM.
We are strongly opposed to the establishment of a
Standing Army. The history of the older countries of the
world proves such a system to be a danger to the liberties
of the people, and a crushing burden upon their indus-
trial progress.
In the wide expanse of ocean that separates us from
the rest of the world, with the spirit and valor of Austra-
lian sons, we can with a Citizen Soldiery and with a rea-
sonable expenditure upon the naval defences rest secure
against foreign aggression.
TRANSFERRED SERVICES.
There is danger that some of the Representatives from
some of the States, where the members of the Civil Ser-
vice are more poorly paid than with us, may endeavour to
drag wages down rather than level them up. Such an
effort would meet with ■ our strongest opposition, con-
vinced as we are that in all but the top grades at least
our own rates should go up rather than down.
We favor the creation of Appeal Boards for the different
Services, with the right of the men to direct representa-
tion thereon.
We claim, too, for the Civil Servant the right to the
fullest measure of political liberty enjoyed by any other
citizen of the Commonwealth.
FEDERAL CAPITAL.
The site for the Federal Capital should be decided at
the earliest possible moment. The land must remain
the property of the Government for all time. Periodic
re-appraisements should be made, so that the constantly
increasing values put upon the land by the growth of this
City and the development of the nation should be credited
to those who create it.
In conclusion, we would again emphasize our conviction
that not by wrangling over the dry bones of Fisealism,
but by making and keeping this a White Man’s Country ;
by bringing about Industrial Peace ; by educating our
people industrially and commercially to a higher plane ;
by giving greater encouragement to the inventors
amongst us, can sound and satisfactory progress be made.
To those who share such views, and who think that a
leavening of the Federal Parliament with some members
of the Labor Bodies, which in the several different States
have brought about more progressive legislation in ten
years than had been effected in the previous forty, we
confidently appeal for a vote on the day of the Federal
Elections for
Yours faithfully,
SAMUEL SMITH,
DONALD MAGDONELL.

Read the Records of Recovery.
The Case of Mr. S. W. FINNIE,
(For the Sydney Star.)
Mr. Sidney William Finnie made an
appointment for our reporter to call at
No. 32 Bay-street, Ultimo, one evening, so
that he would have plenty of time at his
disposal to furnish a complete account of his
wonderful recovery from a complaint which
had caused himself and relatives a great
amount of anxiety. Mr. Finnie is now a
splendid specimen of manhood, being over
thirteen stone in weight, and with a com-
plexion which testifies to the robust health
he enjoys.
“ You do not look a likely subject to
speak with authority about sickness,” said
the reporter.
“No; but if you had seen me a little
while ago, you would have had a different
opinion,” was the reply. “My direful
illness was brought on in a very simple
manner—through treading on a piece of
glass. That little accident was the pre-
cursor of subsequent complications, from
which my sufferings can barely be expressed.
My foot received a terrible gash, which
necessitated a number of stitches. I lost a
large quantity of blood from the wound, and
had to go about on crutches for six weeks.
When I could dispense with my crutches 1
found myself in a dreadfully weak and
nervous condition. I remember getting an
awful shock one day when passing a house,
with a hearse and mourniDg coaches outside.
It was a place where a friend of mine used
to live, and, on making enquiries, I found
that the poor fellow had died of the plague.
My nerves were incapable of withstanding
such a dreadful shock, and I went home
feeling as cold as ice, and trembling from
head to foot. On arriving at my own place
1 was the colour of a ghost, and I quickly
fainted away. When I recovered conscious-
ness, the pulsation at my wrist was light
and fluttering, and my mind was depressed
and disquieted. My thoughts were in a
state of confusion, and I was totally be-
wildered. I had to obtain relief from my
laborious duties and take to bed, as I was
thoroughly broken down. My face became
pallid, and wore a strange expression, with
the pupils of my eyes dilated and yellow
circles underneath. Sometimes my breath-
ing was very feeble, and my skin was cold
and clammy. When laying down I could
hear my heart beating against my ribs, and
I would then sit up to get a little ease. As
soon as I resumed my former position the
palpitation used to come on again. Short
Btretches of sleep came occasionally, and
then I had terrifying dreams, from which
I awoke with a start in a condition of great
nervous excitement, and while still only
partially awake I had impressions of dimly-
seen objects or imperfectly-heard noises,
which became fresh sources of horrible
images and ideas. It took me some time to
regain my normal balance after these experi-
ences, and 1 was often afraid of my own
shadow. Then my digestion went all wrong.
No matter how light my meals were, the
food obstinately remained heavily on my
Btomach, causing exasperating pains, which
were only relieved when the victuals were
vomited. Before being sick I used to belch
sour gases from the stomach, the taste, of
which was never out of my mouth. Pains
in the back of my neck and under my
shoulders caused me a lot of misery, and my
headaches were a source of great torment.
Fits of dizziness were frequent, and when
sitting on a chair I felt as though it was
going round and round with me. Even-
tually I could not sleep at night, and the
vomiting became more frequent, leaving my
head in a whirl. All my strength and
energy had left me, and I was reduced over
two stone in weight.”
“What did your doctor think of your con*
dition?”
“Apparently he did not know what. to
think, for his treatment was not benefiting
me in any way I was afraid it was the
plague I had, and a number of other folks
were of the same opinion. In fact, when I
at length went to consult another doctor,
the neighbours were grouped together dis-
cussing my sickness, and on my return they
were still there, eagerly waiting to hear the
verdict. To our mutual gratification they
were informed that it was not the dreaded
plague.”
“ Did the second doctor manage to restore
your health ?”
“No ; he was just as unsuccessful as the
first, although he gave ine a great quantity
of physic. I became heartily sick of his
advice and medicine, because there was no
of an improvement. I had lost heart,
and did not expect to pull through my illness.
I was in this state of despondency when I
was brought face to face with Clements
Tonic. A friend sent me half a dozen
bottles, and guaranteed me a cure if I took
them I shall never be able to wipe out my
obligation to that man, for a few bottles of
Clements Tonic completely put me right. A
couple of bottles cured my nervousness and
brought me peaceful sleep. My aches and
pains all went away, and I could soon eat
heartily. In about three weeks I was as well
as you see me now, so it is not necessary to
say any more. I have every reason to be a
strong advocate of Clements Tonic, and you
are welcome to publish my opinion and
statements far and wide.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Sydney William Finnie, 32 Bay-street, Ultimo,
Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have care-
fully read the annexed document, consisting of
three folios and consecutively numbered from
one to three, and that it contains and is a
true and faithtul account of my illness and cure
by Clements Tonic, and also contains my full per-
mission to publish in any way my statements —which
I give voluntarily, without receiving any payment;
and I make this solemn declaration conscientiously
believing the same to be true, and by virtue of the
provisions of an Act made and passed in the ninth
year of the reign of her present Majesty, intituled
“ An Act for the n*,-e effectual abolition of Oaths
and Affirmations taaen and made in the various
Departments of the Government of New South
Wales, and to substitute Declarations in lieu thereof,
and for the suppression of voluntary and Extra-
judicial Oaths and Affidavits."

Declared at Glebe, this third day of September,
one thousand nine hundred, before me,
THOS. I NO. DUNN, J.P.

A salutation speech from the 19th Century
to the 20th Century, taken down in shorthand by
Mark Twain :
I bring- you the stately matron, named Christendom,
returning bedraggled, besmirched and dishonored from
pirate raids in Kiao Chow, Manchuria, South Africa and
the Philippines ; with her soul full of meanness, her
pocket full of boodle, and her mouth full of pious hypocri-
cies. Give her soap and a towel, but hide the looking-
glass.
For pure, perfect casualness go to the Aus-
tralian divorce-courts. A few cases in one recent
day, at Melbourne. One petitioner told the
Court that she was married in ’B9, and that,
having no means, the pair lived with her mother
for 18 months. Then respondent left casually,
and stayed away three years, when he returned
and took up the matrimonial contract for a few
days, then drifted away once more. Six weeks
later, she met him in the street, when he kindly
inquired if she was. working, and, being assured
she was, wandered off again—this time, to get a
drink. Petitioner said she was tired of waiting
for him to finish this very long beer, so she
got her divorce. Another couple had married on
nothing a-year, and gone to live with ma-in-law.
Then hubby went to Sydney for a while, returned,
went away again, returned for a few days, wan-
dered off once more, had been met in the street in
’92, and was known to have gone to Germany in
’96. He varied the eternal monotony of the busi-
ness by swearing at his wife in German, Swedish
and English. Another petitioner told the Judge
that, having been away mining for over a year, he
found on his return an unlawful and unwelcome
increase in his family, with reference to which his
wife merely remarked that “she supposed she had
been drugged, or something.” Then he placed
his two children with his sister, and went away
again. Now, he wanted a decree. He got it.
Another deserted husband said he had only been
married four weeks when his wife went to Mel-
bourne to see her sister, and declined to return.
When asked why, in the circumstances, she
had married him at all, she ex pi lined that she
didn’t love him any longer, and that, if she did go
back with him, “ she would be hung for him.”

The Forgiveness of Sancho
Mendoza.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Sancho, stealing from the wharf, was like a tiger
on the footpath : children scattered ; the dogs ran
with side-glances and barked at him from a dis
tance. Sancho had the sleuth-like movements t a t
betokened the jungle-bred Cuban, and a nose t a
gave more than a hint of the Jew; yet his feet a
the tripping movement that goes with men w to
tread the planks of a ship. A lamp-lit pavemen
was no place for him ; his personality cried for a
cane-swamp, or a river-girt plantation packe
with singing negroes.
The riding-light of his sohooner flared dully m
the misty river-bend below; all along the pier
were close-drawn figures of men and women
trafficking in handshakes and other ebullitions
that are the stock-in-trade of the sirens who greet
men from the sea.
Sancho hated the wharf-siren. The monotony
of the sea had bred in him a few fixed ideals, a
love of his wife and hearth ; and it was this love
that kept him from the shallows of the wine-cup
that lead to the murderous deeps where the sirens
are.
His love of his own was the tiger’s love, play-
ful, kittenish, yet behind it a white-toothed
menace that is the first and last prerogative of the
carnivora.
The news of the schooner’s arrival had not
reached his wife Juanita ; her absence was proof
of that. Juanita was a Spanish creole of the com-
monplace New Orleans type; but it had been a
condescension in her to marry Sancho, the Cuban ;
and so Sancho always felt it. He looked up to
his handsome, coarse, passionate wife; and
eagerly did her bidding. And when their little
daughter had come, his man's worship of the
woman had gained something of the devotee’s
worship of his divinity. Sancho’s love was his
religion. , , ,
And Juanita loved him too; though she hec-
tored him and scolded him. Sancho accepted her
rule as meekly as her love; and was grateful for
both.
* * * *
Diving into a narrow street, Sancho slouched
past the garden-fronted cottages where children
played and women crooned in the dark of the
verandahs. He began to wonder if little Estella
had forgotten him at all during the month of his
absence; trading round all the little Cuban coast
villages. It occurred to him that a seafaring life
was not conducive to domestic happiness. The
lonely sea oppressed him at times and filled him
with cravings for his wife’s presence, for the
dimpled caresses of his little daughter. And he
had only the grey seas hammering like Vulcans at
the schooner’s ribs to comfort him. Juanita and
Estella were always so far from him ; but
he thought he knew how they felt in his
absence, how they were filled with a great glad-
ness when he capie home. Some day he would
give up sailoring and open a shop—second-hand
clothes, jewellery . . . good pickings, he mused.
His wife Juanita could sit in the doorway and
keep an eye on the goods hung outside.
Juanita was very pretty. Ah, that was some-
thing to bring a sailor-man home. She had
Spanish eyes. Dios ! her feet were as small as a
child’s.
A thick Cubano took sudden refuge between his
teeth, and its immediate incense cheered him like
a plantation melody. In his pocket was a pearl-
necklace he had bought in Santiago for the
little Estella. His fingers closed over it as he
thought of the pleasure it would give her. Some
of the street pickpockets might ease him of it.
Caramba! they would have to break his hand
first.
* * * *
He turned into a lane where children were beat-
ing each other and howling. The lane was blocked
at the end by a high wall ; his cottage stood
directly beneath. There was a garden in front
with sea-shells and mignonette along ’its borders.
His hand gripped the door-knocker, but a sud-
den thought stayed him ; he seized the door-knob
instead and pushed it open.
The unwelcome darkness of the interior chilled
him. Juanita’s bedroom was at the rear of this
little building. A thin slice of light came through
the door not quite closed. She had evidently lit
a candle. Santa Maria ! how amazed she would
be. He fancied he could hear little Estella’s quick
gasp of surprise. How sweet was his home-
coming ! He flung off his thick coat, and the
roar of the ocean seemed to die from his ears.
“ Juanita, Juanita ! ” he called in Spanish; “I
have come home! I, Sancho. I have come
home ! ” He moved forward and stood.
A terrible oath answered him. Then he heard
Juanita’s sob and the thump of feet on the floor.
The door shut.
Sancho Mendoza leaned against the door while
a cold sweat took him between the shoulders.
“Who is in the room, Juanita?” he whispered
childishly. “ I have been away. Ido not under-
stand.”
A silence followed —the kind of silence that
sometimes gets a hanging-judge by the throat.
“Juanita?” The Cuban spat with dry lips ; a
hurricane of blood tramped through his stooping
body. His wife’s answer came in the slow
whimper of a desperate woman. “ Ay, Dios mio!
Ay, en nombre de todos los santos I ” And
—there was a man with her. She was lost.
There was no pity for her. The devil had
tempted. “Ay, en nombre ”
* * * *
The Cuban heard the rest, and a sound like the
swirl of the Gulf Stream roared in his ears.
Again he felt the loneliness of the ocean and a
silence in which some men measure eternity.
Tick, tick, tick! He heard his own watch as
though it were speaking to the pistol that lay
almost against it.
Juanita wailed like a sick child. Sancho threw
away his cigar, and braced himself. “I have
been away. You do not care. Very well; Ido
not reproach you. Tell the man to come out.”
The voice of the unseen guest answered him.
“ I reckon you’ve bailed me up, senor. By gosh !
you came by wire. What are you going to do ?
Slow down for a scrap, or make it drinks?”
Sancho wiped his face with his blue silk hand-
kerchief. “I tell you Ido not reproach. My
wife does not care ; I do not care.”
“And you’re going to let me quit, senor?”
*‘ By God ! yes ! ” Sancho scraped the door with
his fingers like an impatient cat and spoke in
broken English. ‘ l l will let you go now, mistaire.
But this is my house ; this is my wife. My price
five dollair. Ze price of my house en my wife.
Quick, ze money; or, by Orise, I shoot—l keel
you !”
There was a clink of cash inside the room.
Sancho stooped and picked up a five-dollar gold
piece that was pushed under the door.

to meet his half-shut eyes. He had seen the little

brown hands about her mothers neck, hor one
moment the sight had held him between love and
hatred. He seemed to stand apart from them.
The mother might sinagainst tlieumveKe, and
Estella would still cling and love and cry with
her. They laughed together, understood their
own little gestures, their unrevealed thoughts.
He himself must always stand apart from therm a
beinsr only half understood ; a man who brougnt
them money at certain periods. ' They never knew
how he worked, tramping his schooner across the
seas and round through the choking heat; some-
times at the mercy of a crew of mutinous dogs
shipped from the crimping-dens of Havana or
the fever-swamps of Panama. . . . All m the
day’s work —all for them!
He shook himself suddenly. Juanita was
sitting opposite to him ; there were tears on her
cheek ; the coffee-cup in her hand clashed unneces-
sarily against the saucer. “You are going to
forgive me, Sancho ? I was so lonely. No one
ever came near me ... all day, all night. My
blood is quick, my heart is moved by kind words.
He came. I did not care for him much. I was
lonely. You have been lonely, Sancho, out on the
water. Can you wonder ? ” _
“I say Ido not reproach; that is enough.
“ But you are terrible, Sancho ! And you have
suffered from the loneliness ... in your. ships
full of men. If a woman had come to you in the
night and touched you with her lips . . . Ah, by
your eyes I know you will kill me. You will
never forgive. ”
“I tell you Ido not reproach. I shall never
mention it—never.”
He mumbled over his food, coughed like a tiger
in his haste. “ I love you, Juanita, for ever and
ever. I will never again go away. I will give up
the schooner and open a shop. I will love you for
ever and ever.”
The door opened softly, a dark-skinned child of
five crept in and stole behind Juanita.
*“ Come to me, my little love, my little Estella !
Sancho moved caressingly to where she stood
behind her mother, and fastened the pearl-necklace
about her throat.
Juanita turned slowly’, and saw, hanging from
the clustered pearls—a five-dollar gold-piece.
