ALBA DORION.
Of the 13 lepers in N.S.W. lazaret at the
end of last year only one was Chinese. Since
1883, 33 Chinese were admitted, as against 35 of
all other nationalities. Of the total 32 died.
“ Yarra” discourses to The Bulletin on
Bertram Mackennal’s “ Circe ”
There has been no overrating of Maekennal as far as
his Circe is concerned. The statue is indeed a work of
genius. In the big hall of Melb. National Gallery it is
badly placed, surrounded as it is by a score of other
statues and pictures, but nevertheless the moment the
spectator stands before the lithe, tense figure, omni-
potent in its glorious nakedness, he falls even as the com-
panions of Ulysses under the spell of the enchantress. It
is a psychological statue—a thing of beauty, and some-
thing more ; and though Circe is naked rather than
nude, there is not the faintest suspicion of coarseness or
sensuality about her. She is delicacy itself, and com-
mands worship instead of exciting passion. It is not till
afterwards that one remembers how essentially modern
she is. She is a woman of to-day—a society girl taken
out of her clothes and placed on a pedestal. Her profile
is one of absolute loveliness imbued with just the
faint Hebraic touch that adds tenfold to womanly
fascinations. But in her full face one finds the
true inwardness of the character ; the cold, pitiless,
cynical expression of confident mastery and contempt.
The figure is all muscle—not bulging and protuberant,
but delicate and shapely—and makes other statues near
by look like the Gippsland Fat Family. Relying upon his
art for his effects, Maekennal has discarded all the con-
ventions of the part, and the only accessories are the tiny
snakes wound about her hair.
The pedestal, which the London Academy carefully
draped before it permitted the British matron to view
the statue, depicts a number of youths and maidens
sprawling along the floor in various cuddlesome attitudes.
One gentleman is endeavoring to drag a lady away from
one of his companions who has already secured a tight
hold, and in the corner a girl is weeping—presumably
over her lost virtue.
Mackennal’s other work all bears the imprint of the
master-hand, but is not so ambitious. Melba’s bust is a
striking portrait and a fine piece of sculpture. The tiny
statuette of “ Salome ” is impregnated with the spirit of
brazen and flippant coquetry that might be expected of
that young lady. “ Truth ”is a well-conceived but care-
lessly-modelled statuette, and “ Fame,” the least admir-
able of the collection, is merely a conventional rendering
of the idea found on a dozen advts. every week.
From the sublimity of “Circe” the spectator passes
into a corridor to meet the ridiculousness of Herkomer’s
picture of the late Queen, for which the Victorian Govt,
paid nearly £lOOO. It is draped with crape around the
edges. In the interests of Art heavy drapery should be
hung over the entire canvas! And it should never be
taken off.
Writes a histrionic correspondent from
London to The Bulletin :
Christmas-time—as dull dramatically as panto—ab-
sorbs everything; but the Spring tures will soon be
starting. Several actors, quite unknown “ down under,”
are starring the provinces here as “ Australians.” A
curious impression prevails here that very large salaries
are the rule in Australia —due, probably, to what is
known of the amounts paid to imported English actors
by Australian managers. One pleasant difference here is
the courtesy extended to professionals. Managers act as
if you did them a favour in asking for either orders or
engagement none of the you —be —d d 1 style
of the Australian managers. I have frequently
been refused orders by Australian managers with-
out a word of apology to my stamped envelope,
but here my first application (to Hawtrey) brought a tele-
gram saying that two seats were at my disposal. Tree
refused 'me ; but with such polite explanations that I
cherish no animosity.
Tree has London by the back-hair, and is doing enor-
mous business. The papers are full of puff of his forth-
coming Twelfth Night. He makes speeches at theatrical
dinners, and poses as “ representative ” when the drama
is attacked.
All the West-End theatres close till after the
Queen’s funeral—though the suburban pantomimes
open after two nights’ shut-down. These “suburban”
productions are a revelation to an Australian. The
theatres are good enough for any metropolis, and before
Christmas most of last season’s successes were tiring
them.
I saw “ Bobs’ ” return in Hyde Park. He looked well
and ruddy, but with the sad, keen look familiar from his
photos. A cheer brought his head round my way—in a
wonderfully alert, erect fashion. He raised his cocked-
hat, for an instant was silhouetted against the grey of a
creeping fog, and then passed, leaving a suggestion of a
man with “ hair-trigger ” faculties—but indescribably
pathetic and human. Wales and the Princess were in
front, and Wolseley came later —without a cheer, which
must have been galling.
An example of British bathos re the Queen’s death.
The Daily News, in remarking how universal was the
sorrow, said that it heard a man ask for a 3rd-class rail-
way-ticket “ with tears in his voice ” !
The newly-arrived-Australian’s feeling that here he is
treading historic ground, soon wears off, but it came back
to me with a rush yesterday on reading over a public-
house door “ Established 1580.”
“The Welcome Nugget.”
THE BIGGEST LUMP OF GOLD FOUND
IN AUSTRALIA.
There are few Australians who have not heard
of “The Welcome Nugget,” and there are few
museums existing in the colonies without a model
of that precious find. So heavy was this piece of
gold, that it lay for one week in a shop in Bendigo
at the disposal of any lady who could carry it
away. So precious was it that the original owner
and finder, investing well the money he received
for its purchase, died a semi-millionaire. And so
great was its value from an historic point of view
that few books on mineralogy in any part of the
world are published without reference to the cele-
brated “Welcome Nugget.” The advent of the
nugget brought wealth, but there is not a
single record which goes to prove that
its arrival brought health. How different is it
with Bile Beans for Biliousness ; wherever they
are found they bring health with them. They are
more precious than all the “ Welcome Nuggets.”
And well do Australians know it. Indeed, there
are hundreds of miners in Australia to day who
have been cured by Bile Beans for Biliousness
from complaints owing their origin to the inhala-
tion of foul air, from indigestion, constipation,
piles, and those other complaints peculiar to a
miner’s life. These miners acknowledge that
when their health failed Bile Beans for Bilious-
ness was more precious to them than would be the
finding of the biggest nugget under the bowels of
the earth.
How to Make a Million.
Monet is a, pretty hard commodity to get some-
times, but again it appears to simply flow into the
coffers. Some people say this latter state is a
chance one. Others, again, maintain that wealth
comes only by hard, steadfast work. So be it.
The first essential to hard work is good health.
Bile Beans, the world’s greatest discovery, make
this possible, and also make the possibilities of
amassing wealth greater.
Bile Beans owe their existence to the discovery
by a medical man of a certain vegetable substance,
which, when compounded with other ingredients,
has the effect on the digestive organs of Nature’s
own bile, created by the liver. Finding this to be
the most natural cathartic obtainable, the public
readily accepted it in preference to all others. Act-
ing on the bowels in a gentle manner, Bile Beans
do not weaken nor distress the patient. They have
been aptly termed “ A medicine for the people at a
popular price,” and when it is considered that 30
million doses are consumed in Australia alone
annually the term is well justified. Bile Beans
for Biliousness are an undoubted specific for
Biliousness, Sick and Nervous Headache, Indi-
gestion, Constipation, Piles, Female Weakness,
Pale Face Girls, Irregularities, Bad Breath,
Pimples, Blotches, Dizziness, all Liver and Kid-
ney Troubles, Dyspepsia, Heart Palpitation, Pain
in 'Back and Side. When purchasing see that
you get Bile Beans for Biliousness, and don t be
put off with something which your dealer says is
“ just as good ” ; the “just as good ” kind bears
him more profit, and that is why he prefers to sell
them. He will endeavour to obtain wealth at the
expense of your health, but as health is wealth you
get health by using Bile Beans, and wealth can
then more easily follow. And even if it does not
follow, if you have health you would not exchange
that health for all the riches in the world.

The Love-Star.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There was one star
That never shone to-night.
I searched in vain through all the firmament
To find afar
Its wan but welcome light,
That I might dream its rays for me were sent.
It was so much
Above me, yet so small,
It trembled ’mid the greater glories there.
Red Mars was such
A fierce, red -flaring ball,
And suns and stars eclipsed it every where.
It shrank in fright
Away. And I was glad
To fancy that it knew I watched it shine
The whole long night
With pale gleam, through the sad
\Y ide seas of space—O, little love-star mine !
For now some cloud
Has come between, to hide
My star from me ; and it may never know :
Of that great crowd
It shone so pale beside,
Not one could find a heart to love it so.
W.A.
PROSPECT GOOD.
The Quick and the Dead.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Evek since the beginning of railways in N.S.W.
the passenger who alighted at Redfern Station has
been reminded of what might have happened, by a
sudden and fullviewof adensely-crowded cemetery
just across the street. To the outgoing traveller
it was constantly recalled that in the midst of
railway traffic the possibilities of loose rails, de-
fective and wrongly-turned points and human falli-
bility, are infinite, and that a railway journey is an
exciting gamble with deatli in a dozen shapes.
It was a dreary, desolate old place; covered
with a tangled mass of undergrowth, and the
graves were hideously neglected. Some queer
specimens of humanity seem to have haunted the
place ; and others, veritable ghouls, dwelt among
the tombs. One of the first discoveries made by
the men set to clear away the rubbish was the un-
buried body of a man fully dressed—even to boots
and hat, who had not been dead many years. He
was probably a mysterious disappearer who had
climbed in there to sleep off the effects of liquor,
and had died before recovering sobriety. They
sell some shockingly bad liquor in Sydney.
Another weird find was that of two old rag-
pickers who had been dwelling under a huge, flat
tombstone, supported on pillars at the corners, for
years. Their residence was surrounded by an
musually thick and tangled mass of shrubs
through which they had tunnelled, and which kept
off both wind and rain. They had scraped away
the soft, sandy soil beneath the stone
till their bed must have been within a
few inches of the remains beneath. They
lived in a certain degree of comfort too, with
plenty of food, a fair supply of illustrated
lapers and whole stacks of sensational literature.
they had made deep though narrow drains around
Iheir allotment to carry off any water, and were
better served than the shop-keepers of King-st.
during a recent flood. They shed tears when
Iheir retreat was invaded, and declared that it
was a blow which would drive them to the horrors
of Newington and Liverpool poor-asylums. What
vort of places must Newington and Liverpool
\sylums be when a man and woman prefer to live
ader a tombstone, in a grave, in preference to
(foing there ! The indictment of Warren Hastings
is tame beside this arraignment of N.S.W. Govt.
Charitable Institutions.
The workman sat on the fallen tombstone and
munched his dinner: “ Yes, we’ve seen some rum
sights at this job. The first I was 0:1 was the shiftin’
of ’s bodies out to his new place at (say)
Waverley. He had a vault—leastways, his old
man, what was buried in it, had. He was a nice
sort of a cove, and he came down to take a hand
in the job, and just afore we got to the gate, he
says, ‘Well, chaps, it mightn’t be very nice work,
you know, and yous had better have something
with me ter keep yer peckers up—l don’t care
about starting on it meself without some stimu-
lant.’ Poor bloke! he needed all his stimulant.
So we goes in and the cart was there to take the
coffins away. They didn’t bring no new coffins ’cos
the whole family was planted in leaden coffins,
which lasts for ever—if the thieves don’t get at
’em—which the blankers did in this yer case.
We lifted up the slab at the end of the vault, and
I goes down inside, and the poor bloke what the
people belonged to follows me, and then the other
chap wot was with me. It was kinder dark down
there, and so I struck a light and has a look round
—s’elp me, I’d like to crucify the brutes wot did
it! There wasn’t a coffin in the whole place ;
every one of ’em gone—stole, and sold ter the Chows
fer old lead ; and the bones was scattered about,
and in one corner piled up in an obscene position,
while on the other side lay a skeleton which
hadn’t broken up—most likely the poor bloke’s
father’s, with the arm bent and holding an empty
whisky-flask atween his teeth. The murderin’
cows had drunk the bloomin’ coffins and left him
only the empty flask. Strike me ! but it was low-
down.”
* * * *
He was one of the most reverent workmen
(they all seem to have undergone a regular course
of training in respect), and I waited till he picked
up his basket and started for home. . . . He put
down the deep-sinker, wiped his mouth with the
back Of his hand, and considered for a moment:
“No, I haven’t seen anything what you might
call startlin’ yet. It’s the grief of the relatives
that is worst—’specially the Irish. They seem to
take-on more than the English and the Scotch.
The Australians—my oath, they’re very pure;
they look on it as a day oat, and don’t care a
tinker’s hang. That woman that came down out
of the gate with us was Irish, and we opened up
her little plot to-day; and she_ just sat on the
stone and told us all about it—friendly like. She
came here just 60 years ago with her husband. He
died about six months after she was spliced, and she
bought the plot and put him there, and made a place
ready for herself beside him, for she didn’t expect to
last very long after he’d gone. They all feel that
way at first, you know. But, just like all the
rest of ’em, she went up country and met another
man wot pleased her all right, and they got fixed
up, and she had three children by him. They all
died when they was poor little kiddies, and she
had ’em buried down here} alongside her first
husband, wot didn’t leave her no kiddies. Then
her second husband died and she buried him in
the same old plot, alongside her first husband
and the children. She thought her heart would
break—they all think that, you know ! But it
didn’t, though she felt mighty lonely. She had
seen all her children and her two husbands die,
and she oame to Sydney and took a situation here
so as to be handy to keep the grave nice and tidy.
One day a young fellow wot was paying atten-
tions to her called round and took her for a drive.
Her luck was fair out, and they got smashed up
in Oxford-street; the young cove got killed and
she had her arm took off. He’d no friends here,
so she had him buried in her plot, and her arm
was buried there beside him. Then, hang me, if
she didn’t get married again and go off up-coUntry
and make a big pile of cash and have a big family
of children.
“ She came down from Queensland to have this
attended to. We took ’em up to-day. It must
have bin tryin’ for her, you know, sitting there on
the gravestone watching the bones took up one
by one. Of course she could tell which was the
kiddies’ and which was the men’s; but which par-
ticular man each was she couldn’t even guess, for
the coffins had all gone and they was all mixed up
together. But she swore I hadn’t found her arm.
She said she was certain because it was wrapped
in a sheet of lead, and I dug down about a foot
more and then scraped all round, and by-and-by
found it right behind me. The lead had bust, and
the bone was all hollow, just like a decayed root.
’'She picked it up, looked at it, laid it among the
other bones and began to cry. Poor old soul! She
blubbered and said : ‘ They was fine min, the lot
of thim ; and the children —the pretty dears—and
to think that’s all that’s left of thim ! ’ And she
took-on so it broke me up, and I had to go and get
me pipe, or I’d have cried too; I would ! ”
* * * *
In some graves and vaults coffins have been
taken up that showed absolutely no signs of ever
having contained a corpse. Many explanations
have been given—some suggested by the memory
of the discovery of a bag of human bones
under a tree close to the fence. Body-
snatchers who sold them to the University;
grave-plunderers who drove a lucrative trade in
human skeletons; women who wished their
friends to think them dead after having taken to
a life of vice ; men of the convict period who,
having waxed affluent, wished to go back to Eng-
land and flourish a respectable past; men who,
having left wives and children in other colonies
and countries and got tangled up here, wished to
sever old ties and pass through the grave to a sure
and certain resurrection in the arms of the new
woman.
Tombstones and those to whom they refer seem
to have drifted strangely apart—or else many
people have spent money and tears over the bones
of those who were never related to them. A
woman who had buried a baby there about 40
years ago stood in the midst of the impassive
tombstones with her hands clasped watching the
man digging, waiting feverishly till something
should be discovered. Spadeful after spadeful
was thrown out, and at last the iron grated against
wood. The workman stooped down and brought
out a skull of enormous size. He laid it aside and
was about to resume his search when the poor old
mother pounced down upon the relic and exclaimed
excitedly : “That’s my little pet’s skull—l know
it is—l know it is 1 ” And wrapping it up care-
fully in her shawl she went off moaning and rock-
ing it in her arms, heedless of the remaining
bones and utterly oblivious of the fact that it was
the head-piece of a man of large proportions.
Up near the top of the hill the skyline was
broken by a group round an excavation' in which
two men were working. At one end of the line
was the mother and at the other end a nice-look-
ing, neatly dressed young Australian. Between
these two stood a group of children of various
ages, the youngest a mere toddling mite who said
“muvver ! ” Young Australia is indeed irrever-
ence personified. The youthful members of the
family were all making jocular remarks to the
men digging below. Suddenly, a scraping sound
was heard, and the child who said “ muvver ”
asked, was it “grandad’s box.” It was all
that was left of it, and the reverent work-
man—the workmen are the most reverent
crowd of men this scribe has ever met—
discarded his spade and worked with a piece of
bent hoop-iron as a scraper. A rounded outline
appeared, and the contour of a skull was exposed.
The man who had unearthed it took it up and
wiped it softly on his sleeve —where, no doubt, he
had wiped many others just as tenderly, at Bs. per
day—and held it out to the son. But that young
gentleman held the point of his stick down—
“ Stick it on there ; I don’t care about handling
the old cove’s head, thanks ! ” The man hesitated,
with a side-look at the woman at the other end of
the line, and the filial grandson poked his ferule
into the spinal hole and lifted the grisly thing
into view of his mother. He held it up and looked
at it attentively for a moment, and the little child
left his “ muvver’s ” skirt and toddled round to
try and get it in his hands. The eldest son shook
his head flippantly—“ Alas, poor Yorick, I didn’t
know you ; but, by Jove, I can see the family like-
ness can’t you, mother ? ” Then he held it over
to her, and she took it in her hands and flung his
stick at him with an angry curse for “ making a
show of my father’s bones like that.” The
little child toddled back to his muvver, and hung
on to her till she passed the thing down to him,
and while they watched the rest of the bones being
taken up he amused himself by poking the sand
and rubbish out of the eye-holes.
• » * *
It was in the Roman Catholic portion, and the
ancient and bent matron sat holding her daugh-
ter’s hand, weeping, and watching the man scrap-
ing away the sand. The daughter kept her
feelings under control until a leg-bone was taken
out and placed by her knee. Then she, too, wept.
It was a grave containing several members of the
family, and, as they raked through the sand, a
little mother-of-pearl button, clean and bright,
glittered in the sunlight. The workman picked it
up and held it to the mother, who grasped it
eagerly and told how it was from Em’s shroud.
They wept over it a little and placed it away in
the purse. The top part of the skull had been
brought up and the lower jaw came up separ-
ately. A man who had seen the treasuring of the
little button bent down, loosened a tooth from the
upper jaw, and handed it to the daughter and
mother. They shuddered and shrank away. The
man laid the tooth down with a disappointed
expression. He had meant it kindly enough !
A tall old man, thin-lipped and bony-faced,
with lank, black locks against which the years had
beat in vain. Knotted hands and gaunt red
wrists; his coat dingy and glazed, his trousers
baggy at the knees and worn half-mast. He
shook like a reed, and standing on the edge of the
half-opened grave repeated half-alood, over and
over again, scraps of Holy Writ. “ Son of Man,
can these bones live ? , . Yet in my flesh shall
I see God—yes! that’s it —in my flesh, in my
flesh! ” And he shivered and muttered on, ripped
with fear at what he was about to see. and yet
unable to leave the spot. It was his wife’s
grave they were opening, and the digger looked
up irresolute, and hesitated as though he feared
to distress the emotion-racked man any more.
Someone offered whisky, but the shivering
mortal refused it and looked round the wilder-
ness of grey, grim stones in helpless misery.
The digger threw his spade aside, felt with
his hands and then with an upward look laid
a brown, dirt-enoumbered rorearm-hone at
the old man’s feet. He shuddered, and with his
hands clasped between his knees looked at it
steadfastly for a moment. The-shivering'passed.
diLeront d lato fche gra . v <* ««i motmned the
‘r g ?f r ? * , Sh ® was my wife, you know, and I
don 6 shrink from her poor bones now I” He
groped blindly in the soil and took the remains
out fragment by fragment till he came to the little
round skub. He held it in his hand and sobbed
and stroked it softly as though it was the head of
the living woman. . .
